Eszter's gaze went to his face for half a second longer than needed. Her look was not suspicious, just alert. She missed very little when a house changed temperature.

"Eat later," she said. "You look like you've been parked instead of fed."

He put the apple slice in his mouth and went up.

The service stairs were narrow and hot. On the first floor he could hear Oscar somewhere behind a closed door, speaking in the calm stripped tone men used when the figures were bad enough to make politeness useful. Beni caught only fragments through the thickness of the house.

"...not a question of appetite..."

"...mark it honestly, then..."

"...tomorrow morning, not tonight..."

The words stopped mattering as soon as he reached the second floor and stepped into Nina's part of the house, where everything softened into suede, silk, and low lamp light. Her dressing room was bigger than the front room he slept in at Laci's. Dresses hung in garment bags along one wall. Shoes stood in pairs on lit shelves, the leather and satin colours arranged so carefully they looked obedient. A glass of water sat on the vanity with a crescent of lipstick at the rim. One earring back lay beside it, a gold speck no bigger than a breadcrumb. On a chaise at the far end, an evening shawl had slid half onto the floor and stayed there because nobody in this house was paid to decide whether Nina wanted disorder preserved or removed.

The white Boots bag was on the blue chair exactly where she had said. The black catalogue lay underneath, heavier than it looked, bound with a grey paper band printed in small silver letters. He did not read them. The bag held a blister pack, a prescription box with her surname on the label, and an amber bottle of tablets that clicked against his knuckles when he lifted it. There was also a folded receipt and a lipstick. She had clearly emptied half a pocket while leaving in a hurry and expected the world to carry the pieces after her.

He took both items and left without looking at anything twice.

Back in the kitchen, Eszter was rinsing the knife. She glanced at the bag, the catalogue, then at him.

"Life or death?" she asked.

"Probably not."

"That is where the good jobs live."

He tucked the catalogue under his arm. "I'll be back before Bea."

"You say that like I control time."

"No. Only food."

That earned him a short laugh, surprised out of her more than offered. It followed him up the stairs and out into the mews, a warmer thing than the apple had been.

The drive east tightened around him almost immediately. Knightsbridge stalled. Park Lane was a slow glitter of brake lights and wet taxis. The radio murmured about rail strikes and a minister resigning over something nobody would remember in a month. Beni kept it low and watched the map on the console fatten with traffic colour. He knew faster routes by habit and slower ones by distrust. Today he took Marylebone Road because Euston Road at that hour could eat a school pickup alive, then cut south of Angel and out toward the canal where the city started pretending industry was a style rather than a wage.

By the time he crossed City Road the trip had already added more miles than Nina's lunch was supposed to contain. The number on the dash kept rising in plain digital calm while he sat in traffic beside men in white vans and cyclists with takeaway bags strapped to their backs. Any of them could say exactly where they were going. He was carrying a rich woman's pills and a glossy art catalogue across town so another rich man would not have to wait for either. There was no way to say that aloud without sounding either ridiculous or bought.

He had been to Hackney Wick only twice before, both times dropping people who wanted rough brick and water towers behind whatever glossy version of themselves they were making. Jonas Hale's building sat near a run of studios and breweries in an old warehouse block with a fresh black gate and security cameras small enough to miss unless you were looking for them. Graffiti climbed the lower brickwork. The intercom plate had too many surnames on it and one small gold plaque with HALE taped over the original lettering, temporary and vain at once.

Beni texted the number Nina had used.

Downstairs.

The reply came at once.

Come up. 3rd floor. Don't ring again.

He parked badly on purpose because there was nowhere legal to put the car and no point pretending Hackney Wick would offer a neat Belgravia solution to a Belgravia problem. He took the bag and catalogue and crossed the yard past a pile of broken pallets slick with rain. Somewhere nearby someone was grinding metal. The sound went through the building like dental work.

The freight lift smelled of damp plywood, spray paint, and old piss under citrus cleaner. On the third floor the corridor had been painted white badly enough to keep the industrial bones visible. Jonas's studio door stood open three inches, then wider when Beni knocked with the side of his fist.

Jonas Hale answered in a black T-shirt dusted with plaster and loose charcoal trousers rolled above the ankle. He wore soft suede slippers with one heel flattened underfoot and had a nicotine patch peeling at the top edge of his left arm, lint stuck to the glue where he'd worried it with his fingers. He was younger than Nina by enough to be obvious but old enough to know exactly what use he made of it. The first thing Beni noticed, after the patch, was that Jonas smelled not of paint or cigarettes but of cedar or vetiver hand soap, the kind that lived beside a stone basin in brown glass.

"Yes?" Jonas said.

"Mrs. Ashdown asked me to bring these."

Jonas's gaze dropped to the Boots bag, then the catalogue. Something like contempt passed over his face so quickly it almost qualified as boredom.

"Of course she did."

He stepped back just far enough for Beni to enter the threshold. The studio beyond was huge and cluttered without ever losing the look of a place buffered from consequence. At the Ashdown house, effort disappeared into polish and flowers changed before they drooped. Here effort had been staged as temperament. Plaster dust filmed the concrete floor. Three unfinished sculptures stood under work lights like bandaged bodies. A low cream sofa sagged deep at the middle, paint drying on one arm. Wine bottles crowded a metal sink beside a line of Japanese knives. There were shipping crates stencilled from Basel, a Bluetooth speaker the size of a suitcase, and tulips collapsing elegantly in a chipped champagne bucket. A silk scarf too fine for the room had been kicked halfway under a welding glove. The mess was real enough to smell, but even the disorder had been arranged into a signature.

From deeper in the studio Nina called, "Is that Beni?"

Jonas turned his head only a fraction. "Your supplies have arrived."

She appeared a second later from behind a canvas screen used to divide the sleeping area from the rest of the room. She was barefoot, carrying one heel in her hand and already sliding it on as she crossed the floor. Her blouse had been fastened wrong then corrected; one button still pulled. Her lipstick was blurred at the right corner of her mouth, and there was a pale streak of clay or plaster on the sleeve of her coat where she had brushed against something. She looked at Beni first, not Jonas, the way a person looked at the one thing in a room that might still function.

"Thank God," she said. "I knew you'd sort it."

There was no embarrassment in the words. There was gratitude, yes, but it carried ownership inside it.

He held out the bag and catalogue. "From your dressing room, Mrs. Ashdown."

She set the heel down, opened the bag, and shook two white tablets into her palm from the blister pack with hands steadier than her voice. She swallowed them dry. The muscles in her throat worked twice. For a second her face lost its polish and became simply a face that hurt.

"God, that's vile." She took the catalogue under her arm. "Thank you."

Jonas had already turned away to adjust something on a table, aligning a small plaster maquette by millimetres and giving it more attention than the scene in his studio. "This is absurd," he said, not quite to either of them. "A courier for tablets and an art catalogue. We're one floral arrangement away from a hotel."

Nina's jaw tightened. "Don't be tiresome."

"I'm not being tiresome. I'm observing scale."

Beni kept his eyes on the strip of floor in front of him. He had learned early that rich people preferred witnesses who could be mistaken for furniture, but he had also learned furniture did not usually get spoken at with this much sharpness. That made him too present in the room for his own comfort.

Nina slid the pills back into the bag and held the catalogue against her hip. "Give me five minutes," she said to Beni, making it sound like a timetable adjustment rather than an order. "We'll go straight back."

"Yes, Mrs. Ashdown."

Jonas gave a short laugh through his nose. "You see? Infrastructure."

The word landed in the open space between Beni and Nina and stayed there.

He waited outside in the corridor because staying in the studio felt worse. The door remained ajar. Through the gap he heard nothing clear enough to count as content, only the shapes of pressure: Nina's voice stretching longer than usual, Jonas answering briefly, the knock of something set down too hard, then silence that meant more than the sentences had. Once, very clearly, he heard Nina say, "Don't be childish." A few seconds later, lower, he heard the word please. After that he stopped trying to separate one sound from another.

The corridor window looked over the canal and a yard full of stacked materials. A young woman in an oversized waxed jacket smoked beneath the fire escape while staring at her phone, one hand absently rubbing the ear of a small black dog tucked under her arm. The dog wore a mustard knit jumper and looked bored out of its life. A courier dragged a crate across the concrete below, swearing at a stuck wheel. On the opposite roof somebody had planted rosemary in paint tubs. Hackney Wick sold damage back to people as atmosphere. Beni had seen cheaper versions of the same brick and rust in Hungary with nobody taking photographs of them.

After twelve minutes Nina came out with both heels on, her coat closed, and her face reassembled. Only the missing softness around her mouth gave away how recently she had been something other than composed. She no longer carried the catalogue. The Boots bag was folded flat in her hand.

"Let's go."

Beni turned toward the lift. As he reached the button Jonas came to the door behind them. He had found a nail file from somewhere and was working at the edge of one thumbnail with bored concentration, folding even that into the afternoon's art practice.

"Nina," he said, "if you need half of Belgravia to make a visit possible, maybe shorten the visits."

She did not turn. "Grow up."

Then his gaze shifted past her to Beni, landing on him with fresh irritation. The sight of him spoiled the arrangement.

"Does she send all her errands through boys now," he asked, "or are you a special feature?"

Nina stopped then, one heel lifted a fraction off the concrete. Beni could not tell whether she had expected the insult or hoped it would not be spoken in front of him. She still did not look back.

"I drive where I'm told, sir," he said.

Jonas gave a thin smile, not kind enough to be called one. "Yes. That was clear."

The lift arrived with a thud. Nina got in first. Beni followed. As the doors closed he saw Jonas already turn away, rubbing at the nicotine patch again, bored with the damage as soon as he had made it.

In the car Nina did not tell him where to go because there was nowhere else to go. He pulled out from the canal and headed west. For the first few minutes she said nothing. She sat in the rear left seat, though no one else was with them, her usual position when she wanted the car to function as a room rather than a vehicle. She folded the empty Boots bag once, twice, then tore the chemist receipt into strips so small they stuck to her fingers.

At a red light by Old Street she said, "You can put the air up a bit."

He adjusted it.

Another minute passed. The rain thickened, then thinned again. Cyclists slashed between buses. A siren went somewhere north and kept going.

"Did he say anything else?" Nina asked.

Beni watched a white van edge half into his lane. "No, Mrs. Ashdown."

It was not a full lie yet. Jonas had said enough without giving Beni anything useful to report.

She let out a breath and leaned her head back. "He always does that. He acts as if being needed is an offence against good taste."

Beni said nothing.

She looked at her own reflection in the window, saw him there as well, and went on because he did not interrupt. "I forgot the pills because I was already late. Then I forgot the catalogue because I was trying not to look like someone who needed to bring a catalogue at all. Ridiculous. All of it."

She laughed once, with no humour inside it, and rubbed at the heel of her hand where the torn receipt paper had left a grey smudge.

"I shouldn't have gone today," she said. "He told me he was working. That ought to have been enough information for a sane person."

The word sane hung there longer than it wanted to. She seemed to hear it herself and straighten against it.

"Anyway." She pressed two fingers to the bridge of her nose. "Thank you for not making a face."

He caught her eyes in the mirror for a second. "Mrs. Ashdown?"

"Most people do. They either look pleased you're in trouble, or they look disgusted, or they try to help in some eager way that makes everything worse. You just do the thing."

"I just drove."

"Yes," she said. "That's why I asked you."

The car moved again. Her voice had softened, but the softness did not take the hierarchy out of it. If anything it made it more exact. She did not want understanding from him. She wanted function without commentary, a human extension cord she did not have to thank properly because the current was what mattered.

By the time they turned back into Belgravia she had powdered her nose with the mirror in her phone and fixed the lipstick well enough that only someone who had seen the earlier version would know the difference. She folded the empty Boots bag into her handbag, looked once at the house, and said, "If Bea asks, I was at lunch."

"Yes, Mrs. Ashdown."

She got out before he could come round to open her door. That, more than anything, told him the pills had not yet fully taken hold.

The mews held the kind of late-afternoon yellow that made even clean stone look tired. Beni parked in the garage and killed the engine. The car gave back the house sounds slowly: a delivery van reversing on the next street, cutlery somewhere inside, a plumber's radio from further down the mews. He checked the dash. The run had added exactly what he had known it would add, plus a little more for the crawl back through Marylebone: enough miles to stand out if anyone took the trouble to line them against Nina's lunch.

He was reaching for the afternoon sheet when Oscar Ashdown appeared in the garage doorway.

Oscar was already in his coat though he was not yet leaving, tie perfect, hair still damp at the temples from whatever quick shower or sink-wash he used to turn one part of the day into another. He held his phone and a slim silver tin in the same hand. Beni had once seen him shake fennel seeds from it after lunch instead of taking coffee with the rest of the table. Now he flipped the tin open with his thumb, neat enough to make chewing look procedural.

"Beni," he said. "One small question."

"Sir."

Oscar tipped a few seeds into his palm but did not eat them yet. He looked at the dashboard, then at the sheet in Beni's hand. He did not look suspicious exactly. He looked exact.

"Nina told me she was at lunch on Mount Street and then straight home for Bea." His tone remained courteous enough that a stupid man could have mistaken it for ease. "The mileage is high for that. Did she add a stop?"

There was time, in the space after the question, to tell the truth in a thin practical version. Mrs. Ashdown asked me to collect forgotten medication and a catalogue and take them across town to Jonas Hale's studio. He could imagine the sentence cleanly because roads always arranged themselves in order for him. That did not make the sentence survivable.

He thought of Nina in the studio swallowing tablets dry because she could not bear to ask Jonas for water. He thought of Jonas saying infrastructure and making the word sound like a joke. He thought of the house around them, the service stairs, Eszter's apple slice salted against the sink, Bea needing collecting in less than half an hour, the nine hundred pounds still split over his month like a threat. All of it sat there in the pause with Oscar waiting politely for numbers to explain themselves.

"There was traffic, sir," he said. His voice sounded steady enough to belong to another driver. "Mrs. Ashdown asked me to keep moving rather than sit outside. Then she needed a pharmacy stop before we came back."

Oscar watched him. "A pharmacy where?"

Beni had passed three Boots and one independent chemist on the return and could have named each frontage by colour. He picked the one nearest the lie's shape.

"Sloane Avenue, sir."

Oscar finally put the fennel seeds in his mouth. He chewed once, twice. "That still seems ambitious."

"Park Lane was blocked and Knightsbridge hardly moved. I had to loop up through Marylebone."

Oscar's gaze stayed on him long enough for Beni to understand that politeness could be a form of pressure more efficient than anger. If Oscar had raised his voice, Beni might have felt only insult. This way he felt the calculation. The man was weighing not just whether the story was true but whether it was useful to let it remain true for now.

"I see," Oscar said at last.

He closed the tin. "Next time the route changes materially, let the office know. Untidy numbers make people curious, and curiosity is usually expensive."

"Yes, sir."

Oscar gave a short nod, half thanks, half dismissal, and left the garage. His shoes made almost no sound on the concrete.

Beni stayed where he was. The car had cooled enough that the leather no longer held the shape of Nina's body. In the rearview mirror he could see the empty back seat, the pale crease where her coat had pressed into it, one tiny strip of receipt paper caught in the floor mat like fish skin. He reached back, picked it up, and rubbed it between finger and thumb until it softened.

The lie came in three pieces: traffic, pharmacy, Marylebone.

They were three clean pieces, easy enough to repeat. It was a story built from road names and errands, the kind of story a driver could tell because drivers were expected to know how cities wasted time. He had carried richer people's excuses before in silence: late train, wrong entrance, bad phone signal, she felt ill. He had let them settle around him and had not called that lying because nothing had been asked directly of his mouth.

This was different. Oscar had asked. Beni had answered. The falsehood had crossed his tongue with the same plainness as any route instruction. What made his stomach go light was not that he had protected Nina. He had been protecting Nina since the day she first told dispatch she wanted him by name. The change was smaller and worse. He had heard himself do it and recognized how usable the sound was.

He folded the strip of chemist receipt once more and put it in the bin by the workbench. Then he sat back in the driver's seat with both hands on the wheel and waited for the taste of the lie to leave his mouth. It stayed there, chalk-dry and ordinary. By the time he started the engine again, it was no longer only Nina's.


# Chapter 5

The lie stayed chalk-dry at the back of his mouth all the way home. Inside the flat he stripped the blanket off the sofa bed and snapped it once toward the window, stirring up stale sleep and the lemon spray from the pound shop that could not beat damp plaster or old frying oil. There were forty minutes until Eszter said she would be at the chicken shop on West Green Road. He shoved his Mercer tie into the drawer under the television stand, kicked Laci's spare work boots behind the armchair, and folded yesterday's shirt so its collar stains did not show. The room still looked like what it was: a front room rented as a bedroom because London could always find one more way to charge a man for standing upright indoors.

The buses made the glass shiver every few minutes. Somebody downstairs was frying something sweet with too much cinnamon. Somewhere in the building a child cried in short outraged bursts, stopped, started again. Beni stood in the middle of the room with the blanket in his hands and tried to decide whether he had made it better or only arranged the embarrassment into straighter lines.

At noon, in the Ashdown basement kitchen, Eszter had said it like an instruction.

"I'm finishing at six. There's a place near Seven Sisters that panics before closing and drops the chicken price. I'll get one. You buy potatoes and sour cream. Don't do anything stupid like light candles."

He had looked up from the slice of bread he was eating. "Why would I light candles?"

"Because you're nineteen and frightened."

"I'm not frightened."

"Then don't clean too hard either. I hate entering a room that looks like a murder happened and then somebody got ashamed of it."

Now, with the blanket smelling faintly of spray and the window letting in wet air that was not much cleaner than the room, he understood that she had known exactly what he would do anyway.

He checked his wallet before he left. Twenty-six pounds and some coins: enough for potatoes, sour cream, bread if they needed it, and two lagers if he did not look too long at the better shelf. He put the coins in his right pocket so he would not have to count them in front of the cashier twice.

Eszter was waiting outside the chicken shop with a blue supermarket bag in one hand and a canvas tote in the other, her sleeves pushed up and leaving red elastic marks on her forearms. She had changed out of her work black into jeans and an old green jumper with one cuff gone loose. The hems were dark with rain. A bunch of parsley stuck out of the tote like something trying to escape. When she shifted the bag from one hand to the other, he heard the clink of a tin against a jar.

"You came," she said.

"You told me to."

"That isn't the same thing."

The chicken shop window was fogged with grease. Behind the glass a handwritten sign promised "FAMILY BOX REAL TASTE ENJOYMENT," the last word squeezed in sideways where there had not been room.

Eszter tilted her head at it. "Real taste enjoyment. That's serious English."

"Maybe the fake taste sold out."

She gave him a look that was half surprise, half approval. "Good. You do know how to talk without being interrogated."

The warmth from the bag reached him when she handed it over. The chicken inside was whole, yellow-skinned, still faintly warm from the rotisserie even though she meant to cut it up and drown it in sauce. The tote held three onions, the paprika tin, a small packet of bay leaves, and an apple with a bruise blooming down one side. He noticed the apple because it seemed like Eszter to carry one around all day and then forget to eat it.

"You bought everything," he said.

"No. I bought the important things. You can still contribute potatoes. Unless Tottenham has banned vegetables."

They went into the off-licence next door because it sold more than alcohol if you knew where to look. Potatoes were in a crate by the drinks fridge, dusty and beginning to sprout at the eyes. Sour cream sat beside sandwich meat and energy drinks. Beni chose the cheapest tub that did not expire tomorrow, a kilo of potatoes, and, after standing with the lager shelf open too long, two cans with dented sides because they were forty pence less.

Eszter watched him put the good sour cream back and take the cheaper one.

"Don't buy poison to prove a point," she said.

"It's fine."

"Fine is not a flavor."

She swapped the tub without asking and put the better one in the basket. Eighty-nine pence instead of sixty-five. He opened his mouth.

"If this ruins your financial plan, I'll pay the difference," she said. "Relax."

He paid anyway, coins first, notes after. She did not look away while he counted. That was one of the things about her. She never performed delicacy like it was kindness. If there was something to see, she saw it.

Outside, the rain had thinned to a wet shine on the pavement. Men stood under the bus shelter eating from white clamshell boxes with steam coming up against their faces. Traffic dragged and swore. West Green Road smelled of diesel, wet cardboard, and sweet bread from the Turkish bakery on the corner. Beni took the heavy bag from her, and she let him.

"How bad is it?" she asked as they walked.

"What?"

"Your room. The way you're carrying that chicken like it contains bad news."

He kept his eyes on the pavement where rainwater filled the broken parts in black shapes. "It's not a room. It's the front room."

"I assumed Buckingham Palace was unavailable."

"Laci has the bedroom."

"That makes sense. He's older and probably snores like machinery."

"You haven't met him."

"I know men who work construction. They all sound like they've swallowed loose bolts."

He laughed before he could help it. She heard it and smiled without turning it into a victory.

"It's damp," he said.

"Everywhere is damp."

"There's no privacy."

"Now that is useful information."

He glanced at her. "You can still go home."

"I know. I'm not a hostage."

The reply should have stung. Instead it steadied him a little. She was not pretending she had drifted here accidentally. She was here because she had decided to be.

By the time they reached the building the late light had gone the color of dishwater. The hallway smelled of bleach laid over old cabbage. Three takeaway menus had been shoved through the letter slot and lay dampening on the mat. One had "WE DELIVERS HAPPINESS QUICKLY" in red capitals across the top.

Eszter bent, picked it up, and snorted. "No, they don't."

"Maybe slowly."

"Then they should say that. I'd trust it more."

He unlocked the flat and let her in first because there was no graceful way to block her view. The room was exactly as he had feared: the sofa folded out into his bed, the blanket tucked too tightly in a way that announced recent panic, the radiator crowded with drying socks, the wall by the window bubbled under the paint where damp kept returning. A suitcase sat half under the coffee table. The cheap chest of drawers leaned a little left because one foot was shorter than the others. Laci's spare plastering overalls hung behind the door like a second man waiting.

Beni put the bags down. "It's temporary."

Eszter looked once around the room, not hurrying, not pretending. She put her tote on the table, shrugged out of her coat, and touched the damp wall with the back of one finger.

"Temporary is generous," she said. "This wall is trying to become soup."

Heat rose up Beni's neck.

Then she turned toward him, practical as ever. "Where's the biggest pot?"

He stared. "That's all?"

"What else did you want? An inspection report?"

"No."

"Good. Then get the pot."

He had one decent pot and a frying pan with a handle that wobbled if you forgot and lifted it too fast. She examined both, made a face at the knife, and tied her hair back tighter with the elastic she had been wearing on her wrist.

"This knife has given up on life," she said.

"It still cuts."

"That isn't a recommendation."

She put him to work at once. He peeled potatoes, sliced onions, and jointed the chicken at the counter space beside the sink, which was really only the bit of laminate not yet stolen by the kettle and the dish rack. He had expected awkwardness, some hovering stage where they would both have to invent reasons to touch the same cupboard or laugh at the same thing. Instead the work arrived and took up the space. She salted the chicken hard, rubbing it in with the flat of her hand. He peeled onions and tried not to watch the flex of her wrists, the way she moved as if kitchens made sense in her body even when the kitchen was no more than a bad corner and a sticky hob.

He was used to instructions that sounded like route slips: leave it here, wait downstairs, pick up Bea at four fifteen. But in the car they turned him into part of the mechanism. With Eszter it was different. "Pass me that," "hold this," "taste it now" kept pulling him back into the room instead of wiping him out of it.

The onions hit the pan and started giving themselves up. She took the pan off the heat before opening the paprika, looked at him over her shoulder, and said, "If you burn this, I will insult your entire bloodline."

"I know how paprika works."

"Do you? Excellent. Then you can marry a Hungarian woman over forty and destroy your life properly."

The words landed oddly in him, not because she had meant anything by them; she probably had not. But he felt his hands pause over the potatoes.

She saw it at once. "What?"

"Nothing."

"That answer is getting boring."

He cut another potato just to have something to look at. "I'm not marrying anyone."

"Nobody asked you to. I was explaining the stakes of burnt paprika."

"You make everything sound dangerous."

"Most things are."

She spooned the red powder into the pan. The smell rose up warm and dusty and sweet, instantly deeper than the room had been a second before. She stirred fast, added onion, then water, then the chicken pieces, and the pot began its thick wet muttering. The paprika stained the wooden spoon the color of brick. Steam touched the window and clouded out the estate across the road.

"Taste," she said.

He did. It was not finished, not near, but already it had shape.

"More salt," he said.

"Good. You do have a tongue."

He found himself smiling into the pot.

While it simmered she opened the cupboard above the sink, found tea bags, rejected them, and took one of the takeaway menus from the table instead. She flattened it with her palm and read in a grave voice.

"Chef's Burger Coming With Smile Potato."

"What's smile potato?"

"Chips that know they're trapped."

He laughed again. She kept reading.

"Free chicken pieces for order upper twenty pound." Upper twenty pound. Very grand.

"You have to go above the money."

"Ascend to the chicken."

She folded the leaflet and used it to fan steam away from her face. "These places always sound like they're threatening you with dinner."

"Maybe the food is fine and only the English suffers."

"That is also London."

He leaned against the counter with the knife in his hand and looked at her because she was busy reading the leaflet aloud in a deadpan so dry it made the room seem less wet. He realized after a second that he was simply looking, not checking whether she was bored or bracing for mockery or waiting for an order. He was looking because he wanted to.

She caught him at it. "What?"

"Nothing."

"Again? You're wasting the word."

"I didn't know you'd actually come."

That made her stop fanning the steam. She looked at him more directly then, the joke set aside but not dropped.

"Why not?"

He shrugged. The knife handle was slick in his palm from potatoes.

"Because of this place?" she said.

He gave the smallest nod.

"Beni." She set the leaflet down. "I work in a house where people apologize if the tulips open too fast. I know the difference between embarrassment and an actual problem."

He said, before he could shape it better, "You're older."

There was half a beat of silence, then she barked one surprised laugh.

"Jesus Christ. Is that what this is?"

He wished at once he had said anything else. "I didn't mean..."

"No, tell me exactly what you meant."

He put the knife down. There was nowhere good to rest his hands. "You're twenty-seven."

"And?"

"And I'm nineteen."

"I'm aware. I can count faster than you think."

He stared at the pot. The lid rattled once and settled.

"Do you think I walked here by accident," she said, less amused now, "and only just now discovered your date of birth?"

"No."

"Do you think I need protecting from a sofa bed and one rude cousin?"

"No."

"Then stop saying no like you're asking permission. Say the real thing."

The real thing was ugly in his throat, and it came with all the parts he hated most: he was too young, too poor, too easy to place, just a boy pretending his wages made him a man because the suit fit well enough if he kept his shoulders back. He kept his hands flat on the counter so she would not see the shake in them. The socks on the radiator gave off their boiled-damp smell, and the loose window latch clicked in its frame when a bus went past.

"I don't want you to come here and realize you made a mistake," he said.

She looked at him for long enough that he had to meet her eyes.

"All right," she said. "That is at least a sentence."

Then she turned the flame lower under the pot and reached past him for the sour cream. "Next time say that first. It saves time."

The tightness in him did not vanish, but it changed shape. It felt less like panic and more like being held in place.

She mixed flour and sour cream in a mug to keep it from splitting. He sliced bread. She found the apple in her bag, cut the bruise off with the bad knife, and ate the rest standing at the counter.

"You carried an apple around all day and forgot about it," he said.

"I didn't forget. I postponed."

"That's not better."

"It is if you're busy."

Her phone buzzed once on the table. She glanced at it, typed two quick lines with her knuckle because her fingers were wet, and put it face down again without explanation. He liked that too, the lack of performance. If there was a life outside the room, she did not brandish it or conceal it for effect.

By the time the paprikash was done the room smelled of onion, pepper, chicken fat, and the sour edge of cream turning soft in the heat. It covered the damp almost completely. They ate at the table because there was only one chair and the armchair was full of folded laundry, so he sat on the edge of the bed and she took the chair and turned it sideways, knees nearly touching his. He served her first because the habit was quicker than thought. She noticed, but only by lifting one eyebrow.

"You can feed yourself too," she said.

"I know."

"Good. Then take more. You look like a greyhound."

He did. The first mouthful was too hot and burned the roof of his mouth, but he did not care. The sauce was thick and sweet and sharper at the back from the paprika. The potatoes had gone soft at the edges and held the sauce like they had been built for it. He had been hungry since before lunch, though he had not named it as hunger until the bowl was in his hands.

Eszter watched him eat with an expression he could not sort out at first. It was not amusement exactly, and it was not pity. Maybe it was appraisal, but without the cold part, as if appetite told her something worth knowing.

"See?" she said. "You weren't going to make this for yourself."

"I could."

"You were going to eat toast standing up over the sink."

He wanted to deny it. He was still chewing, so honesty got there first. "Maybe."

"Exactly."

He looked down into the bowl because her gaze had become difficult in a new way. Most of the time when someone studied him there was an angle to it: employers checking punctuality, clients checking discretion, men at the depot checking whether he could be trusted with a better route. Even desire, when it had come to him before, had usually arrived with the feeling that he was being chosen for a use he would only understand too late. This was quieter and somehow harder to bear. She looked as if the fact of him eating, sitting there, existing in the bad room he had tried and failed to improve, interested her for its own sake.

The key hit the lock twice before it found the hole.

Beni knew from that alone that Laci had been drinking.

The door pushed inward. Laci came in with one orange earplug still tucked behind his left ear and a plastic sandwich bag of bent screws in his hand, as if he had picked them up from site rather than let them get thrown away. His jacket smelled of damp plaster and lager. He stopped three steps in, took in the table, the steam, Eszter in the chair, the blanket folded almost carefully at the bed's foot.

"Fuck me," he said in Hungarian. "He scrubbed the room."

Beni set his bowl down. "We made food."

"I can smell that. I said the room."

Eszter held out her hand before Beni could decide whether introductions would make this better or worse. "Eszter."

Laci stared half a second, then shifted the screws to his other hand and shook with surprising care, like he knew his palm was rougher than necessary. "Laci."

"Take your boots off," she said, glancing at the wet prints starting on the floorboards. "Unless you want plaster in the dinner."

He barked a laugh and toed the boots off by the door.

"You hear that?" he said to Beni. "I leave you alone one day and a competent person appears."

"Sit or don't," Eszter said. "But stop dripping."

It could have gone bad there. Beni felt the possibility of it as clearly as a pothole under tyres, the split second where you knew the car could go one way or the other. Laci looked at her again, more awake now under the drink. Then he set the bag of screws in the margarine tub on the radiator and rubbed at the plaster dust caught in his brow.

"There's enough?" he asked.

"If you don't complain while eating," she said.

"Then no, probably not."

But he took a bowl when Beni gave him one and sat on the arm of the armchair because there was nowhere else. He ate too fast for the first few bites, as if he had meant to say something uglier and the food interrupted him.

"Good," he said at last.

"Try not to sound shocked," Eszter said.

"I'm shocked he owns paprika."

"I brought it."

"That tracks."

He ate more, blew out a breath, and leaned back. Up close the early lines around his mouth showed under the stubble. His eyes were bloodshot with site dust and tiredness more than drink. He looked older than thirty-two in the flat light. When he shifted his weight, he touched his right knee first with a quick practiced rub, as if it had been aching long enough to need no discussion.

"Foreman docked us for a broken spirit level," he said to nobody in particular. "As if we ate the fucking thing."

"Maybe you did," Eszter said. "You sound hungry enough."

"I was hungry enough till five minutes ago."

Then he looked at Beni, and the bowl in Beni's hands turned suddenly too hot, as if he had been caught holding evidence.

"Friday," he said.

Beni's face went hot. "I know."

"Knowing isn't money."

Eszter did not look away to spare him. She spooned more sauce onto her plate and said, "Then let him eat before you invoice him. Debt sounds worse when the food is good."

Laci's mouth twitched. It was not a smile exactly, but it was something close enough to pass.

"You work in one of those rich houses too?" he asked her.

"Yes."

"Then you've seen worse than this."

"I've seen cleaner," she said. "Worse is a different category."

Laci grunted, accepting that. He finished the bowl, mopped the rest with bread, and stood. "If you come again, bring a better knife. This one is insulting the chicken."

"I already said that."

"Then you're ahead of him."

He picked up one lager from the table, shook it beside his ear to guess whether it had gone warm, and headed for the bathroom in his socks. The flat had thin walls and a thinner sense of dignity. When the bathroom door shut, the pipe in the kitchen knocked twice. Water hammered into the tub with no subtlety at all.

Beni stared at the table.

"You can breathe," Eszter said.

"Sorry."

"No."

He looked up. "What?"

"Don't do that."

"Do what?"

"Keep apologizing every time your life makes a noise."

He picked at a loose bit of label on the lager can. "He shouldn't have said that."

"About Friday? Why not? Friday exists."

"Not like that."

She pushed her empty bowl away. "Beni, I count money too. I know what rent sounds like when people are behind. It isn't contagious."

The shower stopped. Laci coughed hard once, spat into the sink, ran the tap again.

"It's not just the rent," Beni said quietly.

"I know."

"Then what?"

She leaned back in the chair and looked around the room once, taking it all in again: the suitcase, the socks on the radiator, the blanket, the warped drawers.

"Then this is what you have right now," she said. "It's not a trick. It's not a moral test. It's a damp front room in Tottenham with one cousin, one bad knife, and decent paprikash. I can either be here or not be here."

The bathroom door opened. Laci came out toweling his face with a T-shirt, nodding at them as if the room were his and not his and he had stopped caring which. He took the second lager and limped, very slightly, to the bedroom. A minute later Hungarian football commentary started through the wall, turned down low enough to count as a gesture and loud enough to remain a fact.

The noise should have killed whatever the evening had been. Instead it settled into the background with the buses and the pipes and the building's general complaint. Eszter stood, collected the bowls, and took them to the sink.

"Leave it," Beni said.

"No. You cooked too."

"I mostly peeled things."

"Exactly. Shared labor. Modern romance."

He took the bowls from her anyway and washed while she dried because the sink was too small to let them both do anything else. Their elbows touched and separated, touched again. The water ran hot for thirty seconds, then lukewarm. A bit of chicken skin clung to the drain. She shook out the tea towel and slapped it once over his wrist when he tried to set a wet bowl straight onto the table.

"You really do live like a man on probation," she said.

"From what?"

"Adulthood. Pleasure. Basic housekeeping. Choose one."

He laughed despite himself. "You're very encouraging."

"I didn't come to encourage you. I came to eat."

He put the last bowl down to dry. After the washing up there was no neutral surface left in the place. Her coat hung over the chair, his hands still smelled of onions, and the spoon reddened with paprika lay on the plate beside the stove. They were signs of occupation, not of service or waiting, but of a life behaving, for one evening at least, as if it were allowed indoors.

She picked up another menu from the mat pile and read, "Burger meal with free delightedly."

"What does that mean?"

"It means no one editing this has slept enough."

"That's fair."

She folded the leaflet small and put it under the wobbling chest of drawers foot, improving the room by accident. Then she straightened, looked pleased with herself for half a second, and reached for her coat.

"I should go before your cousin decides football requires singing."

The sentence dropped too quickly into him.

"It's still early," he said.

"It's nearly ten."

"I can walk you."

"I assumed you would. The hallway smells like old soup."

He helped her into the coat because the room was too cramped for elegance. His fingers brushed the back of her neck under the collar, just once, because her hair had caught. She did not move away. He was suddenly aware of everything at once: the warmth still coming off the stove, the football murmur through the wall, the wet cuffs of her jeans not fully dry, the place on his own shirt where steam from the sink had dampened it.

At the door she paused, glanced back into the room, and said, "Buy another sour cream next time. This one barely survived you."

"Next time?"

"Don't be stupid."

They went down the stairs single file because the stairwell was narrow and the banister sticky with old gloss paint. The window on the landing had been painted shut years ago and kept a skin of grime that turned the streetlamp outside into a weak yellow blur. Somewhere below, a television laugh track rose and fell. The building held all its lives badly, with no insulation thick enough to protect anyone from anyone else.

At the first landing Eszter stopped and turned. Beni nearly stepped into her.

"What?" he said.

He had meant it neutrally. It came out careful, almost defensive, as if he were already bracing for some sensible end to the evening. Thank you for dinner. See you tomorrow. Don't worry about the room.

She looked at him for a second with that same directness she had carried all night, the refusal to soften facts into something more flattering. Then she took the front of his shirt in her fist, not hard, just enough to anchor him where he stood, and kissed him.

It was not tentative. It was also not theatrical. Her mouth was warm from the stairwell heat and still tasted faintly of paprika and lager foam. He felt the paint-cold banister at his back through his shirt, the rough knit of her jumper cuff against the side of his wrist, the quick shocked opening in his own chest that had nothing to do with surprise at the kiss itself. Desire did not surprise him. He knew desire. He knew how fast it could turn stupid and how much damage could fit inside a body wanting another body.

What hit him was the looking that had led to it. She had seen the room: the blanket tucked too tight, the damp wall, Laci and Friday and the bad knife and the entire humiliating layout of his life. She had watched him eat as if hunger did not make him small. She had heard him say the worst sentence he had in him and had not mistaken it for a trick. Then she kissed him like all of that had been included, not excused, and not enough to send her elsewhere.

She drew back only far enough to see his face.

"You look robbed," she said softly.

He could not think of a useful answer.

One side of her mouth lifted. "What, you thought I brought paprika for the architecture?"

That got a breath out of him that was almost a laugh and almost not.

"I should go," she said.

"Yes."

"That sounded disappointed."

"Maybe."

"Good."

She kissed him once more, shorter this time, then let go of his shirt and started down the stairs. He stood where he was until she reached the bottom and looked up.

"Tomorrow," she said.

He nodded because speech had deserted him in a way that did not feel like failure.

Then she was out through the building door and into the wet yellow street, gone under the streetlamp shine toward the bus stop with her shoulders set against the damp.

Beni stayed on the landing a moment longer, his hand still half lifted from where it had failed to find anywhere to rest on her. The stairwell smelled of dust, boiled cabbage, and the ghost of her coat after rain. Above him the football commentary lifted in sudden outrage through Laci's wall. A child somewhere in the building laughed so hard it turned into a cough.

When he went back upstairs the room was the same room: damp in the wall corner, radiator ticking, two clean bowls on the drainer, blanket coming loose already at one edge. Nothing had improved except that now the place held evidence against him. Someone had come there on purpose. Someone had eaten at his table, mocked his menus, told his cousin to take off his boots, kissed him on the landing. It made a different life imaginable in one ugly clean stroke.

That was the dangerous part. It was not wanting her; wanting had never been the worst of it. The dangerous part was understanding, all at once, how much he wanted to be seen whole and not immediately put to work by it, and how little protection there was against losing a thing like that once it existed.


# Chapter 6

The loading bay outside the gallery had been swallowed by catering vans and a black Range Rover with diplomatic plates, so Beni was on his third circuit of the block by the time he slid into a space half on the yellow line. The rain had settled into the kind that never looked serious from inside a warm room and still found the gap at the back of your neck every time you stepped out in it. On the far corner a warden wrote one ticket, tucked the pad back into his jacket, and lit a cigarette under a shop awning. That gave Beni maybe six minutes before the man worked his way down this side of the street.

Nina had gone in at eight twenty-three in a black coat that looked simple until you saw the cut of the shoulders and the lining when she moved. She had said, Stay close, it won't be late, in the bright clipped voice she used when other people might hear. It never meant anything. In her world nobody said the real length of an evening out loud. They said one more drink, ten minutes, do stay, and the staff waited with the engine running while the meter of their own lives ticked somewhere else.

He moved the car again. The Mercedes glided forward one length, then another, silent as long money. The leather still held traces of Nina's perfume from the ride over, something peppery and expensive that sat above the radiator smell and the faint wool damp from her coat. Beni adjusted the rearview mirror by a fraction and watched people go in through the lit glass doors: men in dark jackets cut close at the waist, women in silk trousers and black boots that cost more than a month of his rent debt, a white-haired couple with the dry pink faces of drinkers who lived in heated rooms. They all entered as if the weather and the waiting cars were somebody else's arrangement, which of course they were.

At nine seventeen his phone buzzed in his inside pocket.

Inside. Now.

There was no thank you, no please, no explanation. He pulled into the nearest legal spot around the corner, keyed the meter with two pounds he should have kept, then stepped into the rain and locked the car. Water got through the seam of his collar at once. By the time he reached the gallery door, the cold had already worked a line down his spine.

The room inside was warmer than the street and no more comfortable for him. The walls were white, the floor was concrete, and tall welded shapes stood around the room looking to Beni like gates after a fire, though the little labels beside them would have had some cleaner word. The air held champagne, wet wool, perfume, and a tray of miniature tartlets already going soft. It was the kind of place where nobody raised a voice because the prices were hanging on the walls and had to be treated with the same manners as the guests.

The girl at the desk glanced at him, took in the Mercer suit, and stopped reaching for a guest list. She had a nicotine patch behind one ear and a silver pen tucked into the sleeve of her black knit top. Service recognized service even with the wages far apart. She nodded toward the back room.

Beni found Nina before he found Jonas because she was the only person in the room standing completely still. She had one hand around the stem of an empty champagne flute and the other resting on the crook of her opposite elbow as if she were posing for an old photograph of composure. Her smile was on her face but nowhere else. Across from her, Jonas Hale was talking to a collector Beni recognized from tabloid photographs, a woman with a lacquered bob and a husband who looked as if he had been ironed into his dinner jacket. Jonas had one thumb tucked under the edge of his glass, rubbing at a black stripe of graphite or clay that had lodged in the nail and would not come clean. He was half turned toward Nina and fully turned toward the people who might buy the work.

He looked younger than the room wanted him to look, which was part of his value. He was not young like Beni. He was young in the cultivated way: hair that had taken time to seem accidental, dark suit with no tie, one shirt cuff still carrying a dusting of white from the studio as if he had left in the middle of making something important. He was handsome enough that older women would call him interesting and mean it.

"There you are," Nina said to him.

She did not sound relieved. She sounded as if she were refusing the room the satisfaction of hearing anything softer.

"Your coat, Mrs. Ashdown?" Beni asked.

Jonas's eyes flicked to him in recognition. The pause was small and complete. Beni saw him place the memory: the pills, the catalogue, the errand boy in the Mercer suit.

"Perfect timing," Jonas said. His voice had that cultivated slackness Beni had heard before, the kind that pretended not to care even while arranging every word for effect. "Nina, your car's here."

Nina kept looking at him, not at Beni. "We said we'd go on somewhere after."

"I said I might, if this didn't become a performance."

The couple beside him performed not hearing that. The woman turned her head toward one of the sculptures with such exact politeness it became another way of staring.

"A performance," Nina said.

She smiled when she said it. That was worse. Beni had seen drunks, spouses, men outside clubs with split lips and women in hotel foyers fighting over keycards. This was different. Nobody here would admit a scene existed unless somebody broke a glass. They just let a sentence hang in the air and watched it do the work.

Jonas sighed very lightly, as if Nina were a scheduling problem he had hoped his assistant had already handled. "I'm not leaving my own opening at nine twenty because you've decided you're done being here."

"You've been done being here for half an hour."

"Nina."

He said her name with a warning softness that made three nearby people suddenly care very hard about their wine. Then he made the mistake or the choice of glancing past her shoulder at Beni again.

"Please go home," he said. "And don't make me manage this in front of everyone."

Manage was the word that landed. Beni felt it in the room the way you felt a car drop into the wrong gear. Quiet enough for cover, it gave everyone present a clean way to claim later that nothing dramatic had happened at all.

Nina did not slap him or cry or do anything useful to the story people would tell afterward. She set the empty flute on the nearest plinth with careful fingers, and Beni saw the base leave a wet ring beside a small card that probably described the work in language nobody in Tottenham had ever needed. Then she turned to him.

"My coat," she said.

Beni took it from the stand by the door and held it while she slipped her arms in. Her right hand missed the sleeve once before finding it. That was the only visible crack. Up close he could smell the champagne she had barely drunk and the colder sharper smell under it, the kind of sweat people produced while trying not to look as bad as they felt.

Jonas was already turning back to his collector. "Camilla, as I was saying, the earlier series was too obedient to the foundry."

Nina laughed once under her breath, sour and private. "Of course it was."

Then she walked out, and Beni followed.

On the pavement she did not ask for an umbrella. Rain spotted the satin at her shoulders before they reached the car. He opened the rear door and she got in with a speed that told him she wanted the evening reduced to mechanics as fast as possible.

"Home?" he asked once he was behind the wheel.

"Drive."

"Where?"

"Just drive, Beni."

He pulled away from the curb and let the satnav route home idle silently on the screen without touching it. At this hour central London thickened instead of clearing. Taxis nosed into gaps that did not exist. A bus leaned red and wet along the next lane. Pedestrians crossed against lights holding tote bags over their heads as if canvas could keep November out. The wipers made their flat, expensive sweep. In the mirror Nina looked less wrecked than sharpened, which was another thing money bought: the right mascara did not run, the right foundation turned blotching into almost nothing, the right coat hid the tremor in your shoulders from everybody except the man paid to drive you.

They sat in silence as he crawled them past Berkeley Square and down toward Hyde Park Corner. He kept his eyes mostly on the road because mirrors could become invitations if you used them wrong. When he did glance back she was no longer holding the flute. She had taken off one heel and was rubbing the side of her foot with two fingers, slow and hard, as if punishing the place where the shoe had bitten her. The other heel still hung from her toes. Her head was tipped back against the seat.

"He does that with his thumb when he's lying," she said.

Beni waited a beat. "What?"

"That filthy little nail. He rubs it as if he can erase himself into authenticity. God."

The laugh this time had more air in it and less control.

"I shouldn't have gone," she said. "That was the vulgar part. Not sleeping with him. Turning up after he'd already started wriggling away and pretending I was too grown up to care."

Beni turned left with no room to spare between a taxi and a van. The wheels hissed through gutter water. "Do you want me to take you home now?"

"Did you miss the last sentence?"

"No."

"Then don't rescue me with logistics yet."

He drove another block. He knew the route home three different ways, knew where traffic would loosen and where it would clot again near Knightsbridge, knew the timing of lights well enough to save three minutes if he caught the slip at the right moment. The road was easier than the person in the back seat. Roads told the truth if you watched them. People did not.

"Were you watching?" she asked.

"At the gallery?"

"No, at Wimbledon. Yes, at the gallery."

He could have lied. He had become good at the small clean lie, the one that let rich people keep their faces. But there was a strain in her voice that wanted the truth and might punish softness more than cruelty.

"I saw enough," he said.

"Enough." She rolled the word around as if testing its cut. "That's your whole method, isn't it. Enough to know where the bodies are, never enough to count as having been in the room."

He did not answer.

After a moment she leaned forward between the seats. Her hand came to rest on the leather beside his shoulder, close enough that he could smell rain on the cuff of her coat. Her rings tapped once against the trim.

"Did I look pathetic?"

He tightened his grip on the wheel. The question sat there like a child on the road. If he hit it, he was finished. If he swerved, maybe also.

"No," he said finally.

"That pause was very nearly honest."

"You didn't look pathetic."

"I looked older than him."

Beni said nothing because anything he said next would be used for something she had already decided to need.

"That's not the same thing as ugly," she went on. "I know that. I'm not fifteen. But rooms like that can smell age the way dogs smell fear. Especially on women. A man grows lines and he's serious. A woman grows lines and suddenly she's meant to have become wise or harmless."

Her hand moved from the seat to the back of his headrest, fingers curling there, almost touching him.

"He was cruel on purpose," she said. "That was the point. To let me know he could do it without even raising his voice."

"Yes," Beni said before he could stop himself.

She was quiet. Then she laughed again, low and ugly now, not for display.

"There. That's better. Christ, you're practically a luxury good, do you know that? A man who answers when the answer hurts."

He felt heat rise under his collar, not cleanly. Praise from her never came without a hook buried in it.

"I'm your driver," he said.

"For work, yes."

They were coming off the roundabout now, traffic easing just enough for speed. The city outside smeared itself into wet lights and shop fronts. Beni could feel the engine wanting a cleaner road beneath it. In the mirror Nina had sat back again, but the air in the car had shifted. It had lost the dead public chill from the gallery and become something smaller, more dangerous, as if the walls had edged inward.

"How old are you, Beni?" she asked.

He knew she knew. The Ashdown house knew everything it needed about the people it paid.

"Nineteen."

"Nineteen," she repeated. "My God."

The sound came out appraising, warmed by appetite. She was laying one fact beside another and enjoying the heat that came off the contact.

"Do you have any idea," she said, "what it costs to humiliate yourself properly at forty-one?"

He thought of answering with money, with hours, with a number exact enough to break the spell. Instead he said, "No."

"No, of course you don't."

The road narrowed where vans had half blocked a lane for some late delivery. Brake lights flared. Beni slowed.

"But you know something about wanting to be picked," she said.

This time he did look in the mirror. Her gaze met his and stayed there. There was no softness in it. That was not what made it hard to leave.

"Mrs. Ashdown," he said.

"Don't."

The single word came quick and sharp.

"What?"

"Don't hide behind that now."

He looked back at the road. He should have turned the radio on low, reset the air, restored the ordinary scale of things. He should have delivered her to Belgravia, opened the door, and driven north with the smell of rain in his clothes until the whole night shrank to another shift. He thought of the back seat where Bea's school bag sometimes slid on corners, of the polished step plate his own shoes were meant to avoid scuffing, of the stupid precise knot of his tie. He thought, stupidly and too late, of Eszter kissing him on the stairwell landing with onion and paprika still on both their hands. That memory should have pulled him back. It flashed and vanished.

Nina did not speak again until he turned into the mews behind the townhouse.

"Close the garage," she said.

He clicked the remote. The door rolled up with its mechanical groan, white light falling across the bonnet. He drove in, straightened the car between the painted lines, and cut the engine. The hush afterward was never true silence in those garages. Metal ticked as it cooled. Rain rattled once on the door before the roller came down behind them. Somewhere above, in the house, a pipe knocked and settled.

Beni unfastened his seat belt. "I'll get your door."

"No."

He turned slightly. She was leaning toward the middle now, one heel on, one off, coat open over the black dress. The humiliation from the gallery had not left her face. It had changed temperature.

"Come here," she said.

He knew exactly what she meant and still bought himself a second with stupidity. "Where?"

She looked at the back seat as if he were being tiresome on purpose. "Must I diagram the car?"

"You should go inside."

"Yes, I should. I should do about twelve things that would look better from the outside." She reached down, slipped off the remaining shoe, and let it fall onto the floor mat. "I'm not asking about best practice. Come here."

He ought to have got out then. The door handle was already in his hand. Cold garage air touched the side of his face. Her dropped shoe lay on the floor mat between them like something he had been called back to clear. He had spent weeks moving the instant she named the job: pills to the studio, Bea to school, wait here, come upstairs, not that door, use this one. "Come here" landed in the same trained part of him. By the time he opened his own door he was already obeying.

He opened his door, stepped out into the cold strip of space beside the car, and crossed to the rear. He had opened that door for Nina dozens of times. He had done it for Bea, for Oscar, for donors who did not look at his face. He had never climbed in himself. The motion felt wrong before it felt intimate, like entering a room in the house he was only supposed to clean around.

The leather was warm where Nina had been sitting, and it was warmer where she had shifted. The back seat smelled of her perfume, rain, and the faint powdery trace of Bea's school days that the valeting never fully erased. Beni pulled the door shut after him and the garage disappeared. There was only the dim overhead light, the windows already filming over again, and Nina beside him, close enough now that he could see the small dry crack in her lipstick at the center of her lower lip.

"Are you frightened?" she asked.

"No."

"You should be a little."

She said it almost lightly. Then she reached for his tie and drew him toward her.

The first kiss was not elegant. Her mouth was cold from the street and warm underneath, champagne sharp on her tongue. She held him by the tie as if she did not trust him to stay otherwise. He put one hand on the seat behind her to keep from crushing her and felt her reject that caution at once; she caught his wrist and pulled his hand to her waist, to the seam of the dress, lower.

"Not like I'm breakable," she said.

"I didn't think that."

"Good."

She kissed him again, harder now, and bit his lower lip when he hesitated. The pain went through him like a wire. His hand stayed where she had dragged it, lower at her waist, the way it stayed on a door once somebody richer than him had already walked through.

She leaned back enough to look at him. "If you don't want this, get out now."

She offered him the door in a tone that made leaving sound like failure. His tie was still in her fist. His knee was jammed against the seat. Her bag pressed against his thigh. The garage light hummed over both of them. He could still move. He did not.

"I didn't say stop," she said quietly.

"I know."

The pins or clasps in her evening hair had begun to loosen; one section had fallen against her neck, and when his mouth touched the skin there it tasted of rainwater and expensive powder and the salt under both.

She shrugged one shoulder free of the coat and then the other. It bunched beneath her like another person pushed down and forgotten. Under the dress she was warm and firm in some places, softer in others, more real than the rooms upstairs ever allowed her to be. Her bra strap had rubbed a faint red line into the top of her shoulder. The sight of it made something low in him turn over.

"Take this off," she said, tugging at his tie.

He fumbled the knot loose. She watched, impatient and intent, while he pulled it over his head and let it drop to the floor. Then her fingers were on his shirt buttons, not bothering with patience there either, opening enough to get her hand inside and drag her nails once, lightly, down the center of his chest. He sucked air through his teeth.

"You're shaking," she said.

"It's cold."

She smiled for the first time in the car, and there was no kindness in it. "Liar."

The dress had a hidden zip at the side. He found it by touch because she pushed his hand there. It came down with a small metallic rasp. Black fabric loosened over her hips. "Go on," she said.

Whatever else the scene was, it was not theatrical from the waist down.

"Again," she said.

He did it again. Slower. Her head tipped back against the seat and thudded once against the leather. The sound, the enclosed space, the neat dark shape of the garage beyond the fogging windows, all of it sharpened the fact that he could still stop. He could say her name and pull away and hand the night back to the house. Instead he let her guide his wrist. He watched the tendons in her throat move when she swallowed. He was already losing the part of himself that could describe this cleanly.

"Take them off," she said, meaning the underwear.

He hooked his fingers into the silk and dragged it down over her hips and thighs. She lifted herself impatiently to help him. One heel had come entirely off now; the other stockinged foot pressed against the opposite seat. The underwear caught at her ankle and she kicked it loose onto the floor beside his tie.

For a second he just looked at her. Forty-one sat on her body differently than it would have sat on a woman in a film or an advert or one of the smirking fantasies boys built in locker rooms. It sat where life had pressed: the slight heaviness low at her belly, the pale lines at the top of her thighs, the breasts shaped by a bra she had paid a lot for and a daughter she had carried seventeen years ago. None of it lessened the charge. If anything it made it worse. It made her less like a beautiful idea and more like an actual person who had decided to use him.

He went down between her knees.

The floor well was too narrow for comfort. His shin knocked the center console and his shoulder hit the door once before he found an angle that let him breathe. The awkwardness ought to have broken the spell. Instead it made the whole thing meaner and more exact. He was in the back of the car on his knees, mouth on the woman who paid him, while the townhouse stood above them with its polished banisters and bowls of lilies and a husband somewhere in the same city. That knowledge ran through him with the same hot shame as her fingers in his hair.

She tasted of champagne and skin and something darker under both. When he licked her she made a sound she seemed angry to have made, grabbed the back of his head, and held him there, not enough to force him and more than enough to direct him. He let her. "That's it," she said, almost to herself. "Don't look at me. Just do that."

The words dropped on him like instructions at a service door. He kept his eyes on the silk bunched at her waist and the task in front of him, and he did as he was told. Her hips moved against his mouth with impatience first, then with the loss of rhythm that meant she was giving up on appearances. The windows clouded thicker. The car smelled of sex now, blunt as heat in a laundry room. When she came it was not graceful. Her whole body went tight and then rough, thighs clamping once hard enough against his shoulders to hurt.
