"God," she said. "I needed that."

He was breathing too hard. His cock ached painfully against the seam of his trousers. She saw it at once. Of course she did.

"Bag," she said, nodding toward the corner where she'd dropped it.

He reached for the small black evening bag and handed it to her. She opened it without embarrassment. Phone, keys, a compact, the blister pack of pills he had once carried across London for her, and a square foil packet. She took the condom out and held it between two fingers for him.

The packet had been sitting there while she stood in the gallery waiting for Jonas to choose her in front of his buyers. The thought flashed through him so hard he almost dropped it.

She saw some part of the thought on his face. "Don't be adolescent about this."

He tore the foil open. His hands were clumsy now, less from inexperience than from the pressure of being watched. She watched anyway. When the condom was on, she hiked the dress up higher, turned in the seat with one knee under her and one foot braced on the floor, and took his hand to place it on her hip.

"Come here," she said again, softer this time, almost hoarse. "And don't start apologizing with your body."

He pushed into her and nearly lost the ability to think. The angle was wrong, then right, then too right. She sucked in air through her teeth and pushed back hard enough to take more of him. Leather creaked. The seat belt buckle knocked against the door in a small stupid rhythm. One of her rings scraped the fogged window as she caught herself there.

He had fucked before. The body's knowledge was not new. What kept striking him was everything around it: the condom from her evening bag, the heel turning on the floor mat, the seat belt buckle knocking the same door he opened for her after lunches and school runs, her voice giving orders in the same register she used for routes and timings. "Harder," she said, and he did it harder. She never asked if the angle was killing his knee or if he was close. She asked for more, and he kept moving.

"Harder," she said.

He did it harder. The car rocked once on its suspension. Both of them froze and listened. There was nothing from the house and nothing from the mews, only the cooling tick under the bonnet and their own breathing.

"Again," she said.

He put his hand over her mouth before the next thrust without even thinking why, only that the house was close and rich people hated being made audible by the people who served them. Her lips opened against his palm. She bit him lightly, not as refusal. The sound she made then went into his skin instead of the garage. The intimacy of that felt uglier than the sex itself.

He thought, suddenly and with complete uselessness, of another car years ago on a wet road outside town, of a married woman's hand on his wrist. The memory did not stop him. It only put wet road under the leather for a second, cheap sweet smoke under Nina's perfume.

Nina turned her face enough to speak against his hand. "Don't stop."

He didn't. Her body tightened again, different now, less sharp and more dragged from deeper down. She came with her forehead against the window, fog blooming around it, his hand still over her mouth. The moment after she did, she pulled his hand away and reached back for him by the hip as if claiming the right to finish the scene properly. The generosity of that, if it was generosity, nearly undid him. He came with his face against the damp cloth at her shoulder, trying not to make noise and failing in a way small enough that only the car could hear.

For a few seconds neither of them moved. His pulse went on hammering in the cramped expensive dark. Her breathing slowed first.

"All right," she said.

The words were for herself.

He pulled back carefully. The condom held. He tied it off with fingers that still did not feel entirely attached to him. Nina reached into the bag again, found a tissue packet, and handed him two tissues the way someone might pass a pen during a meeting. He cleaned himself, then the inside of his wrist where her lipstick had printed faintly in the struggle. The mark did not come fully off.

She adjusted her underwear, then paused, glanced down, and seemed to remember it was on the floor. Beni picked it up before she had to ask. The silk was damp and warm in his hand. He passed it to her without looking straight at it. She stepped back into it beneath the cover of the dress with practical impatience, hitched the stocking tops, pulled the zip up, smoothed the fabric over her thighs.

The transformation back into Mrs. Ashdown happened in pieces and faster than he would have believed if he had not watched it. She straightened her coat, pinned her hair with two sharp movements, opened the compact, and repaired her lipstick. By the time she looked at him again she was not the woman who had gripped the window and told him not to stop. She was close enough.

Only the loosened hair at the nape gave her away. That and the fact that he now knew how hard she had to hold her jaw not to show need.

"You should fix your shirt," she said.

He looked down. Two buttons open, tie on the floor, one cuff twisted. His hand had a crescent bite on it where she had caught him by accident or design.

"Right."

He tucked himself back into order. The tie took longer because his fingers wanted to shake now that the task was over. Nina watched him once, expression unreadable, then looked away and opened the door on her side.

Cold garage air slid in, smelling of wet concrete and petrol ghosts.

"Leave the car a minute before you bring it round in the morning," she said. "It needs air."

That was all. No thank you. No warning. No tenderness she would later have to retract.

She stepped out barefoot first, found the dropped heel, put it on, and then closed the door with soft expensive care so the sound did not carry into the house.

Beni stayed where he was.

Through the windscreen he could see only the lower panel of the garage door and the shine of water along its edge. Through the side window he saw Nina cross the short stretch to the inner door, one hand already lifting to her hair, posture gathering itself back into something the hallway would recognize. She did not look back. The door opened. Warm yellow light cut her shape for a second, then took her away.

The car kept the rest.

His tie lay in the footwell beside the crumpled foil and the tissue wrapped around the condom. One of her hairs, dark blond and almost invisible, had caught in the button of his cuff. He wound it off carefully, not out of sentiment but because leaving it there felt too much like evidence. The leather smelled of her and of the mechanical sweetness of the car's climate system trying to restore neutrality.

He sat in the back seat another half minute, maybe more. From there the front of the car looked orderly enough to belong to someone else: satnav dark, console shut, the driver's seat squared off and waiting for his shoulders. In the footwell lay his tie, the crumpled foil, the tissue twisted tight, and the damp crease her knee had left in the leather. Upstairs a pipe knocked once. The house was already swallowing the noise.

When he finally climbed back into the driver's seat, the mirror gave him his own face and, behind it in the dark glass, the blurred outline of the back seat where he should never have been. For one sick second cheap sweet smoke and rain on a county road in Hungary shoved Nina's perfume aside. Nothing new had opened tonight. His hand found the wheel with the same sick certainty it had once found a wet gear stick in another car, after another married woman. There was nothing to do now but drive on and leave the rest for morning.


# Chapter 7

At six forty-two the mews was still more night than morning. The strip of light under the garage door looked grey enough to wipe with a thumb. Beni had the rear door of the Mercedes open and one knee on the concrete, his good trousers darkening at the knee while he worked a towel into the seat seam. The leather had gone cold again. It still gave off stale perfume under the citrus spray from the valet kit, and every time he pressed harder the memory came back through his hands before it reached his head: Nina bent against the far door, one heel scraping the mat, his belt catching on the buckle, the windows turned white with breath so thick he had not been able to see the brick wall on the other side of the garage.

He had gone home for one hour and twelve minutes, which had been long enough to peel off the shirt that smelled of her mouth and the garage dust and long enough to stand under Laci's coughing shower while the pipe knocked in the wall, but not long enough to sleep. His body had the ugly looseness that came after not enough rest and too much adrenaline, a false softness that made him clumsy. He had tied his tie twice and still had not got the knot right. Now the tie hung inside his jacket pocket while he scrubbed at what the first wipe had missed: one cloudy thread of himself dried into the stitch line of the seat, a smear near the seat belt latch, glitter from Nina's face powder caught in the crease like pale grit.

There was a torn foil packet in the side pocket and one of her earrings on the floor mat, a small gold hoop with a pearl hanging from it. He had set the earring on the center console beside a folded receipt from the gallery caterer and a hair tie that was not hers. He did not know why he had kept lining things up instead of throwing them away. It felt like work if the objects stood in a row. It felt like less than what it was.

Bea would be in the back seat in less than two hours if Nina did not change the morning. That thought kept his hand moving when the spray smell made his stomach tilt. He cleaned for the girl as much as for Nina. He cleaned because the child would sling her school bag onto the same leather and complain about traffic and not know she was leaning against the place where her mother had bitten his shoulder through the shirt.

The roller door rattled once, then lifted halfway. Nina ducked under it before it had finished rising, one hand already on the hem of her camel coat so the metal would not brush it. She had a pair of dark glasses in her hair although there was no sun. Her face was washed clean and expensive-looking, pale around the mouth from a night that had not ended the way she had wanted. She had put herself back together so completely that the only proof of anything was the missing earring and the ladder in one stocking, visible where the coat opened over her knee.

She looked first at the console. "Thank God."

Beni stood up too quickly and felt the blood leave his head. He took the earring and held it out. "You dropped it."

She put it into her palm and shut her fingers around it in a quick protective fist. "It was my grandmother's," she said. "Or rather that's the family version. The true version is that my mother bought it at Sotheby's because my grandmother had much worse taste than anyone likes to admit."

It was the kind of thing Nina said when she wanted to sound amused instead of exposed. Beni watched her slide the hoop back through her ear with practiced fingers. She did not ask whether he had slept. She did not look at the inside of the car for more than a second.

"Is it dry?" she asked.

"Nearly."

"It needs to be dry. Bea has school at eight fifteen."

"It will be."

Nina nodded, already elsewhere. She reached into the inside pocket of her coat and took out a cream envelope, unsealed, the flap tucked in. "You've got Bea, then Petersham Cleaners, then the pharmacy on Elizabeth Street. I need the blue dress from Sloane before lunch, the navy one altered if they've finished ruining it, and stay reachable this afternoon because I may need you again."

She held out the envelope. He did not move.

"Take it, Beni."

He dried his hand on the towel before he took it. The paper was warm from her body.

"For the extra hours," she said. Then, because he was still looking at the envelope instead of pocketing it: "And because I hate untidy accounts."

He looked up. She had said it lightly, almost kindly, and that was worse than if she had made it vulgar. The garage smelled of the citrus spray and the petrol seep from somewhere under the wall and her perfume buried beneath both. It was hard to believe the night had contained anything frantic at all.

"Mrs. Ashdown," he said.

She made a face. "Don't do that."

He stopped.

"Not in here," she said. "Not after you've had your hand up my skirt. It turns us both into idiots."

The words were quiet. She was not angry, and it was not flirtation either. It was just an adjustment, as cool as changing the radio station because she did not like the song. She stepped closer and straightened the tie hanging half out of his jacket pocket, not bothering to ask. Her knuckles brushed the sore place on his chest where she had gripped him in the dark.

"Be normal in the house," she said. "Please. I'm not asking for a tragedy in a service corridor."

"I wasn't going to."

"Good." She glanced past him into the car at the damp patch darkening as it dried. "Last night was ugly enough before it improved. We don't need to turn the morning into theater."

He could have said no to the envelope before she touched him. He could not say no after the list of errands, the school run, the easy assumption that of course he would still be there, of course he would fold whatever the night had been into the day because that was what he was good for. He slipped the envelope inside his jacket.

Nina's mouth softened for one second, almost pity, almost triumph. "Thank you," she said. "And Beni. Keep the windows cracked for a bit. You went mad with the spray."

Then she was gone, heels clicking away through the mews toward the house. The roller door stayed open behind her. Cold air rolled in over the concrete and under the car, and Beni stood with the towel in his hand until the smell of her thinned enough for the citrus to win.

He finished the seat, though he no longer trusted it. The leather took the cloth and gave nothing back. By seven ten he had thrown away the foil packet in the outside bin, folded the towel into a black rubbish bag, and driven the Mercedes from the garage round to the front of the townhouse. The house had already begun its daily split. The upstairs windows held yellow lamp light and money. The basement steps were wet from a bucket someone had sloshed too quickly, and the smell rising from below was coffee, onion, bleach, toast. It was a machine that ran on polished quiet above ground and heat and wet cloth below.

He sat behind the wheel with both front windows open a finger's width and finally looked inside the envelope.

There were seven fifty-pound notes inside, new and stiff at the corners.

He counted them twice because his hands did not believe the first count. The total was three hundred and fifty, more than the night's work and the morning errands justified and less than enough to change anything. Laci's face came into his mind with automatic precision, the number the same each time: nine hundred. It meant Nina had put a price on the part that had not been in the job description and had chosen a number large enough that refusing it would feel like vanity.

"Is it?" she said, and got in without waiting for him to open the door because she never did if she was annoyed with the existence of school.

When he pulled away she sniffed once, then leaned forward between the seats.

"Christ. It smells like a dental surgery in here."

"Sorry."

She dropped back. "Not to me. To the car."

That was all. She put one earbud in and left the other dangling, looking out at the wet square with the expression she used for people who had personally arranged her timetable. At the red light on Eaton Terrace she bent to retie the loose shoe and muttered, not really to him, "If Mum buys one more arrangement of white lilies the whole house is going to smell like a funeral home."

It was a teenager's complaint, impatient and exact, and it hit him harder than if she had asked questions. He kept his eyes on the road and took the turn too neatly, putting extra care into the wheel because there was nowhere else to put it.

The morning packed itself with tasks. He dropped Bea at school and circled back west. He waited outside Petersham Cleaners behind a man in a waxed coat arguing about cashmere, picked up Nina's dress bag, long and uselessly heavy in tissue and plastic, and stood in a pharmacy that smelled of eucalyptus and money while the woman at the counter said Mrs. Ashdown's name the way receptionists said the names of women who never waited alone. He took the cream and the little white box the pharmacist added in a paper bag and did not look at the label.

He was good at this part. If the work stayed broken into destinations, tickets, timings, and the pressure of the steering wheel under his palms, he could move through it without letting the pieces touch. There was a route between Belgravia and Sloane Street that avoided the worst delivery lorries after nine. There was a place on Elizabeth Street where the wardens watched less closely if he stopped under the tree with the split trunk. There was a sequence for carrying expensive clothes without letting the plastic catch in the rain. There was even a way to hold the pharmacy bag away from the dress bag so the cream could not leak onto the silk if some idiot had failed to close the lid.

What there was not was a clean route between the woman upstairs, who had told him not to make a theater of it, and the woman in the basement kitchen, who had kissed him on a stairwell landing with onion on her fingers and no witness at all. Driving did not help with that. Every time he stopped at a light his mind tried to put both women in the same house at the same hour and failed like an overloaded fuse.

By a quarter past eleven he was back at the mews entrance with the dress bag on one arm and the pharmacy packet hooked in two fingers. Rain had started properly, not a mist now but a fine mean drizzle that found the back of his neck between collar and hairline. He took the service stairs down first because the kitchen was warmer and because he wanted five minutes before he went upstairs with Nina's things. He told himself he wanted coffee.

Eszter was at the stainless counter with her sleeves pushed up, scraping burnt milk skin out of a saucepan with the back of a spoon. Her hair was tied with a blue elastic that had stretched thin enough to show white lines in it. Someone had left a voice note playing low from her phone near the kettle: a child's voice in Hungarian, bright and indignant about a lost glove, then a cough, then Eszter's mother's slower answer from farther away. Eszter hit pause with the heel of her hand when she heard the door.

"There you are," she said. "The man of the hour. You look like shit."

Her tone was ordinary, almost cheerful, but it landed in him where the morning had not left room. He set the bags on the table more carefully than the objects deserved.

"Thanks," he said.

"You want coffee or you want to stand there growing mould?"

"Coffee."

She pointed with the spoon. "Then take the pot. My hands are disgusting."

He poured from the metal coffee pot into a chipped mug she always gave him because the handle had been glued twice and she did not trust anyone else not to finish the job of breaking it. The kitchen was damp-warm from boiling things, a good heat that got into his face and made the lack of sleep obvious. Eszter looked at him once, properly, and her expression shifted by a fraction.

"Long night?" she asked.

"Yes."

"Gallery people again?"

"And errands this morning."

"Mm."

The sound held more attention than agreement. She rinsed the spoon, wiped the counter with one swipe of a cloth, then moved the bowl of chopped parsley three inches to the left, the kind of needless correction that meant the room was getting on her nerves. Beni knew it. He knew it well enough now that seeing it should have warned him to stop talking like a route sheet.

Instead he lifted the mug and burned his tongue because he had forgotten to wait.

Eszter looked at the dress bag, then the pharmacy packet. "She has you shopping now."

"Today, yes."

"Lucky you."

He might have smiled another day. This morning the shape would not come. He drank again, slower.

The kitchen door swung open behind him. Nina came in without a coat now, only a pale cashmere sweater and dark trousers, barefoot in backless slippers that whispered on the tiles. She had put the missing earring back in. Her face held no trace of the garage except a little dryness at the corner of her mouth where she had bitten the lipstick off in the night. She glanced first at the bag on the table, then at Eszter, then at Beni, counting the room for hazards.

"Did you get the hem fixed?" she asked.

"They said two o'clock."

"Of course they did." She lifted the pharmacy packet and peered in. "And the cream?"

"In there."

"Good."

She did not thank him. She rarely did for ordinary work. Instead she set the paper bag down, reached past him for the dress bag, and in the same movement smoothed two fingers over the front of his jacket where a drop of rain had darkened the wool. The gesture lasted half a second. It was nothing if you had not seen what her hands did when the garage door was down. It was too much if you had.

"Keep your phone on," she said. "I may send you out again after lunch."

"Yes."

"And open the car up before you collect Bea. It still smells appalling."

Eszter's eyes moved, very slightly, from Nina's face to his and back. Nina must have felt it or decided she had said enough. She gave Eszter the kind of smile employers used when they wanted to appear intimate with staff while keeping every rung of the ladder intact.

"Could we have the lilies taken out of the front hall?" she said. "They've turned the place into a chapel."

"I'll do it."

"Thank you."

Nina lifted the pharmacy bag again and left. The slippered whisper faded up the service stairs.

For a few seconds the kitchen held only the kettle ticking on the hob and the slow tap from the leaky pipe above the sink. Eszter took the milk pan, rinsed it once, then set it back down and dried her hands on a tea towel. She did not look at him while she spoke.

"What happened?"

He should have had something ready by then, a human sentence with weather or traffic in it, something that sounded like a person talking to a woman he wanted rather than an employee answering a question in a hallway. What came out of him was the first available shape.

"Nothing happened. It was just late."

Eszter looked up. "That wasn't what I asked."

"The gallery ran late. She was in a mood."

"She's always in a mood."

"I know."

"Then try again."

He put the mug down. He could feel the damp of the envelope through the lining of his jacket where the rain had reached it, a paper softness over the notes. He had forgotten he still had it until her gaze dropped to his chest pocket.

"What is that?" she asked.

"Cash."

"I can see that."

"For the hours."

She laughed once, no amusement in it. "You sound like a payroll form."

The words stung because they were true. He was still answering like he was protecting a client, not speaking to her. He reached into the pocket, took out the envelope because hiding it suddenly looked guiltier than showing it, and laid it on the table. He kept one finger over the flap out of reflex.

"She paid extra," he said.

Eszter wiped the counter again although it was already clean. "For what?"

"For keeping me late."

"Until when?"

"Late."

"Jesus Christ, Beni." She threw the cloth into the sink. "Do you hear yourself?"

He did hear it. He heard the defensive flatness, the way each answer got smaller and dumber the more he trimmed it. The trouble was that the fuller version did not exist in any language he could use here. There was the truth upstairs in Nina's hands and the not-truth he needed downstairs with Eszter, and between them nothing that felt human enough to say aloud.

"She needed driving," he said. "Then errands today. That's all."

Eszter watched him for a moment so long he had to stop himself from filling it. Her eyes were dark and level and not kind in the lazy way some people called kindness when they meant softness. When she was still with him like this it felt worse than shouting.

"No," she said at last. "That isn't all."

"It is."

"Bullshit."

He looked past her shoulder at the row of labeled jars by the wall: flour, rice, lentils, pasta. They were ordinary words, safe ones. Eszter followed his glance and got angrier, not louder.

"Don't do that cupboard-face thing with me," she said. "I'm not one of her chairs."

The pronoun hung in the room between them, more dangerous because she had not named Nina. Beni felt heat go up the back of his neck despite the damp shirt cooling under his jacket.

"I'm tired," he said.

"Yes. I can see that." She took the envelope off the table before he could stop her, weighed it in her hand once, then put it back down. "This isn't tired money."

"You know she pays cash."

"I know she pays cash when she wants a thing done off the books. I also know what your face looks like when you've been driving all night, and I know what it looks like when something is wrong. Those are different faces."

He said nothing.

"Did she shout at you?" Eszter asked.

"No."

"Did she cry?"

"No."

"Did she make you sit there while she talked at you for three hours and forgot you were a person?"

He almost laughed because that would have been easier to explain. "No."

Eszter folded her arms. "Then what?"

He should not have looked at the service-stair door. The glance was tiny, an animal thing, but she saw it. Her mouth tightened.

"Ah," she said. "So it's that kind of what."

"You don't know what kind."

"No. I don't. That's the problem."

Rain clicked against the high basement window above the sink. Somewhere upstairs a vacuum cleaner started, stopped, started again. The house went on being a house. That was what made it hard. If there had been sirens or smashing or some dramatic proof, he could have met that. Instead there was a saucepan drying on the rack, a child paused inside a voice note on a phone by the kettle, and Eszter giving him the look she reserved for dirty things set down beside the bread.

"If you don't want me asking, say that," she said. "Don't stand there feeding me work answers."

"I'm not feeding you anything."

"No? 'For the hours.' 'She kept me late.' 'Errands.' You might as well hand me a bloody invoice."

He rubbed at the wet mark his coffee mug had left on the table. The surface was scarred from years of hot pans, knives, bleach, honest damage. He liked that table. He had liked sitting at it because nothing he said there seemed to matter less than food or more than it should. Now he had brought the wrong language to it and could not get it back out.

"I can't tell you every detail of her business," he said.

The moment the sentence left him he knew it was the wrong one, not because it admitted too much but because it put Nina between them like a fact of nature, something official, something that deserved guarding. Eszter's expression went flat.

"Her business," she repeated.

"You know what I mean."

"I do now."

She turned away and took the paused voice note from the counter, listening long enough to hear the child's cough again before she stopped it. When she spoke next her voice was steadier, which meant she had chosen each word.

"Listen to me. You don't owe me a report. We're not married, and I'm not your mother. But if something is off, don't piss on my shoes and tell me it's weather."

He let out one breath through his nose, nearly a laugh and not one. "I wouldn't say that."

"No. You'd say 'for the hours.'" She opened a drawer, took out a roll of bin bags, tore one off harder than needed. "You think because you lie politely it doesn't smell."

"Eszter."

"What?"

"Nothing."

"Exactly."

The word landed cleanly. She pulled a loaf from the bread tin, sawed off two thick slices, and set one on a plate without looking at him. She spread butter, then lard, then a dusting of paprika. Her movements stayed practical. That was worse than a scene. It meant she was making herself continue the morning even while he irritated every nerve in the room.

She pushed the plate toward him. "Eat. You look as if the radiator coughed you out."

He had not realized he was hungry until the smell hit him. The bread was still a little warm in the middle. He took a bite because refusing would have turned this into something larger and because he needed the steadiness of chewing. She watched him once and then looked back to the sink.

"I was going to ask if you're free Sunday," she said.

He swallowed too fast. "Sunday?"

"Yes, Sunday. It comes after Saturday. We could go to Finsbury Park if the weather isn't disgusting. There's a market, and I need a few things. Lili wants a stupid pink pencil case from the shop near the station, the one with glitter hearts that will fall off in a week." She shrugged one shoulder. "I was going to ask. Past tense now, apparently."

The chapter had been breathing around that possibility without him naming it: train ride, food, the market, an ordinary day that belonged to neither work nor Nina. Hearing it aloud now made the envelope in his pocket feel heavier than the notes deserved.

"I might be working," he said.

Eszter nodded once, clipped and exact. "Maybe."

"I don't know yet."

"Of course you don't."

She took the plate when he finished the first slice and set the second closer, an automatic kindness her face had not agreed to. He wanted to tell her yes to Sunday, yes before Nina filled the afternoon and the evening and whatever came after that. He wanted to say he would make himself free. What stopped him was not guilt in the grand sense. It was the more practical filth of having no idea whether he could keep that promise without inventing another sequence of explanations by tomorrow.

"Eszter," he said, and because he had no truthful continuation ready, the name just sat there.

She gave him a tired look that aged her and softened her at the same time. "That's not a sentence either."

His phone buzzed in his pocket.

They both looked at it. He did not need to check whose message it was. Nina had a way of timing things; the rest of the house bent around her schedule.

Eszter stepped back from the table. "Go on."

"It could be Mercer."

"And I could be the Queen."

He took out the phone. Nina's message sat on the screen in a blue bubble with no greeting and no apology for existing:

Come up when you're done.

He should have put the phone away without showing her, but he had already looked, and the change in his face told her enough. Eszter did not lean in to read. She did not need to.

"Right," she said.

"I've got to take the dress up."

"Obviously."

"She needs the alteration note."

"Beni."

He stopped because the way she said his name was not soft. It was exact.

"I'm not stupid," she said. "Whatever this is, I'm not going to beg it into English for you."

He opened his mouth.

"No," she said. "Save it. If you want to tell me something real later, do that. If you don't, then don't. But don't come down here with that face and that envelope and then make me the rude one for noticing."

She picked up her phone, thumbed the voice note back to the beginning, and turned away from him toward the stove. The child's voice started again in a burst of Hungarian complaint, bright enough to make the kitchen feel briefly full of a life that had nothing to do with him or Nina or the garage. Eszter listened with her head bent, one hand braced on the counter, and he knew from the shape of her shoulders that the conversation was over unless he was ready to break something open properly.

He wasn't ready.

He took the envelope from the table, then the dress bag and the pharmacy packet. At the door he looked back once. Eszter had already set a pot on the hob and was reaching for the onions. She did not tell him to stay. She did not tell him to go to hell either. She simply left him outside the circle of the next task.

Upstairs the house was warmer and quieter, the heat from the radiators trapped in carpets thick enough to swallow footfall. He climbed with Nina's dress bag knocking his leg and the envelope pressing against his ribs, and by the second landing he was already arranging the next version of the day in his head. Nina had kept him late after the gallery. Nina had paid extra because of the hours. Nothing had happened. Something had happened but not anything Eszter could prove. Sunday was uncertain. Sunday was probably work. The car smelled of spray because he had been overcareful.

The lies were not hard one by one. That was the ugly part. Each of them was small enough to carry. The work was in keeping them from touching, in remembering which stair belonged to which life, which woman got the clean answer and which one got the useful one. By the time he reached Nina's door he could already feel the strain in it, not moral, not yet, but mechanical, like driving with the wheels a fraction out of line. The car would still go where you pointed it. It would just keep asking for correction.


# Chapter 8

Beni put eight pounds on the meter before he changed his shirt. He did it in coins because that was what he had in the mug by the bed, and because feeding the machine one pound at a time let him feel, for a minute, like heat was something he had chosen instead of something he chased from room to room. The radiator clicked, then hissed. The front room warmed by degrees. It was not enough to matter to London, but it might matter to a woman coming over after work in damp sleeves.

He stripped the bed and made it again with the less-grey sheet. He folded the other one and pushed it behind the armchair with two old Mercers shirts and a towel that never finished drying. The flat still smelled of damp plaster, frying oil from downstairs, and whatever bleach Laci had spilled on his boots the night before. The window held a skin of rain. Each time a bus went down the High Road, the loose pane gave one thin rattle in the frame and then settled.

He should have liked the room better for being empty. Laci was on a late job in Wood Green, or at the pub after it, or both. His boots were gone from the hall. The television was dark. No lager cans sweated on the sill. But privacy only made the place look smaller. The bed took most of the floor. His jacket hung from the back of the chair with Nina's cash still in the inside pocket, folded once, too much money for such a cheap piece of cloth. He turned the jacket around so the pocket faced the wall. He had half expected the evening to die in the Ashdown kitchen, with her hand braced on the counter and her shoulders already turned back to work.

When Eszter knocked, it was with two quick taps of her knuckles, not waiting for the bell that had not worked in months. He opened the door before she could do it again.

She came in with a supermarket carrier looped over her wrist and a small blue Nivea tin already in her hand. Before she kissed him she flipped the lid with her thumb, dug a little cream out with her nail, and rubbed it into the raw places at the base of her fingers.

"Your stairs still smell like boiled socks," she said.

"That means it's home."

"That means some man in this building should be arrested."

He laughed, which relieved him more than he wanted to show. Her hair was tied back badly, the elastic sitting crooked and two damp pieces already loose by her ears, a repair done fast in a staff loo mirror between tasks. She had a dark coat damp at the shoulders and trainers with one lace replaced by a strip of black cord. She looked tired, but not the kind of tired rich women wore while other people reached for chairs and coats. She looked work-tired, and her mouth was still quick.

He took her coat. She took the room in with one fast glance that managed to be honest without being cruel.

"You made the bed," she said.

"I do know how."

"I didn't say you didn't. I said you made it like your landlord was coming to inspect your morals."

"Maybe my morals are very tidy."

"Then they're not yours."

She held up the carrier bag. Inside were a roast chicken in a cloudy plastic box, two white rolls, a jar of pickled yellow peppers wrapped in yesterday's Metro, and two cans of cheap lager.

"I wasn't cooking," she said. "If you want courtship, find a woman with more time."

"This is already courtship. You brought peppers."

"I brought peppers because English roast chicken tastes like someone told it bad news."

He took the bag from her and set it on the bed. She sat cross-legged against the wall without asking, on the cleanest stretch of blanket, back in the corner where the plaster flaked least. He liked that. Within a minute the bed looked used instead of displayed, and the flat's meanness stopped feeling like an accusation.

They ate with their fingers because there were only two clean forks and he did not want to leave her alone in the room while he checked whether Laci had washed up or only rearranged dirt. The chicken was still a little warm at the breastbone and already greasy-cold at the legs. Eszter peeled the plastic skin off one side and dropped it onto the lid with disgust.

"See?" she said. "Depressed bird."

"Maybe it worked a double shift."

"Then I respect it. I still don't want the skin."

She tore off a wing and handed it to him. He opened the jar of peppers. Vinegar and paprika hit the air hard enough to beat back the damp for a minute. Outside, a siren ran along the High Road and thinned into distance.

"What was for dinner upstairs?" he asked.

"Sea bass that nobody liked and potatoes arranged like architecture. Bea ate half a piece of toast and left for studying. Nina didn't come down. Oscar came in late and asked if there was lemon. There was lemon. It became a whole event."

"How many lemons?"

"Two. Sliced. As if I had personally hidden the rest of the citrus from him."

She mimed his voice so dryly that he nearly choked on bread.

"Cruel," he said.

"Accurate."

He watched her drink from the can. She wiped the foam from her top lip with the back of her hand, then reached for another piece of chicken with the ease of someone who had never once apologized for being hungry. That, too, he liked. With Nina everything had happened inside a car that smelled of leather and expensive perfume and the stale held breath of secrecy. Here there was grease on the blanket, pickle brine on his thumb, her knee warm against his thigh. The mess stayed in plain sight.

"You look wrecked," she said.

"I worked."

"Everybody works. You look like somebody ironed you while you were still inside the shirt."

"That's a good service. Mercer should offer it."

"No. They'd charge the clients and still make you do it."

He smiled. She kept looking at him.

"What?" he said.

"Nothing dramatic. You're just doing that thing again."

"What thing?"

"The one where you answer the first question and hide behind it."

He pulled a piece of bread apart to give his hands some small job. "I said I worked."

"Yes, and if I asked what color the sky was you'd tell me it was above us."

He looked down. The bread had gone gummy where the chicken fat touched it.

Eszter leaned back against the wall and bumped his shin with her foot. "I'm not interrogating you. Eat."

"I'm eating."

"See? Again."

The day had not even been bad by recent standards, only long and full of being useful. Nina had been cool with him in the morning, which had been worse than if she had been cruel. The extra cash sat in his jacket pocket like something hot. He lifted the jar of peppers instead.

"These help," he said.

"Beni."

"What?"

"If you don't want to talk, don't talk. But don't act like bad peppers are a full personality."

He laughed despite himself. "That's unfair. They're excellent peppers."

"Good. Then marry them."

They kept eating. It should have turned awkward then and somehow did not. She told him about a woman in Belgravia who had ordered a cleaner back to re-fold a blanket over a chair because the first fold looked "distracted." Beni told her about a Mercer client who once made him drive three extra streets because he did not want anyone seeing him get out outside a dermatologist's office. She switched into Hungarian to imitate the woman's horrible English vowels; he answered in the same language, and his shoulders dropped before he noticed. The jokes got meaner and funnier there. He did not have to set each word down like breakable crockery before it left his mouth.

When the food was half gone and both lager cans were empty, she licked pepper brine from her finger and said, "Tell me about Tottenham, then. Properly. Not driver version."

"What does that mean?"

"Driver version is mileage and traffic. Properly is where you buy cigarettes when you don't want the man at the corner shop asking why you look tired."

"That man asks anyway."

"Then he's doing his duty."

He shifted back against the wall. The bed springs gave one rusty complaint.

"Tottenham smells worse in rain," he said after a minute. "Everything gets pushed up together. Frying oil, wet cardboard, diesel, that sweet shit smell from the fried-chicken place by Seven Sisters when they burn the old grease. The buses sound different when the road's wet. Heavier. They don't hiss, they shove. If you miss the last one after two, you walk with all the other idiots pretending it isn't far."

"Better."

"The pigeons are violent."

"That's London."

"No, these are specific pigeons. These ones would take a baby's tooth if it looked edible."

"Very nice. Tourism board will call."

"The guy at the off-license has a cat with one ear. The cat's friendlier. In summer the lads stand outside the betting shop in socks and slides like they're on a beach holiday in hell. One window in the building opposite has had Christmas lights up for two years. Blue ones. They make the whole room look like a fish tank."

"There," she said softly. "That's a place."

He shrugged. He had not known he was holding something back until the words started moving and did not hurt.

"You?" he said. "Debrecen."

She grinned and folded one leg under herself. "Wind. Flat stupid wind because there is nothing to stop it and no hill to blame. The tram bells by the station. Those ugly concrete underpasses where everybody says not to go and everybody goes anyway. In winter the women on the number one tram all smell of cold wool and hair spray. In summer everything looks dusty even when it's clean. The town square pretends it's grand. It isn't grand. It just has room."

"And the food?"

"Better than here, obviously."

"Obviously."

"At the station you could get langos so hot the cheese slid off and burned your wrist if you were greedy. Which I was. The old women at the market sold sour cherries in plastic bowls and stared at your shoes to see if your life was failing. If I walk through Tottenham market and somebody stares at my shoes, I assume he wants my phone."

"Maybe he wants both."

"Then London wins."

He watched her while she talked. She used her hands more when she spoke about home, even now, even lightly. Her fingers still looked sore despite the cream rubbed into them. There was a pale crescent scar near one knuckle, probably from a knife, probably from work, probably from one fast stupid second long ago that no one else would remember. The urge to put his mouth on that scar embarrassed him by being both tender and hungry.

"You do that thing when you listen," she said.

"What thing?"

"You go very still like you're hiding under the table."

"Maybe I'm listening."

"Maybe. Or maybe you're halfway out the window."

He wiped his hand on the Metro page and read the same headline twice without seeing it.

"I'm here," he said.

Eszter nodded once. "Then be here."

She did not make it heavier than that. She took the chicken carcass back into its box, screwed the lid on the pepper jar, and moved the rubbish into the carrier bag with quick neat motions. Her hands got more precise when she was irritated. She was not angry yet. She was just done with doing both parts of a moment. He knew enough now to see that.

He reached for her wrist before she could sit back fully. "Stay."

"I'm staying."

"No, I meant..."

"I know what you meant."

She let him pull her closer. Her mouth tasted of lager and the sharp vinegar from the peppers. He kissed her once with his shoulders locked and his mouth barely landing, then again hard enough that the tightness dropped out of him, and that was better. She climbed over his legs to get nearer. Around them the bedsprings complained, the radiator ticked, the bus window gave its weak rattle. None of it ruined anything. Her hand went to the back of his neck and stayed there with a firmness that felt less like permission than like a decision.

When she kissed him she did not do it to be adored. She did it to get closer faster. He liked that too. He liked the weight of her when she half sat on his thigh, liked the practical way she took his hand and put it at her waist instead of waiting for him to guess correctly. Her jumper came off over her head with a static crackle. Under it she wore a plain dark bra, one strap twisted, the hook at the back bent enough to scrape his knuckle when he found it.

"Cheap bastard thing," she muttered.

"Me?"

"The bra. Though let's keep options open."

He laughed into her shoulder. She smelled of detergent, skin, and the ghost of whatever hand soap they used in the Ashdown staff bathroom. Nothing about it was perfumed or staged. He kissed the place where shoulder became chest. Her skin warmed under his mouth.

She touched the scar at his brow with one thumb. "This?"

"Bike."

"You were a terrible child, then."

"I was excellent. The bicycle was weak."

"Of course."

He slid his hand up her back and felt muscle under skin, the small damp warmth at the base of her spine. When he kissed her again she opened for him at once, with no rich-person calculation about timing or effect, and his body answered before he could think himself out of it. Want was the easiest part. Want was always the easiest part as long as nobody asked him to put words around it.

They shifted down awkwardly because the wall was there and the bed was narrow and one of his feet was still half trapped under the blanket. She laughed when he nearly elbowed himself in the face.

"Very elegant," she said.

"I trained for this."

"At Mercer?"

"Strict course. Advanced falling."

"I can tell."

She lay back against the pillows, hair half out now, and pulled him down with her. He kissed her throat. Her hand moved under his shirt and up his spine. He had spent so much of the last week being touched in ways that turned him into equipment that this almost hurt. It was not because it was gentler. It wasn't gentler, not really. It was mutual in a way that left him nowhere to hide.

He pushed his palm under the back of her bra. Her skin was hot there. She made a low sound in her throat, impatient more than shy, and he felt the sound in his stomach. When he kissed lower she let her head tip back against the wall with a soft thud.

"Careful," she said, half laughing. "If I die in your bed, your cousin will be unbearable."

"He'd ask if I still had rent."

"He'd ask before the ambulance."

"Probably."

Her knee opened against his hip. He slid his hand under the waistband of her tights and then under her underwear and found her already slick. Relief hit him hard and stupid. For one beat he treated the wet heat on his fingers as evidence that might spare him the harder part. She caught her breath and pressed into his hand once.

"Beni," she said.

He lifted his head. Her face had changed. It was not colder or afraid. It was just direct.

"What?" he asked.

"What do you actually want from me?"

The bus outside kept going, the radiator kept ticking, rain kept needling the pane. Even so, the question pinned him so cleanly that each sound seemed to come from farther away.

He knew what answer would work badly and what answer would work worse. He could say this, and sound like a boy. He could say you, and sound lazier than he felt. He could say I don't know, which would be true in a coward's shape. His hand was still between her legs. His mouth was on the warm skin above her bra. Want had been simple ten seconds earlier. Now it had to pass through his throat and come out attached to something larger than the bed, and the bed was suddenly all he could think about: the damp wall behind it, the chair with his jacket turned to hide cash he had not earned cleanly, the meter eating up the last of his coins so the room could feel less poor for two hours.

"Eszter..." he said.

"Don't say my name like you're buying time."

He shut his eyes. Her body under him stayed open, waiting, and that made it worse. She was not asking for forever. He knew she was not. She was asking him not to arrive in pieces. She was asking him not to use heat as a substitute for being there. The answer should have been small enough to say.

"I want you here," he said finally.

She watched him. "Here tonight?"

"Yes."

"And outside tonight?"

His hand had gone still. He made himself move it again, because stopping felt like another failure, but now he touched her like a man trying to pass a test with his hands while the rest of him stood outside the door: two dutiful strokes, no heat in them at all. She caught his wrist and held it, not hard.

"That's not what I mean," she said.

He nodded once. It was a useless substitute for language and he knew it.

"Then say what you mean."

He looked at the damp stain on the ceiling above her shoulder, the one shaped like something spilled and never scrubbed up. In the dark edge of it he could see road maps, borders, any line that let him think in distances instead of this. His body was betraying him in real time. He could feel himself softening against her thigh, not all at once, not theatrically, just enough that shame arrived before there was time to disguise it.

She felt it too. Her face did not change much. That made him more ashamed than if she had laughed.

"Fuck," he said quietly.

"Hey." She pushed herself up on one elbow. "I'm not taking marks."

"I know."

"Do you? Because you look like you're about to jump out the window."

He rolled off her and sat against the wall with his knees up, taking air in thin measured pulls that left his chest tight and his damp skin cold. The room felt overfull: chicken skin cooling in the plastic box, vinegar from the peppers, her jumper half on the floor, the sour heat from the radiator, his own skin suddenly cold where sweat touched air. He dragged both hands over his face and came away smelling pickle brine and her.

"Sorry," he said.

"Yes, obviously." She sat up too and fixed the twisted bra strap under her blouse without hurrying. "That's not the useful part."

With Nina the problem had not been words because words had not mattered. The body had been enough there, or less than enough, and that had been the whole point: he could leave the rest of himself out of it and still be wanted for something. Eszter's plain question felt less like accusation than like being handed a tool and finding no hand free to hold it. None of that would survive his mouth. It would come out self-pitying, or vague, or young in the worst way.

"I don't know how to answer right," he said.

"There isn't a right answer." Her voice had gone flatter now, cleaner. "There's only whether you're here with me or hiding in your own neck."

He let out a breath that scraped. "I'm here."

"No." She shook her head. "Your body's here. Then I ask one real thing and you go somewhere else. I can feel it happen."

He said nothing.

"I'm not asking for a wedding," she went on. "I'm not even asking for next month. I'm asking what this is when it isn't kissing in a stairwell or me feeding you in a kitchen. Because if it's only when you're hungry or lonely or hard, fine. Maybe that's enough for one night. But then say that, and I decide if I want it too."

He stared at the carrier bag by the bed. The Metro date showed through a grease mark: Wednesday. He had folded the paper around the jar without looking at the page. Some minister was smiling over some disaster, and the smile meant nothing.

"It's not only that," he said.

"Good. Then what else?"

He heard himself swallow. The radiator knocked twice. Down on the street somebody shouted and another voice answered, cheerful and drunk.

"When you're not in the kitchen," he said slowly, "I notice that first."

She did not interrupt.

"I wait for you there. Even when I don't need food. Even when I should go. If I hear you talking before I come in, I..." He stopped, then forced himself on. "I listen outside the door for a second like an idiot. On the road, if something stupid happens, I think about how I'd tell you. And then I don't tell you because I don't know how without sounding..." He shrugged once. "Like this."

Her face eased, but only by a little. "Better."

He gave a short laugh with no humor in it. "Very flattering."

"Don't be a prick. I'm helping you."

"Sorry."

"Stop apologizing for whole paragraphs. It wastes time."

He looked at her then. She had pulled her blouse closed but had not buttoned it. The bent bra hook still sat wrong under the fabric. Her hair was falling out around her face. One of her knees had a faint patch of flour or dust on the tights. She looked solid and tired and unsentimental enough to survive the truth, if he were brave enough to have one.

"I wanted you to come," he said.

"I know. I came because I wanted to."

"I know that too."

"Good. Then don't make me do all the speaking for both of us."

He rubbed his thumb along the seam of the blanket. The cloth was pilled rough as old felt. "I thought if I said the wrong thing you'd go."

"And instead you said almost nothing and nearly left without moving."

He winced.

"That's what I mean," she said, not unkindly. "You think disappearing is safer because it feels polite from inside your own head. It doesn't feel polite out here. It feels like being shut in a room with someone who keeps turning the light off."

That landed so cleanly he had to look away.

She stretched for the lager bag, found nothing in it, and clicked her tongue. "Do you have water?"

He got up at once, grateful for the job. In the kitchen he filled the plastic bottle from the tap, listening for Laci's key in the front door though he knew it was still early. The mirror over the sink showed a face too young for the kind of shame it was trying to carry with dignity. He came back before he could think long enough to make himself clumsy again.

Eszter took the bottle, drank, and handed it back. "Thank you."

That simple thank you made him feel worse than the rest of it.

"I didn't mean..." He stopped. Started again. "I wasn't trying to do that to you."

"I know. Intent isn't magic." She tucked one foot under herself and reached for her jumper, then changed her mind and left it where it was. "Listen. I like you. Quite a lot, which is inconvenient because you're nineteen and sometimes you act all nineteen years at once."

"I'm twenty in January."

"Marvelous. Then in January maybe you'll speak in full sentences."

He laughed before he could help it. She smiled, but only with one side of her mouth.

"I'm serious," she said. "Awkward is fine. New is fine. Even bad is fine, for a while. But blank isn't fine. If you don't know, say you don't know. If you want sex, say sex. If you want me, say me. If you get scared, say scared. Don't go dead and call it being careful."

He sat back down beside her without touching her yet. "All right."

"No. Not all right. Do you understand?"

"Yes."

"Say it like a person."

He looked at his own hands. "I understand."

"Good."

They sat in the warm, ugly room with the remains of the chicken between them and the rain working steadily at the window. He felt emptied out and overexposed at the same time. Still, the pressure in his chest had changed shape. It was not relief. Relief would have been simpler. It was closer to the pain in a hand after it came back from numbness.

After a minute she nudged his shoulder with hers.

"Also," she said, "for the record, you could have just said you wanted me to take my tights off and keep being nice to you. I'm not against simple requests."

He looked at her.

"What?"

"Nothing."

"Liar."

"A little."

"I noticed."

He smiled. She picked at the blanket where a thread had come loose and wound it once around her finger.

"Are you staying?" he asked.

"I said I was."

"I meant staying staying."

"Until your cousin starts pounding on the door or your meter dies. Whichever romance arrives first."

"The meter has eight pounds."

"Look at you. A provider."

"Don't mock my infrastructure."

"Never. It's all we have."

She lay down again, this time on top of the blanket, and patted the space beside her. He followed slower this time, leaving a hand's width of space until she closed it. When he turned toward her she took his wrist and set his hand flat on her stomach under the blouse, not lower, not yet. It was a reset. He understood that and was grateful for the plain rule of it.

Her body rose and fell under his palm. Outside, another bus shoved through the wet road. Somewhere in the building a child cried once and was quieted. The heat had run out of the moment. Now there was only her breathing under his hand, the damp window, the chicken smell turning stale. The drop into ordinary almost hurt, but he knew better now than to mistake ordinary for nothing. Ordinary was what people had to build on purpose.

"In Debrecen," she said into the dimness, "my aunt used to say men from flat places are bad liars because there is nowhere for the lie to hide."

"Is that true?"

"No. Men lie everywhere. She just liked sayings."

"We have old women for that too."

"I'm sure."

He traced one small circle over her blouse without meaning to. She caught his fingers before he could turn the touch restless again.

"I'm staying because I want to," she said. "Not because you earned some reward for admitting one thing."

"I know."

"Good. And next time, if you start disappearing while I'm in your bed, I put my bra back on and I go home. Understand?"

"Yes."

"And if there's something else going on with you, some real thing, don't hand me your silence like it's good manners."

He felt the words go through him and lodge. On the chair his jacket hung turned toward the wall, Nina's money still in the hidden pocket, paper weight dragging the cloth out of shape. In a few hours he would pull it on again for the Ashdowns. He lay still beside Eszter and listened to the radiator spend his coins a little at a time.

"I understand," he said.


# Chapter 9

The photograph was small enough to miss if you wanted to. It sat halfway down a tabloid site between a footballer's divorce and a television chef's tax trouble, four grainy frames under a headline about a married patroness slipping east after midnight. Nina's face was mostly turned away. Jonas Hale's corrugated studio door showed clearer than she did. The Ashdown Mercedes took up the front of the shot, black paint holding a stripe of streetlight and enough of the plate to make it theirs.

By half past nine it had already moved through more hands than Beni liked to imagine. One of the florists on the front railings had it open on her phone while unloading lilies from a white van. The porter in the mews had folded a printout in quarters and slid it under a clipboard as if hiding it turned it back into paper. In the basement corridor somebody had sent it to the house printer, grayscale and cheap, and the toner smell sat over the usual polish and radiator heat.

Beni parked by the garage wall and killed the engine. The jacket he had lifted off Eszter's chair a few hours earlier still held Nina's cash in the hidden pocket. The mechanical hush rushed in after the fan died, leather cooling under him, his own breath too loud for a second. On the passenger seat lay the route wallet for the day, clean enough from the outside. Inside it were timings, pick-up slips, the ordinary work of getting people rich enough never to know what their own days cost. One blurred photograph on a gossip site had turned all that ordinary work sharp.

He took the wallet upstairs by the service stair and found Marian Bell waiting outside Oscar's morning room with one shoe off.

Marian worked from Oscar's City office three days a week and from the townhouse the other two, moving papers between both places with a speed that made staff step aside without being told. Today she had a blister plaster above her right heel and her navy pump lay under the side table beside a damp umbrella. She was holding a peppermint lozenge in one cheek and rolling its twisted wrapper into a white cord between thumb and forefinger.

"Mr. Varga," she said. "Could you come in for a moment?"

The room smelled of coffee gone thin in the pot and printer ink. Oscar was not there. His laptop sat open at one end of the table, screen asleep, a legal pad squared carefully beside it. Marian had spread three pages in front of the empty chair: a printout of the article, a household diary sheet, and a blank note page headed with Oscar's office address.

"You saw it?" she asked.

"Yes."

"All right." She sat, put her bare heel flat on the carpet, and looked at him over the printout. "One of the captions names Jonas Hale. A second one identifies the car as the Ashdown Mercedes. It doesn't matter if that identification is speculative. If it stays online all morning it becomes fact by lunch."

Beni looked at the page instead of her. Nina was getting out of the rear seat with one hand at her hair, shoulders tucked against the flash. Jonas's roller shutter stood behind her with a strip of white paint on the lower edge, the same strip Beni remembered from the first night she had him circle east instead of taking her home. He had spent weeks making trips like that vanish into traffic, into cash, into the sort of respectable vagueness rich people called discretion when they wanted it from someone poorer.

Marian tapped the household diary. "On Tuesday evening Mrs. Ashdown was logged out for Hamilton White in Mayfair. Back in for home by ten twenty. That's what the diary says. Oscar needs route confirmation before he asks Mercer for anything and gets an answer that doesn't line up."

"There was traffic," Beni said.

"There's always traffic." She did not smile. "I need route, sequence, and approximate times."

He kept his face as blank as he could. "We left the gallery late."

"Late enough to be photographed in Hackney Wick?"

The question came clean, almost gentle. That made it worse. If she'd pushed, he could have braced against it. Instead she let the distance sit there between Mayfair and Hackney Wick, all the bridges and lights and turns he knew by feel, all the miles he had pretended were something else.

"Mrs. Ashdown had a change of plan," he said.

"Fine. Then I need the changed plan." Marian unwound the lozenge wrapper and rewound it. "I don't care where she went in any moral sense, Mr. Varga. I care whether the answer in this room matches the answer in Mercer's records if Oscar asks for it. That's all."

It was not all. It was payroll, reference, who got spoken to in what tone when something embarrassing moved from gossip into administration. But she was right about the shape of it. Nobody in the room was interested in Nina's feelings. They were interested in whether there would be a paper trail and whose handwriting might sit at the end of it.

"I can check at the depot," he said.

"Please do." Her gaze flicked to the open notebook. "Before noon, if possible."

Beni nodded. He should have left there. Instead he heard himself say, "Mrs. Ashdown asked for me on those runs."

Marian's fingers stopped on the wrapper. It was the first sign she had actually registered him as more than a moving part. "I'm sure she did."

Nothing in her face changed. Still, he felt the answer land with the flat weight of payroll. She was offering neither protection nor gratitude, only the obvious fact that this was how it worked.

She slid the article toward him by two inches. "If there is an innocent explanation, bring me the one Mercer can live with. If there isn't, bring me the version that won't make them defensive. Defensive companies discover principles very quickly."

"All right."

"And Mr. Varga." She bent to put her shoe back on, wincing once before she hid it. "Don't improvise twice. The second version is always the one people remember."

He went out by the service corridor and down the back stairs. In the basement the printer tray still held one extra copy of the article. Somebody had left it face down but not before smudging a thumb over Nina's shoulder. Cook's radio was off. Two women from the flower company stood in the hall with wet cardboard under their shoes and stopped talking when he passed. On any other morning the house swallowed embarrassment whole. Today the embarrassment had a URL, and that made everybody feel permitted to glance at it.

Outside, the mews air smelled of wet leaves, diesel, and the hot-metal breath coming off the boiler vent. Beni sat in the driver's seat without starting the engine. The garage sex had happened only last week and already felt older than that, the back seat still there under him, the stitched leather, the place where Nina had pulled him by the tie like she was selecting something from her own house. Since then there had been cash, messages, extra errands, the way dispatch now gave him the Ashdown work before he asked. He told himself he was protecting the job because he needed the money. That was true. It just wasn't the whole truth.

He took out his phone. No message from Nina. Nothing from Mercer either. The silence felt deliberate, though that was probably vanity. Houses like this did not need to speak to make a man move.

At the depot the article had got there ahead of him too.

The drivers' room television was on mute with a sports panel nobody was watching. Someone had left the Metro open on the table, but the tabloid image was on two phones and the dispatch desk monitor, blown up until the pixels broke into blocks. Terry from valeting was dragging a hoover hose past bay three and slowed long enough to look at Beni, then away, then back again with the frank curiosity of somebody who did not have enough money for discretion to be a professional requirement.

Noor was by the radiator with his lunch tiffin open on the bench beside him. One latch on the steel container had long ago snapped off and been replaced with a wrap of blue electrician's tape. He was eating with a plastic spoon from the top tier, lentils thick with chilli oil, and doing the quick crossword in biro on the back of a dispatch printout.

He looked up at Beni once, properly, and folded the paper in half.

"You look rough," he said. "What, did one of them die?"

"Not yet."

That got the corner of Noor's mouth up. It stayed there half a second, then went. "Right. So it's work."

Beni sat opposite him. The radiator clicked behind Noor's shoulders. From the workshop came the hollow bang of a car door and the high whine of a buffer. Everything in the room sounded too ordinary for what Beni had carried in.

"You've seen it," Beni said.

"Everyone's seen it. Terry saw it, and Terry thinks Hackney Wick is a football formation." Noor nudged the tiffin toward him. "Eat."

"I'm not hungry."

"That's not what I asked."

Beni took the spoon because not taking it would have made the refusal childish. The lentils were too hot and over-salted in the way Noor liked them, enough chilli to sting the split at the side of his lip. The heat made his eyes water slightly. Noor pretended not to notice.

"Oscar's office wants route confirmation," Beni said.

"Of course they do."

"The book won't line up."

Noor leaned back, spoon hanging between two fingers. He looked not surprised, only annoyed on Beni's behalf, which was worse in a different way. It meant the pattern was already familiar to him.

"How far off?" he asked.

"Enough."

"That's not a number either."

"East enough to matter."

Noor let out a breath through his nose. "All right. Here's the bad bit. This isn't about a photo. Photos blow over. The minute somebody in a nice shirt asks for paperwork, it stops being an affair or a favour or whatever bollocks they call it in that house. It turns into admin."

He wiped his spoon on the lid with his thumb, looking at the metal more than at Beni for a second.

"Admin is where they leave you," he said. "Not them. You. Her name turns into 'client'. His name turns into 'press interest'. Your name stays your name and your initials stay on the line."

Beni looked toward the dispatch office. Through the glass he could see the top of the mileage cabinet and the row of labelled key pegs behind it. Ashdown had its own slot, brass tag polished more often than the others because that was the sort of client Mercer liked to keep.

"Maybe it can still be explained," he said.

"Maybe." Noor scraped the rest of the lentils together. "Then say what happened before anyone starts tidying it."

Beni gave a thin shrug. "I can't tell them where she was."

"I'm not telling you to." Noor put the spoon down. "I'm telling you not to confuse getting picked with getting covered."

The sentence sat between them with no drama around it. Noor did not dress it up. He did not lower his voice. He just said it as if it belonged in the same world as tyre pressure and unpaid congestion charges.

Beni stared at the folded crossword. One clue had a circle around it in Noor's blunt handwriting. Five letters. Quiet.

Beni said nothing.

Noor watched him another moment, then reached into the lower tier of the tiffin and came up with half a paratha wrapped in foil. He set it on the table between them and nudged it across with two fingers.

"Take that as well," he said. "You do stupid things faster on an empty stomach."

"I'm not doing anything."

"Course you're not." Noor's tone stayed easy, but his eyes did not. "You've got that look already."

The buffer whined again in the workshop. Somebody shouted for keys. A printer started up in dispatch, the paper feed making its dry mechanical cough. The day kept moving. Beni felt himself outside it for a second, like he had stepped half a foot off the curb and not yet decided whether to go back or cross.

"If the sheet's wrong," Noor said, "tell them it's wrong. Tell them she added a stop and you wrote it badly. Let them bollock you for it."

"Will they?"

"Maybe. Maybe not. Still better than making the book prettier than the night was."

Beni looked up at him. Noor had gone still except for one finger tapping the foil parcel. His suit cuffs were shiny with old wear, the black cloth gone grey where steering wheels and jacket sleeves had rubbed them for years. There was nothing grand in him, no sermon in the face he was making. Just a man who had done enough of this work to know where the trap door usually sat.

"You think I'd do that," Beni said.

Noor gave him a look that was almost tired. "I think you want to keep that job. I think you like that she asks for you. That's enough."

There it was, and because Noor had not said it cruelly Beni had nothing easy to fight.

He remembered Nina in the back seat after the gallery, one heel on the floor mat, one knee on the leather, saying his name as if she had discovered it could buy her another hour of not being humiliated. He remembered the cash envelope the next morning, thick and neat, laid beside the errands list like a tip and a warning at once. He remembered Eszter on his bed asking what he actually wanted and his own body going blank at the question. Chosen was not the same as known. He knew that. He just kept behaving as if the difference might close if he was useful enough.

"I'll sort it," he said.

Noor leaned back.

"Yeah," he said. "Don't say that to me."

He took the spoon from Beni's hand and packed the tiffin shut with the heel of his palm. The blue tape gave a soft tacky sound.

"If you go near that book," he said, "call it what it is."

Beni stood because staying seated would have meant either arguing or agreeing. He wanted neither. "I've got a school run."

"Then do the school run."

"I'm doing it."

Noor looked at him one last time. "You're nineteen, Ben. Nineteen feels old when you're paying rent. It isn't. Don't put rich people's secrets in your handwriting."

Beni left before the warmth in Noor's voice could make anything softer. Outside, the air had gone colder. It was not yet three and already the light was thinning into the dirty silver London used before dark. He took Bea to school, brought the car back, waited in Belgravia while a dog walker blocked the mews with two soaked retrievers, and drove Nina nowhere because she did not come down. At six the house released him. At seven dispatch sent him on a Paddington station collection. At nine thirty he came back to the depot with rain on the roof and the Ashdown keys in his pocket.

The night dispatcher was on the phone with a late Heathrow client and did not look up when Beni signed the key sheet. The office glass reflected the fluorescent strips in pale bars. Behind it the mileage cabinet stood half open because nobody thought drivers needed a crowbar when they already had trust.

Beni hung his coat on the back of the chair and waited until the dispatcher turned away again, voice pitched falsely calm for whoever was shouting in his earpiece. Then he stepped inside and opened the grey drawer marked `ASHDOWN`.

The book was heavier than it looked, black cloth over board, corners blunted by years of hands. Mercer liked paper that suggested permanence. Inside, each page was ruled in blue with columns for date, driver, odometer out, odometer in, principal stops, and notes. At the top of the cover sheet a typed line had been taped in place months ago:

Any amendment must be struck through once and initialled by dispatch.

Beni set the book on the desk and turned to Tuesday.

His own writing stared back at him. He recognised the way his sevens leaned left, the extra pressure he always put on capital letters in English as if he still expected them to be inspected. The line he needed was midway down the page.

`Tue 12 Nov | BV | out 31418.3 | in 31430.8 | Hamilton White W1, home SW1 | late traffic`

That was the lie as he had first told it: small, plausible, meant to disappear. Gallery to home. Twelve and a half miles on a wet night with bad roads and no further questions.

The photograph online had put the car in Hackney Wick at 21:52.

He pulled the route slips file toward him. Tuesday's carbon copy still sat clipped behind the invoice sheet, thinner paper, his timings in the margin:

19:06 Belgravia out  
19:31 Hamilton White  
20:48 car called  
21:52 outside  
22:37 Belgravia in

He had not written where outside was. At the time that had felt clever. Now the blank looked like a spotlight.

If he left it, Mercer would hand over a gap. If he owned the gap, Marian might call it sloppy and Oscar might still keep him because clients disliked retraining drivers. If he altered it, the lie would line up. That was the lure his mind kept sliding back to: not innocence or safety, only the ugly relief of making the numbers line up.

He opened the pen drawer and found the usual company issue, black Pilot rollers in a mug with three snapped pencils. He tested one on scrap paper. The ink came out slightly wetter than the line already on the page.

In the workshop beyond the office wall someone dropped a socket wrench. The metal clatter made him stop with the pen tip in the air. The dispatcher kept talking into the headset, explaining surcharge bands to somebody rich enough to believe the rules were personal insults.

Beni bent closer to the book.

First he changed the destination line. A single clean strike through `home SW1`, one line only, the approved way, and above it in smaller letters:

`White Post Rd donor call, home SW1`

The words crowded the column and made the rest of the line list upward. Donor call. The kind of phrase people in Nina's life used to make one room in East London interchangeable with another, as if charity and culture and private dinners all sat in the same expensive blur.

Then the odometer.

He had thought the number through in the car. Belgravia to Mayfair, then east to the canal, then back west again through traffic. Ten extra miles if the lights were decent, a little more if they were not. Ten miles gave him a round figure and a chance to alter the page without rewriting every digit from scratch.

He put the pen down and used a fingernail first, rubbing at the paper beside the existing `31430.8` as if measuring how much pressure the fibres would take. Then he drew in the additional loop and stem carefully, turning the `3` in the tens place into a `4`, added the extra upright before the final zero, and let the line settle:

`31440.8`

It was good enough at first glance. Too good, maybe. The black sat glossier where it was fresh.

The Tuesday mileage now made sense with the image. The problem moved immediately to Wednesday morning, where the first school run began with the car supposedly leaving at `31430.8`.

He turned the page.

Wednesday was all Ashdown. Bea to school. Nina to a lunch in Knightsbridge. A chemist run for the house. Back to the mews. Four lines. Four openings and closings chained together like linked hands. He had never really noticed how much trust a day required until he sat there breaking it.

He changed the first `31430.8` to `31440.8`.

Then the closing number under the school run, which had to rise by the same ten.

Then the next opening.

By the third alteration the work lost any remaining glamour and became what Noor had called it. Admin. Ink drying under fluorescent light. His shoulder knotting from bending over a ruled page. The lie stopped being a sentence and became arithmetic.

He moved to the route slip file and found Wednesday's carbon. The opening odometer there had to match the book. Carbon paper took pressure differently, and the scratched-over digits showed through like a bruise under skin. He wrote slowly, covering the old number with the new one, then initialled the margin with a forged `LT` because Len Turner was the dispatcher who usually reconciled Ashdown mileage and Beni had seen his initials often enough to copy the lazy cross-stroke.

On Tuesday's route slip he added one more line between gallery and return:

`21:18 White Post Rd donor stop`

He kept the time before the photograph's timestamp. If anyone wanted the innocent explanation Marian had asked for, there it was: an east London stop dressed in donor language, client still in the car, no lover named.

His hand cramped. He flexed it once and looked down at the small crescent of black ink on the side of his little finger. There was no drama in the room, no thunderclap of guilt, nothing except the dry office air and the dispatcher now laughing politely into the headset because the late client had turned conciliatory. That almost made it dirtier. The worst choices he had made in his life had all happened in rooms that would have looked ordinary to anyone else.

He turned one more page to Thursday to make sure the running total still held. It did, barely.
