Friday's fuel receipt was stapled in at the back of the week with a handwritten odometer that would not reconcile if anyone compared too hard, but Mercer almost never compared too hard unless a client threatened to leave. That was what he was buying, really: laziness higher up the chain, the hope that everyone above him would want the smoother version badly enough not to inspect it.

He heard Noor's voice anyway. Don't put it in your handwriting.

Too late.

The dispatcher pushed back his chair in the outer office. Beni shut the route file, slid it under the invoice stack, and blotted the wettest correction with the side of his thumb. When he lifted it there was the slightest drag in the paper, a matte streak over the new `4`. If Len or Marian or Oscar looked under a lamp long enough they would see it. If they pulled the old invoice copies from storage they would see more. If they asked why Tuesday's donor stop had no client note in the house diary, the answer would have to breed again.

He put the pen back in the mug.

Then he signed off the Ashdown week at the bottom of the page with his own initials because the line was there waiting and leaving it blank would have been more suspicious than anything else. `BV`. Small, neat, undeniable.

When he slid the book into the grey drawer, the cloth cover rasped against the metal like sand in a gearbox. He closed the cabinet softly. The dispatcher was still turned half away, one finger in his free ear, explaining that yes, sir, an additional waiting charge had been applied and no, sir, it was not negotiable.

Beni took his coat, the Ashdown keys now hanging light from the peg because their day was officially complete. Outside the office window the depot yard shone black with rain. Valeting had gone home. The buffer whine was gone. A single security lamp held the parked cars in a cheap yellow wash, each bonnet catching water and giving it back. He stood there long enough for the cold from the glass to find his knuckles, then turned back toward the drivers' room instead of the yard.

Until now he had been carrying Nina's secret in disposable forms: a sentence said mildly, a route left vague, cash folded into his wallet, the stitched back seat under him, his tie tightening at his throat in Nina's fist, his body going where she tugged it. Those things could still pretend to be confusion if he needed them to: youth, traffic, a misunderstanding in a rich house.

The book in the drawer was different. It would sit there tomorrow and next week and a month from now with his numbers nudged by hand and his forged initials beside the correction and his own real initials at the foot of the page. If the secret ever opened, it would not look like improvisation anymore. It would look exactly like a record.


# Chapter 10

At 1:23 the Mercer drivers' room smelled of burnt coffee, wet nylon, and the cold coins drivers emptied from cup holders before handing keys back. Beni had one heel up on the leg of the plastic chair, eating the end of a petrol-station sandwich he did not want, when Nina called his private mobile instead of dispatch.

He looked at her name for one ring too long before he answered.

"Where are you?" she said.

"Depot."

"Good. Come to Hackney Wick. The studio on White Post Lane. Now."

Her voice was too even. It had the scraped-flat sound she used with caterers and women on charity boards when she was furious enough to be elegant about it. Behind her he heard music cut off mid-bar and a metal door slamming somewhere farther inside the building.

"Are you all right?"

There was a pause. Then she said, "No. Get the car."

The line went dead.

Beni folded the sandwich back into its paper and dropped it in the bin. Noor's warning from yesterday still sat in him like a stone under the ribs. Rich people's secrets are never worth a poor man's paperwork. He took the Ashdown Mercedes anyway, because saying no to Nina after one in the morning did not feel like a choice he possessed. He wrote nothing on the movement board. The hook clacked empty behind him when he lifted the key.

Rain had been falling for hours without force, the kind that never turned dramatic from indoors and yet got into every seam. By the time he brought the car east past the dark offices at Old Street and the wet arches near Bethnal Green, the windows had furred over at the corners. The wipers made a patient metronome across the glass. London at that hour was not asleep, only badly hidden. Office towers kept whole lit floors for no one. Men in aprons hosed sour foam out of restaurant doorways. A woman in trainers smoked under the awning of a hotel that cost more for one night than Beni paid Laci for two weeks on the floor space in Tottenham. Delivery riders hunched under insulated boxes bright as toys, waiting for people richer than them to keep not cooking.

Hackney Wick arrived in pieces: shuttered breweries, canal water black under security lamps, brick made expensive by being near artists and by being old in the correct way. Jonas's studio sat in a yard behind a metal gate that had failed to latch. Beni nosed the Mercedes in past two white vans and saw Nina before he saw Jonas.

She was standing under the strip light by the entrance, coat hanging open over a dark silk dress, one bare leg wet from the knee down where the rain had blown at her. She had kicked off one shoe and let it dangle useless from two fingers. Her other hand was wrapped in a dish towel that had soaked through at the center to a black red. Jonas stood just inside the doorway in paint-marked work trousers and a charcoal T-shirt, rubbing one foot up the back of his other calf against the bite of the concrete floor. There was gray clay under one thumbnail. He kept twisting the tea towel in his hands even though it was already twisted to rope.

"She cut herself on a glass," he said when Beni got out. "It needs looking at."

Nina turned her head toward him without looking at him. "Do not brief the staff, Jonas."

"For fuck's sake, Nina."

"That isn't even inventive from you."

Beni moved between them before the argument found shape. Up close the towel around Nina's hand smelled of whiskey, iron, and the clean lemon soap people used when they had never had to buy the cheap kind in bulk. Blood had run over the heel of her palm and dried in the fine lines of her wrist. Her mascara had held. Her mouth had not. The inside of her lower lip was rubbed raw from her teeth.

"You should go to hospital," Beni said.

"I absolutely shouldn't go to hospital."

"It might need stitches."

"Then it can remain artistically unfinished."

Jonas shut his eyes for a second. "You see? This is what I mean."

She laughed once. It was a bright little sound, almost girlish, and it made the back of Beni's neck tighten harder than shouting would have. "No, darling. What you mean is what you always mean. You mean I've become inconvenient."

"I mean it's the middle of the night and you've smashed a tumbler because I said I was done."

"You said other things."

"Because we were speaking."

The blood had started fresh under the towel. Beni opened the rear door and said, more firmly than he usually spoke to her, "Sit down, please."

Nina met his eyes then, properly, and for a moment he thought she might slap him or kiss him or laugh in his face. Instead she climbed in with the broken shoe still in her good hand and sat sideways on the leather like someone boarding a train she fully intended to complain about. Jonas took half a step forward.

"Take her somewhere she'll get that cleaned properly."

"I'm not going with you," Nina said, not to Beni but still looking at Jonas. "Let the record show that you have found your boundary at last."

Jonas's mouth tightened. He was handsome in the thin-skinned way of men who had spent enough years being told so that annoyance came to them with a mirror already inside it. "You're drunk and bleeding."

"And you are so brave when a woman is already leaving."

Beni shut the door before Jonas could answer. He did not want the next line, from either of them. He went around to the driver's seat, put the car in drive, and took them out into the rain.

For two streets she said nothing. He could hear only the blower, the tires on the wet road, and the faint dry crackle of the towel when she adjusted her grip on it. In the mirror he saw her lean back, close her eyes, then snap them open again with a small panic crossing her face.

"There's a first-aid kit under your seat," he said.

"Of course there is."

"If you let me stop, I can wrap it."

"Drive first."

"Mrs. Ashdown."

"Don't do that."

He waited.

"Not tonight," she said. "Just Nina, if we're still pretending names matter."

He took the A12 west because at that hour it was the cleanest line out, though clean in London only meant fewer chances to sit at lights beside drunk men tapping on the glass. Rain silvered the barriers. The Olympic Park slid by to their right in dead gray metal and sleeping cranes. On a billboard over the carriageway, a development by the river promised studios from £2,150 pcm in letters big enough to be seen from a speeding car. Beni read the number twice without meaning to. More than half his monthly pay for one room and a view of water cold enough to kill you.

At the lights before Bow Roundabout he pulled the hazard switch on and turned half around.

"Give me your hand."

"We're in traffic."

"We're at red."

She made a face at him like a displeased schoolgirl and then held the injured hand out. The towel peeled away with a wet reluctance. The cut ran diagonally across the base of her palm, not deep enough to see anything white, deep enough to keep opening every time she flexed. Blood welled fresh and fat. Beni took saline from the kit, tore it with his teeth, and washed the grit away while the light changed and the man behind them honked once, offended by a world that had not organized itself around him quickly enough.

Nina sucked air between her teeth. "That stings."

"Good."

She gave him a quick look, almost offended and almost amused. "You've been spending time with Eszter."

His fingers paused for half a second. He was glad the light had gone green and the car behind them had moved off, because the man would have seen him sitting there like an idiot with his body turned toward the back seat, one hand around Nina's wrist as if he had forgotten who was employed by whom.

"Pressure," he said.

He folded gauze thick, set it over the cut, and wrapped her hand with the white bandage from the kit. Her skin was warm from drink and shock. The tendons in her wrist shifted under his thumb. He could smell the whiskey on her breath, the sharper green scent of her perfume gone sour with rain, and under both of those the clean iron smell of blood. His body answered before his mind did. He hated that the answer was so old and so immediate.

"Can you move your fingers?"

She did, slow and obedient for once. "There. A triumph of roadside medicine."

"If it keeps bleeding, we stop."

"If it keeps bleeding, you'll drive faster."

He pulled back into the lane. The city tightened around them as they came through Mile End and Whitechapel and then loosened again into the broad damp shine of roads built for finance men to leave after midnight. When he reached Tower Hill she leaned forward.

"Take the river."

"Where do you want to go?"

"Along it. I don't care. West first."

"Home eventually?"

"Eventually everything. West first."

He brought the Mercedes down onto the Embankment and the river appeared to the left, black and broad and carrying strips of torn light. The city made more sense to him from a driver's seat than it ever did on foot. The lane markings held. The bridges arrived in sequence. Buses breathed at stops and moved on. Cyclists in black sprayed water from their wheels. River-facing flats rose in smooth glass stacks with one kitchen lit here, one blue television there, whole dark elevations bought as investment and left to stare at weather. A sales office near St Katharine Docks glowed with a model apartment no one had cooked in and a sign that said from £3.1 million. Down by Blackfriars a man in a suit slept sitting upright on a bench under a shelter ad for luxury retirement living. London knew how to place one insult beside another and call it a skyline.

Nina had stopped pressing the bandage. That worried him more than the blood.

"What happened?" he asked.

She watched his eyes in the mirror. "You mean what was the line? There was a line. Men like Jonas always believe history occurs because they have spoken. Before that there is only atmosphere."

He said nothing.

"He ended it. Properly, this time. No pauses for dignity, no absurd promise to call when things were less complicated, no little speech about timing. He was almost admirable in it. He's been practicing, I think."

At Temple the road narrowed around roadworks and Beni had to drop speed. A taxi cut across him with no signal. He braked, swore softly in Hungarian, and Nina laughed again, this time with actual amusement.

"There you are."

"What?"

"A personality. I was beginning to think you only had shoulders and hands."

He kept his eyes on the lane. "He told you not to come back?"

"He told me he couldn't keep fitting me around the rest of his life. That I had become all appetite and administration. Imagine. Administration. Like I was a filing system he'd once found erotic."

She leaned her head back. The strip of city light crossing her face showed where rain had marked her foundation in two pale tracks. "He said he wanted less consequence. That was the actual phrase. Less consequence. He is forty pounds of damp emotional laundry and a trust fund, and suddenly he wants less consequence."

"How much had you drunk?"

"Enough for him to use it."

"How much?"

"Whiskey. Then his natural wine, which tasted like a horse had died in a plum."

Beni almost smiled. He did not let it happen where she could hear it.

"Did he hit you?" he asked.

She opened her eyes. "No."

"Did you hit him?"

"No. I'm not sixteen." She shifted, then corrected herself. "I threw the tumbler at the wall. It missed the wall in the sense that walls are not made of glass."

He took them over Waterloo Bridge, turned south, then west again because she had not given him any destination he could complete. The river stayed beside them, sometimes close enough to smell its cold metal, sometimes lost behind hotels, office blocks, and apartment buildings with concierge desks brighter than hospitals. At the Savoy a man in a coat that cost more than the car insurance stood under the awning smoking while a porter angled an umbrella over him so carefully even his cuffs stayed dry.

"He said something worse before I threw it," Nina said.

"What?"

She worried the edge of the bandage with one thumbnail. He could see her trying not to pull it loose. It was the first scared thing she had done all night.

"He said he was tired of being cast in someone else's emergency."

Beni's hands stayed steady on the wheel, but inside him something shifted and found purchase. She had called him from the middle of hers anyway.

"Maybe he meant tonight," he said.

"He meant me."

They passed the London Eye half extinguished, its great circle a ghost over the wet road. Men in orange waterproofs were washing yesterday's filth off the pavement with hoses. The water ran toward the drains in black ribbons. A homeless woman's blankets under the bridge had been wrapped in blue tarpaulin with folded corners almost domestic in their care.

"Do you know what is vulgar about being forty-one?" Nina asked.

He thought of several answers and kept them all.

"Everyone congratulates you on how little damage it shows. As if the achievement were not having been alive. You become this before-and-after comparison with no before visible. Men your own age want peace. Younger men want novelty with references. Your husband wants optics. Everyone is delighted when you are appetizing and self-containing. They become very injured if appetite leaks."

Her voice had lost the hard polish from the phone call. Now it moved in longer lines, spilling and correcting itself, the way expensive wine loosened her without making her sloppy until all at once it did. Beni had heard versions of this register in back seats, outside restaurants, on the way home from dinners where rich women discovered the evening had not repaired whatever the day had done to them. With Nina it was different because she knew exactly what she was performing and still could not stop.

"Oscar likes me best at tables," she said. "Not in private. Certainly not in arguments. At tables I know where my hands go and which story about Bea is harmless and which donor's wife hates whom. Jonas liked me in hotel rooms and in cabs and at his openings, when I made him seem chosen by a woman who understood things. But the moment I wanted an actual hour from him that wasn't arranged around his appetite, I became oppressive. Middle-aged. Embarrassingly available."

"You have a daughter. A house. A husband."

"Thank you, Beni. Do continue the inventory of my prison."

He felt the rebuke and the perversity of the heat that came with it. He knew that tone now, the one that made contempt sound like private attention. Six weeks ago he would have heard only the attention.

"I mean people see those first," he said.

"Yes. Exactly." She leaned forward between the seats until he could smell her hair, damp at the ends. "That is exactly what I mean. They see arrangement. They see frame. They see what can be used to explain why I should be grateful and contained. Jonas used to say I made him feel looked after. Like I was some gracious hotel with opinions."

She settled back again. A few cars ahead, a minicab drifted half into the bus lane and then corrected. The river on their left widened and darkened near Chelsea, beyond it whole terraces stood in white stone patience, each window curtained against a life someone else would have called fortunate.

"Pull over a second," Nina said.

He glanced at her. "Why?"

"I want a cigarette."

"You don't smoke."

"Tonight I very much do."

He found a legal bay on Cheyne Walk where no one was likely to care for ten minutes and stopped under a plane tree dripping old rain. The river wall was slick. Across the water the power station's chimneys stood ghost-pale in the thinning dark. Nina opened her bag one-handed, swore softly, and let it spill into the seat beside her: lipstick, card case, a crushed packet of mints, one small perfume bottle, receipts folded into soft white knots, and finally a cigarette case in black enamel.

"You've had those how long?" Beni asked.

"Since a summer in Antibes when I was nineteen and feral."

"Do you know where Antibes is from Tottenham?" he said before he could stop himself.

That made her laugh properly. She bent over the laugh, forehead briefly against the back of his seat. "There he is again. God, don't become funny now. I've got enough to miss already."

She put the cigarette between her lips and held it there, then realized her injured hand made the lighter awkward. Beni reached automatically into his coat pocket for the cheap black lighter he carried for clients who lied about not smoking. The plastic was warm from his body. For one stupid second he almost put it away again. Then he turned in his seat and lit Nina's cigarette with a flick practiced long before London.

She drew in, the tip flaring orange, and looked at the lighter. "That's not yours by class."

"No."

"Was it a gift?"

He shut it and put it back in his pocket. "Smoke or don't."

She exhaled through the crack in the open window. Rain smell, river smell, cigarette smoke, warm leather, blood. It made the car intimate in a way he did not trust. A red double-decker went by nearly empty, top deck lit like an aquarium for one man asleep against the glass.

"He said I make everything heavier," Nina said.

"Did you?"

"Of course I did. What else am I for?" She smiled without humor. "I'm a mother. I'm a wife in technical dispute. I'm a donor list, a seating chart, a car account, a body that has already been interpreted by other people for twenty years. If I love anyone, it arrives with furniture."

She tapped ash into the empty water bottle from the door pocket because even drunk she would not drop it on the leather. That small neatness made him sadder than the blood had.

"Why'd you call me?" he asked.

"Because you answer."

"You could have called anyone."

"No." She looked out at the river. "That's the whole point. I couldn't."

He waited, because waiting was the work and because some part of him still wanted what might come next if he left enough space for it.

"Oscar would turn this into liability by breakfast," she said. "A doctor would make notes. A friend would make it about whether I'm humiliated enough yet. Jonas had finished being noble for the evening. Bea is a child and also far too observant to be used for this. You..." She tipped ash again, careful as a hostess over a linen cloth. "You're good in disasters."

"I drive."

"You contain."

The word sat between them.

Beni looked out through the steamed windshield at the river wall, the slick pavement, the basement light burning in a house where someone had already started breakfast for someone else. He could feel the line drawing tighter, the one between being seen and being used. It was always there with Nina. Sometimes sex blurred it. Sometimes money did. Tonight nothing was blurred enough.

"If you want to go home, I'll take you home," he said. "If you want a clinic, I'll take you there. If you want me to wait until morning, I'll wait. But don't talk about me like I'm furniture."

For the first time all night she went still.

The cigarette hovered at her mouth and did not move. Then she lowered it and turned toward him fully, one bare foot tucked under her on the seat, the broken shoe hanging from two fingers. Up close she carried both women at once: older than daylight, younger than dinners. He could see both and understood how exhausting that must be.

"You're not furniture," she said quietly. "Furniture is expensive and stupid. You... no, that's not fair. You're a relief."

He did not answer.

"Do you know how rare that is? A man who doesn't immediately start bargaining. You don't ask for declarations or where this is going. You don't make me soothe you because I've disappeared. You don't turn one bad night into an invoice."

The rain ticked in the branches above the car. Somewhere along the embankment a fox barked, sharp and ridiculous and lonely. Beni felt the old reflex rise in him, the one that stepped aside from pain and called the movement practicality. He knew how to let a sentence pass over him. He had done it in two languages for years.

Nina mistook his silence for permission and went on.

"It's your silence, Beni. That's what makes you useful to me. You can sit in the dark and leave a thing alone."

He looked at her in the mirror.

At first he felt only the simple hot sting of it, the way anyone would at being praised for the damage they had spent so long teaching to sit still. Then something colder arrived behind the sting. He saw, with a clarity so clean it almost calmed him, that she did not mean he was discreet or kind or loyal. She meant he was built for absence. She meant he could be put where need went when need had no respectable address. She meant the worst thing about him had been convenient to her.

Outside, beyond the river wall, a houseboat window lit and a man in a vest stood brushing his teeth over a sink the size of a shoebox. Even in that lit shoebox he had more room than Beni felt.

"Drive," Nina said, softer now. "Please."

He put the car back in gear.

He crossed Albert Bridge, its lights trembling in the wet, and stayed on the south side after that, taking the long eastbound pull because he was not ready to point the car toward Belgravia while her words were still warm in the air. The city thinned toward the hour when night staff gave way to morning staff. Street cleaners replaced drunks. Bakery vans replaced black cabs outside clubs. Newspaper bundles swelled under plastic outside stations, waiting for hands numb with cold.

Nina smoked half the cigarette and then could not stand it, crushing it carefully into the water bottle and leaving even her disgust in a neat residue.

"Jonas said he's tired of hiding me," she said after Westminster, looking past him to the dark river glass. "Tired of being hidden by me. He said sneaking me in through the yard made him feel cheap."

Beni kept both hands on the wheel. "Hold the bandage higher."

She talked over him. "Oscar hasn't touched me in months unless there's a photographer about. Tonight he moved my hair off my necklace because the flash was catching on it. That was the most intimate thing anybody did to me all week."

"Drink some water."

"I looked at myself in Jonas's bathroom and thought, there she is. Forty-one, dress half open, begging in somebody else's mirror."

His grip tightened on the wheel until the leather seam bit his palm.

"I could've cut deeper," she said. Not dramatic now. Just flat.

The Mercedes drifted an inch toward the center line before he corrected it. He took his foot off the accelerator and dropped them to forty.

"Don't say that again."

She turned her head. "Why? Because then you have to hear it?"

"Because you're drunk and bleeding and I'm not dragging your body out of this car as well. Hold the bandage up. Now."

For a second she only stared. Then she obeyed, lifting the white pad above her lap with resentful care. At the next red light he took the water bottle from the cup holder, twisted the cap loose one-handed, and passed it back without looking at her.

"Drink," he said.

After that he did not take the car above forty.

By the time they came through Pimlico the rain had thinned to mist. Not light yet, only a little less black in the cloud, but the city felt it. Early buses had begun to gather bodies on the lower deck. A woman in running gear waited at a crossing with the defeated face of someone who had promised herself discipline before coffee. A row of townhouses stood wrapped in scaffolding printed with a fake image of the facades beneath, wealth covering its repairs with a photograph of itself.

Nina had gone quiet enough that he checked the mirror twice to make sure quiet was all it was. She was awake, watching the river through half-lidded eyes. The fury had drained from her face. What remained was worse. Not sorrow exactly. Exposure.

"Take me home," she said at last.

He turned inland.

Belgravia before first light was indecent to him, a district that had its rubbish removed before daylight could accuse it of being inhabited. The square garden railings shone black with rain. Window boxes hung trimmed even in November. A florist's van was already unloading pale roses into a side entrance that smelled of bleach and money. When he pulled into the mews behind the Ashdown house, the paving stones reflected the garage lamps in one long expensive ribbon.

He got out first and came around to open her door. She sat a moment without moving, gathering herself with the discipline of someone who had built a whole adulthood out of entering rooms correctly. The bandage on her hand had spotted through at the center, a rust-dark bloom no wider than a coin.

"You'll need that looked at in the morning," he said.

"Yes."

"Actually looked at."

"You're becoming very authoritarian for nineteen."

"I'm serious."

"I know."

She put her good hand on the edge of the door and began to stand. Then she stopped and looked up at him from the seat, her hair roughened by the night's damp, one foot still bare on the black rubber mat. In that moment she came down to ordinary human scale, and none of the danger went out of her.

"If Oscar asks, I broke a glass taking off my earrings," she said. "You picked me up because I didn't want the house calling an ambulance."

Beni held the door and said nothing.

Her mouth changed. Not quite a smile. Not quite defeat.

She stepped out, balancing the broken shoe by its strap, and passed close enough that the fabric of her coat brushed his wrist. The contact lit his skin stupidly, treacherously. She felt it or guessed it; he could not tell. She touched the knot of his tie once with two fingers, straightening nothing.

"Sleep a little," she said. "You look wrecked."

"Go inside, Nina."

Then she went through the side door and vanished into the house that always seemed to swallow evidence by breakfast.

Beni stood in the mews until the automatic light timed out and the garage front went dim. Rain tapped somewhere in the guttering. A plane crossed low and unseen above the city. He got back in the driver's seat because there was nowhere else for him to put what the night had become.

The car smelled of cigarette smoke now, and blood under perfume, and the mechanical warmth of expensive leather being asked to hold too much human mess without showing it. On the rear seat a mint had rolled from Nina's bag and come to rest in the crease by the armrest. He picked it up, looked at the clean silver wrapper, and set it in the cup holder instead of throwing it away.

His private mobile showed 2:24. In a few hours the house would wake. Someone would bring up coffee. Someone would lay out Bea's breakfast. Someone would remove the towel from whatever sink Nina had dropped it in. If she chose, she could make the night into nothing more than a difficult mood and a small domestic accident. Jonas would sleep. Oscar would read numbers. The city would begin charging for itself in daylight rates.

The Mercer phone buzzed in the console pocket: 05:00 sign-in. Terminal 5 after. He looked at the screen until it dimmed. Not enough time to go home, sleep, and come back clean. Enough time to get across town, sign the sheet, drag a clean shirt off the rail, and make his face into something a stranger could trust.

Beni started the engine and keyed the heater higher.

He put the car in reverse, backed carefully out into the wet mews, and drove toward Mercer, where the coffee would be burnt, the fluorescent office too bright, and the route slip already waiting for a man who did not care what had happened before dawn as long as the car was clean and the driver kept quiet.


# Chapter 11

Airport pickups were good until the client got in. For twenty or thirty minutes the car was only a car: leather gone a little cold overnight, heater low, route slip clipped to the vent, the white name board on the passenger seat waiting to become a person. Beni liked that part. Nobody asked for a charger, nobody cried, and nobody decided his silence meant they could pour whatever they liked into it.

He was due at Terminal 5 for a man called Arvind Mehra off the first New York flight. He had already done the burnt coffee, the fluorescent office, the clean shirt off the rail. The route slip had the booking reference, the flight number, the note that the client preferred still water and no radio. Beni had not slept enough to feel hungry. Nina had kept him in the car until after two, talking herself hoarse and then going quiet in the dark like she had spent even that. He had signed back into Mercer at five. Coffee sat under his ribs, bitter and flimsy. The shirt on his back had come from the depot rail and still smelled faintly of starch and somebody else's aftershave.

The M4 was wet but not bad. The rain had thinned to mist. Spray lifted from tyres and hung low over the tarmac so that every tail light dragged a red seam through it. Beni kept to the left lane where he could, wipers on intermittent, both hands at ten and two not because Mercer trained that into him but because it kept his shoulders quiet. The satnav spoke once and he muted it. He knew this road: the slow bend by the hotels, the stretch where the barriers crowded close and the lane felt narrower than it was, the overhead signs with the airport glyphs shining green in the wet.

He passed a fuel tanker, then a white van with two ladders tied to the roof. Up ahead, a curtain-sided lorry in the middle lane ran almost level with him for half a minute, its trailer dirty enough to hold finger marks, the company logo half hidden under brown road spray. A torn strip of red tape flapped from the rear hinge and hit the metal in a fast, stupid rhythm. Beni noticed it because he always noticed the stupid small things: tape, cracked indicators, bad retreads, the way some men sat too near the wheel because they were scared of distance.

The lorry's left indicator never came on.

It just came.

It did not happen fast. That was the worst part. It came with the dull confidence of something too large to think it needed permission. The white wall of the trailer moved a foot toward him, then another, the rivet line drifting across his windscreen. Beni hit the horn. The sound was swallowed at once by insulation and rain. He checked his right mirror and saw only the lorry's flank. To his left there was only barrier, hard shoulder barely a strip, and tarmac slick with standing water. The trailer kept coming.

He braked just enough not to lose the back end. The Mercedes shuddered under him, expensive and obedient and nearly useless. The wheel gave a quick dry chatter through his palms as the front tyre touched the ribbed line. He eased left, not yanked, because a yank spun cars and a yank killed people. The trailer was close enough for him to see black grease around a bolt head. Water sheeted off its side onto his windscreen in a hard slap. The heater vent hissed. His chest locked around the sound.

He saw white, a chemical white, and the burnt-plastic stink arrived before the pain. Klara laughed with her lipstick on the filter because she never kept it off the cigarette for long. The road in Hungary opened the wrong way under the headlights. The line in the middle of it vanished by rain and then he was on the wrong side, no, the side was wrong because he had put them there. He saw her bare knee against the gear stick and the headlights where they should not have been. He felt the wheel going light and then too heavy. He heard her say his name once, not frightened yet, more annoyed than frightened, as if he had missed a turn.

The Mercedes found the hard shoulder and ran along it with all four wheels humming over grit. Beni got the hazards on without seeing his own hand move. He brought the car to a stop under a gantry that said Terminals 2, 3, 4 as if those places had anything to do with him. Traffic tore past on his right. Each lorry shoved a pulse of air against the bodywork. The whole car twitched with it.

He sat very still.

The indicator for the hazards clicked. The heater kept breathing. There had been no impact. The bonnet was clean in front of him. The windscreen held only rain and the red wash of other people's brake lights. But the smell in the car had changed. It had not changed. He knew it had not. The car still smelled of leather, damp wool from his coat on the back hook, coffee in the cup holder, and the lemon cleaning product from the depot valeter. Through it all came the hot dusty tang of deployed airbags from another country and two years ago, so clean and false he almost respected it.

His right foot would not come off the brake.

He tried. The muscle in his thigh worked. The shoe stayed planted as if the pedal had risen through the sole and into bone. He looked down and the foot was his, the trouser leg his, black Mercer cloth with the hem starting to shine. None of it helped. He had the violent certainty that if he lifted his foot the car would already be turning, already spinning, already meeting the white burst that split a life into before and after.

Beni took his hands from the wheel by force. His left palm had left a damp print in the leather. He reached into his coat pocket and closed his fingers around the silver lighter he carried without ever using. The metal was cold and blunt-edged, scarred at one corner. He had no idea why that made anything worse, but it did.

His phone was vibrating in the cup holder. Dispatch was calling. He let it rattle twice before he could pick it up.

"Yeah," he said, and his voice came out from too far away.

The dispatcher wanted the booking status. Beni could not remember the client's name for a second, which frightened him more than the near miss had. Then it came back off the route slip: Mehra, Terminal 5, arriving. He said there had been a lane incident on the M4, no contact, car was fine. He did not say that he was not. The dispatcher asked if he could continue.

Out on the road, every set of headlights seemed to come toward him in the wrong lane before the angle corrected itself. The shoulder under the open sky was too narrow. The barrier post outside his window had a black number on it. He read it three times and kept forgetting what number was after the slash.

"Noor's nearest," the dispatcher said. "Ten minutes. Sit tight."

"I'm all right," Beni said, because that was what he said when there was no useful answer.

"Didn't ask," the dispatcher said, not unkindly, already elsewhere. "Put your hazards on if they aren't on."

He laughed once, a horrible dry sound, and cut the call.

He got the door open and the cold hit him with motorway filth in it, wet diesel, brake heat, the metallic taste of winter rain. His knees held. That annoyed him. If they had folded there would at least have been a shape to the problem. He walked to the front wing and put one hand on it. The paint was beaded with mist. His stomach came up all at once and he bent over the hard shoulder, coughing coffee and acid onto the black grit. The traffic noise did not alter for him. Nothing on the road ever did.

When he wiped his mouth with the back of his hand, he saw that the fingers were shaking so badly he could not line them up with each other.

He sat on the barrier because standing made the tarmac tilt. The guardrail was wet through the seat of his trousers within seconds. Cars passed so near he could feel the air rake his coat against his spine. He kept the lighter in his fist. It warmed there and stayed a piece of old weather.

Noor's car arrived eight minutes later, maybe nine. Beni saw the black Mercedes in the mirror first, Mercer plate bracket, low dipped lights, then the clean deliberate move into the shoulder behind him. Noor did not hurry in the panicked way some men did around roadside trouble. He got out with the driver door half shielding him from the lane, shut it with his hip, looked once up the traffic stream and once back, then came forward. His suit was the same depot black as Beni's, only older in the cloth, the cuffs polished shiny where they rubbed the wheel all day. He had a dented green thermos in one hand because he said motorway coffee tasted of pennies and refused to pay for it anymore.

"You hit?" he said.

Beni shook his head.

"Good. Means I can be irritated, not busy."

That was Noor's one joke. After it he went practical at once. He crouched to Beni's eye level, not touching him yet. "Look at me."

Beni did.

"Head on the wheel?"

"No."

"Chest?"

"No."

"You blacked out?"

"No."

Noor took that in and looked past him to the car, the tyres, the straight line of the bumper, then back at Beni's hands around the lighter. "All right," he said. "Stand up."

"I can drive."

"No, you can't."

"The car's fine."

"I'm thrilled for the car." Noor straightened. "Stand up anyway."

Beni did because refusing would have required more balance than he had. Noor took the thermos cap off and poured tea into it, dark and sweet-smelling with cardamom. His mother sent him to work with it the way other mothers sent children with scarves, whether they wanted them or not. He held the cap out.

"Drink."

Beni took it. The tea burned his mouth because he swallowed too fast. It tasted of milk cooked a little too long and sugar not quite dissolved. It was so ordinary he nearly cried from it, which was intolerable, so he did not.

Noor watched him swallow. "Now tell me true. If I put you back in that seat this second, what happens?"

The open driver door waited there, wet leather darkened by rain, the wheel bare and ready for his hands. In his head the white trailer still moved toward him with patient certainty. Under that, older and worse, another road opened under rain, another steering wheel, Klara's voice in his ear and the stupid fond way she said his name when she wanted something.

"My hands don't work right," he said.

"Fine. Better answer."

Noor took the route slip from the vent, glanced at it. "Mehra, T5. Flight landed ten early, because of course it did." He looked back at Beni. "Can you sit in the passenger seat without vomiting on the dash?"

"Probably."

"Brilliant. That's all I need from you for now."

He moved around Beni's car with quick exact motions, checking for damage, kicking lightly at a front tyre, wiping road spray from the side mirror with the side of his hand. Then he got into the driver's seat as if it were the simplest thing in the world to take over another man's wheel. Beni stayed where he was.

Noor leaned across and pushed the passenger door open. "Come on, then. Unless you want Highways to stop and ask why you're sitting on the barrier looking haunted."

Beni got in. The interior was still warm from his body and that felt obscene. He held the thermos cap empty in one hand and the lighter in the other until Noor looked down at both and said, "Choose a relic." Beni put the lighter back in his pocket and passed over the cap.

Noor settled the seat in three movements, adjusted the mirrors though Beni had already had them right, and pulled out with a cleaner line than Beni had managed even on his best mornings. Traffic folded around them. Noor drove as if the road had personally offended him years ago and they had since reached a grudging understanding.

Beni kept seeing the lorry come at them in the wing mirror though it was long gone. He sat too straight. His own knees looked borrowed. The name board for Mehra slid a little on the passenger floor mat with each bend and tapped the plastic trim like a metronome.

"Talk to dispatch?" Noor said.

"I did."

"What'd you tell them?"

"Lane incident. No damage."

"Good." Noor checked the mirror, indicator on, off. "They know I'm on it now. I told them you'd had a shock and I was taking the pickup."

Beni turned his head. "Why?"

"Because it's true."

"They'll mark it."

"Everything gets marked. That isn't the interesting part."

They ran the last stretch toward Heathrow in a silence that was not empty. Noor kept one hand loose on the wheel and the other near the indicator stem. Beni tried to make himself useful by watching the road signs and the lane arrows, but each overhead gantry broke into two images for a second before it settled. He gave up and watched Noor's cuff move over the wheel, the shiny cloth catching the grey light each time the car passed under a lamp.

At Terminal 5 short stay, Noor parked on level three between a Volvo estate with two child seats and a black Range Rover that had not seen real dirt in its life. The concrete smelled of damp tyres, cleaning fluid, and the fry oil from somewhere below. Every few seconds a suitcase rattled over the painted lines and someone swore in three languages at once. Planes climbed overhead so low the ceiling seemed to feel it.

Noor cut the engine but left the key in. "You need air or you need not-air?"

"I don't know."

"Fine. Stay confused in here, then."

He went to arrivals with the white name board, shoulders broad under the thin work coat, thermos tucked under one arm like it belonged in every part of his life. Beni watched him until the automatic doors swallowed him.

The stillness after driving was worse for a minute. The concrete pillar outside held the number C38, and Beni fixed on that until it stopped breaking apart. Around him, Heathrow morning happened without asking him to join it. A toddler wailed. Wheels hit an expansion strip over and over. Somewhere nearby a man laughed the kind of laugh rich men learned in clubs, all mouth and no fatigue. Beni put both palms flat on his thighs and felt the damp cloth cooling there.

When he shut his eyes, he was back in Klara's car so hard it hurt.

It had not been a smart car or a lover's car or anything built for beautiful trouble. It had been a silver Opel Astra with biscuit crumbs in the back from her daughter and a cracked saint medal glued to the dash because her mother thought every vehicle needed watching over. The heating only worked on the highest setting, so the windscreen always smelled faintly of hot dust. Klara kept lipstick in the side pocket, cheap red that bled into the little lines around her mouth by evening, and a silver lighter in the cup holder because she was forever losing the disposable ones.

She had been thirty-four, maybe thirty-five. To Beni at seventeen that was nearly another species. She had a husband who did not look at her when he spoke and a girl old enough to leave hair clips under the front seat and ask why Mummy was wearing perfume on a Tuesday. Klara laughed too loudly in small shops. She wore blouses that were one step better than the town required and boots with thin heels that sank into mud outside the school gate. She was never only one thing at a time; she could be maternal and mean, warm and showing off, greedy for touch in the way hungry people were greedy for bread, without apology.

She had liked that Beni listened. At first that was all. He carried crates for a neighbour one Saturday, then he was at her kitchen table because she had given him coffee while the rain passed, then she was touching his wrist to make a joke last longer than the joke needed. By winter she was collecting him in the Astra on roads nobody used after dark. The booster seat stayed in the back under his jacket. Once one of the child's glitter hair clips stuck in the sole of his shoe and came home with him. He kept that too for three weeks before throwing it away.

The day of the crash had been April and filthy with rain, the kind that turned every verge in eastern Hungary to black soup. Klara had told him the children were with her mother. She had driven them to a day-rate room above a bowling bar on the edge of Debrecen because, she said, nobody there cared who was ruining themselves. The room smelled of bleach, old smoke, and the fryer downstairs. She had laughed at the bedspread and then fucked him with the laugh still in her throat, one hand over his mouth because the wall was thin and because that made it hotter for her. Afterward she sat on the sink with her slip around her waist, smoking out the little vent window, and told him she couldn't leave her husband before summer because schools did not break for desire.

Beni had been angry before they even got back to the car. Nothing righteous lived in it. The feeling was worse than that, a young humiliated anger of the kind that still needed the person it hated. She kept touching him while she talked, as if touch settled arguments. She had taken two swallows from a bottle of pear palinka she kept in her bag, said her eyes were tired, and dropped the keys into his hand. He had loved that weight too much. He had felt chosen. He had felt trusted. He had got behind the wheel like it meant anything good.

It was a two-lane road outside town, nothing dramatic, fields flat on both sides, the kind of road you could drive half asleep and still talk yourself into thinking you were alive. Rain hit so hard the wipers lost and won and lost again. Klara had kicked off one heel and tucked one leg under herself on the seat. Her belt was on at first. Then she unhooked it because it cut across her blouse and because she wanted to turn toward him properly. He told her to put it back. She laughed and said he sounded older in a car.

They were fighting about London.

She did not mean real London. She meant the version of it she had started saying when she wanted to make the affair feel bigger than hotel sheets and farm roads. She had a cousin in Luton she had not seen in six years. She said they could go there. She said people left every day and the world did not close behind them. She said he was wasted in that town, in that body, in that frightened polite way he had of accepting crumbs like they were wages.

He drove faster because he wanted the fight over and because speed made him feel less like a boy she could put down and pick up again. The road markings were drowned half the time. Water sat on the tarmac in black plates. She kept talking. She kept leaning toward him, her hand on his thigh, then on the wheel for one stupid teasing second, then off again. He told her not to do that. She laughed at the wrong moment, exactly as a truck came through the rain ahead, its headlights high and yellow and impossibly close.

The wrong lane was his.

That was the fact. It stayed that way no matter how often memory tried to drag it loose from him.

He had drifted over while looking at her. He had not looked long, but he had looked long enough.

He jerked the wheel left. The rear of the Astra let go at once on the wet paint. The car slewed, caught, slewed harder. Klara's cigarette flew from her hand and burned a black dot into the seat before the world hit white. The passenger-side airbag burst with a sound like a fist through cloth. Something struck Beni above the brow so hard he saw blue. The car spun off the road and took the ditch sideways with the passenger side first. The tree was thin, not old, almost an insult, but it punched through the door skin as if the car were cardboard.

When the motion stopped, the wipers were still going.

That was the first thing he understood. The wipers kept going, smear, slap, smear. The engine ticked like it wanted another try. The chemical stench of the airbags was thick enough to chew. His own belt had him pinned. His left hand was under him and numb. Klara was making a sound he had never heard from a person before, not a scream, not even pain at first, more like her body had forgotten where air was supposed to go.

Blood had run from her nose into the lipstick and turned the whole lower half of her face dark and sloppy. The airbag had gone off, but the tree had taken the door and half the space where her ribs should have been. She kept trying to lift her right hand and could not. He said her name. He thinks he said it. He must have. Her eyes found him once with full furious clarity, as if this were still a thing she could blame on him properly. Then they slipped.

He had to kick his own door twice before it opened.

Rain got in at once, cold and clean and useless. He leaned back over her from outside because the passenger side was crushed into the ditch. He told her he was there. He told her the ambulance was coming when he had not called it yet. He called with fingers that kept missing the screen. There was mud on his tongue. A lorry had stopped. Somebody shouted. Everything happened and did not happen. By the time they cut the passenger side wider, she had pissed herself and gone heavy in the face. One of the paramedics looked at him the way people look at boys who have done exactly what everyone warned them about.

In the parking gravel behind the short-stay bay, someone knocked on Noor's window and Beni flinched so hard his knee hit the glove box.

Noor was back. He opened the driver's door and slid in with the smell of rain and airport air on him. "Flight's delayed at the gate. Ten minutes." He set the name board down and looked once at Beni's face. "All right. Now tell me what actually happened on the motorway."

Beni did not answer.

Noor leaned back, not soft, not cruel. "Don't give me this 'I'm all right' shit again. I watched you try to open the passenger door with the thermos cap in your hand because you forgot what hand a door uses. You're not embarrassed enough to lie well."

Beni stared at the windscreen. On the concrete pillar opposite, a child had stuck half a dinosaur sticker under the parking instructions. Only the green tail remained. It shook slightly each time a car passed.

"I crashed once," he said.

"Yeah. I got that far by myself."

"In Hungary."

"Who was in the car?"

Beni swallowed. "A woman."

Noor waited.

"Klara Fenyves," Beni said. Saying the surname in London felt indecent, too whole. "She was married."

Noor let out a breath through his nose. "And?"

"And I was seventeen."

That turned Noor's head, not dramatically, just enough. "Right."

"It was... it wasn't one time." Beni rubbed the scarred corner of the lighter through his pocket lining. "Months."

"She die there or later?"

"There. Maybe on the road. Maybe before the ambulance. I don't know exactly."

He heard himself keep going because stopping made Noor look about to ask the exact right question.

"We'd been in Debrecen. Cheap room over a bowling bar. She'd had palinka after and said her eyes were tired, so I drove the Astra back. It was pissing rain. She took her belt off. We were fighting. She kept turning toward me, talking about London like it was already paid for."

Noor said nothing.

"I looked at her instead of the road. I crossed the line. A truck came up through the rain. I yanked us back, lost the rear, and put her side into a tree."

Noor took that in the same way he took bad traffic reports: quickly, with no appetite for theatre. "Your fault?"

He turned to Noor then. "Yes."

"Drink?"

"Not me."

"Police?"

"No charge. Wet road. Young driver. Her not wearing the belt helped explain it to them." The last part came out uglier than he meant. As if explanation and help belonged in the same sentence as a dead woman.

Noor's jaw moved once. "And every time a lorry comes sideways, you go back there."

"Not every time."

"Today."

"Today."

Noor reached for the thermos, found it empty, and set it down again. "Anyone here know?"

"No."

"Family?"

"At home, yes. People knew. Afterward everyone knew soon enough. Only before."

"At Mercer?"

"No."

"Girl in Belgravia?"

Beni's head came up too fast.

Noor snorted, not amused. "Relax. I didn't ask which one. I asked whether anyone in this city knows what happens to you behind the wheel."

"No."

For the first time Noor looked genuinely angry, though he kept his voice level. "That's stupid, mate."

Beni had no defense that did not sound like a child protecting a hiding place. It was money, work, shame, and the fact that if he named it, it became something other men could decide against him. He looked back at the green half-sticker on the pillar.

"I know," he said.

"Do you?"

"Yes."

Noor turned in the seat to face him more fully. "No, hear me properly. You can't have a thing like that living in your hands and then just hope it'll be polite forever. This isn't some office panic where you shut the toilet door and wait it out. You're doing seventy in a two-ton car for money."

Beni felt the words land because Noor had stripped them of pity. That helped. Pity would have made him lie. This made him listen.

"I didn't think..." he started, then stopped because the sentence was too stupid to finish.

"Exactly," Noor said. "You didn't think because thinking would've meant telling someone and maybe losing work. So you did what everybody does when they're skint and ashamed. You prayed the problem would stay private."

Beni laughed once without humour. "You're making it sound very ordinary."

"It's ordinary. That's why it kills people."

They sat with that. A plane roared low enough overhead to tremble the mirror glass. Somewhere down the row, a boot slammed and a woman said into her phone that she had told him twice already and was not repeating herself.

Beni took the lighter from his pocket and turned it in his palm. The silver had gone dull except where his thumb had polished one side over time. Noor looked at it.

"That's hers?"

Beni nodded.

"You smoke now?"

"No."

"Then why carry it?"

Beni watched the blunt scar at one corner catch the car park light. "I took it from the cup holder after."

Noor waited again.

"They were cutting the door," Beni said. "There was glass everywhere. Mud. Her bag had split open. Lipstick, tissues, a little packet of raisins for the child, all in the water. The lighter was there by the handbrake. I took it." He shrugged and hated the shrug. "I didn't want to leave with nothing. Which is disgusting."

"It's not disgusting."

"It is."

"No. It's strange. That's different." Noor rubbed his thumb over the seam in the steering wheel. "People do odd things when the world breaks in front of them. My uncle kept the watch from his brother's body for twenty years and never wore it once. Thought if he polished it enough he'd eventually understand why it was still ticking when the man wasn't. Humans are trash at grief. That's not news."

Beni looked down at the lighter. "I wasn't grieving properly."

"Who gives a shit about properly?"

"Her daughter was at the funeral."

That shut Noor up for a second, not because he had run out of words but because he knew some facts were heavy enough to deserve the pause. "All right," he said finally. "That's bad."

"She had the same hair clips." Beni heard how crazed it sounded and kept going because stopping now would have been cowardice. "The ones from under the seat. Pink plastic stars. I saw them in the girl's hair and thought, I know those. Like that was the worst thing. Not the coffin. The stupid hair clips."

Noor stared through the windscreen. "Brains are cheap little bastards."

Beni smiled without wanting to. It vanished at once.

"Why tell me now?" Noor said. "Why not two months ago, or never?"

The concrete, the painted arrows, the people dragging hand luggage toward money and return held him for a second. "Because the lorry moved and I wasn't here anymore."

"Fair."

"And because if I'd been with a client..." He stopped.

"You weren't."

"No."

"But you could be next time."

Beni nodded.

Noor drummed his fingers once on the wheel, three quick taps, deciding something. "Here's what happens. I finish this job. You sit there and don't pretend you're helping more than you're helping. After that I take you to the depot, I tell them the truth they need and not the truth they don't. Near miss, driver shaken, shift covered. Tomorrow you're off. If they complain, they complain to me."

"I can't lose the day."

"You already lost it."

"Noor."

"No." Noor's voice sharpened, not loud. "Don't start counting pennies at me while you're still smelling ghosts. You get one day. Maybe that's not medicine, but it's better than putting you back on the M25 and seeing which memory wins."

Beni closed his hand around the lighter again. "I never told anyone here."

"You just did."

The radio crackled with dispatch asking status on Mehra. Noor picked it up before Beni could move.

"Client delayed at gate," Noor said. "We're in position."

Beni heard the word we're and looked away.

When Mehra finally came through arrivals, he was not the clean hard businessman Beni had imagined from the booking notes. He was a tired man in a navy quilted jacket with one shoelace untied, dragging a carry-on by the side handle because the telescopic grip had jammed. A paper conference wristband still circled his right wrist, and under his other arm he carried a duty-free Toblerone big enough to make a child believe in him for at least an hour. Noor took the bag before the man could apologise for its wheel and got him settled in the back with his water and the heater turned one notch up.

Mehra smelled of plane air, wool, and stale deodorant. He asked only once if traffic was all right into town, then looked at his phone and shut his eyes before they reached the spirals out of the terminal. Noor drove. Beni sat in the passenger seat with the empty name board at his feet and watched London rebuild itself lane by lane out of the wet morning.

Nobody spoke for most of the return.

That suited the client. It suited Noor too. His kindness was not the kind that needed displaying every five minutes to prove it existed. Once, by Hammersmith, he reached over without looking and nudged the thermos against Beni's knee. There was another half cup in it somehow, sweet and lukewarm now. Beni drank it anyway.

At Pembridge Villas, Mehra woke in the back with the blind daze of a man who had slept in four-minute bursts across an ocean. He thanked Noor, then thanked Beni too, because he had no reason not to believe both men had done the same work. He wrestled the dead-wheeled carry-on onto the pavement, tucked the giant Toblerone under his chin, and went into a white stucco house without seeing either of them clearly.

Noor waited until the door shut. Then he pulled away.

"See?" he said.

"What?"

"World didn't end because somebody else drove."

Beni looked out at the rain-dark railings and the clipped little trees in square grates. "Not for you."

"Don't be precious."

That made Beni laugh properly, once. It hurt the back of his throat and felt better than not laughing.

By the time they reached the depot, the sky had lifted into that flat London grey that never became day so much as a brighter kind of damp. Vans idled by the shutters. Somebody had dropped a sausage roll wrapper in the yard and it was plastered to the concrete by rain. Noor parked, cut the engine, and turned to Beni.

"Tomorrow," he said. "Off."

"All right."

"If you get a flash like that again before then, you don't get heroic. You pull over and you call. Me, dispatch, whoever. I don't care if it's embarrassing."

"All right."

"And whatever rich-person nonsense you've got yourself tangled in, sort it before it sorts you."

Beni stared at him.

Noor lifted one shoulder. "You don't nearly leave the road over an old dead woman and tell me there's nothing current feeding it. I wasn't born yesterday."

Beni should have denied it. Denial was the habit that paid him. But the part of himself that had once relied on silence had already cracked open in the short-stay car park under the planes. Lying now would not have closed it again. It would only have made him smaller inside it.

"I know," he said.

Noor nodded as if that was enough for today. There was no forgiveness in it, no advice, no plan. Just a line put down where a line belonged. He picked up the thermos, opened his door, and paused. "And Beni."

"Yeah?"

"Bin that shirt. You smell like old coffee and fear."

He got out before Beni could answer.

Beni stayed in the passenger seat another half minute with the yard beyond the windscreen and the empty wheel to his right. The silver lighter sat in his palm. For two years it had been a private object with a private charge, something he could carry like a rotten tooth under the tongue, touching it alone in other people's houses, at red lights, in bed after Nina, in the Mercer locker room while everyone else talked about routes and Arsenal and fuel receipts. Now Noor knew what it was, whose it had been, and why a lorry moving one lane too far could take the blood from Beni's hands in a second.

Nothing practical had improved. Laci still wanted money. Nina still existed. The altered logbook still sat where he had hidden it. Sunday off meant Sunday unpaid. But the story was no longer sealed under his skin. Another man could name it now. Another man had looked at the ugliest fact and not stepped back.

Beni closed the lighter, though it had never been open, and put it in his pocket. Then he got out of the car and went inside to sign off a day he had not finished and had somehow still survived.


# Chapter 12

Sunday felt wrong because yesterday had stopped in the middle and daylight had got to him first.

He woke after nine with the damp patch above the window already visible and the buses on the main road shaking the glass in its frame. His suit trousers were over the chair where he had dropped them, still holding the crease from yesterday, and his phone had two messages from Mercer. The first had come from dispatch just after he got home: `No shift Sunday. Rest. Report Monday 10:00.` The second was from Noor an hour later: `Take the day, brother. If you fall asleep in the car again I'm not covering for your ugly face.`

Beni read Noor's message twice. The joke was cheap and kind, which made it worse somehow. He had spent most of the night not sleeping, lying on top of the blanket in his T-shirt and listening for the lorry again. Every time a truck changed gear on the road outside the flat his body had prepared for impact before his mind caught up.

He washed at the little sink in the corner of the front room, keeping the tap low so he would not wake Laci if Laci was still in the back room. The water came out metallic and cold. He shaved badly, nicked the point of his jaw, and stared at the pale shape of himself in the mirror screwed to the wall above the sink. He was nineteen, with a scar at the left brow and eyes older than he liked in the morning. He had slept in daylight before, plenty of times after late shifts, but this felt different. Rest had been ordered. Rest was what happened when other men had decided you were not safe with a car.

Eszter texted at half past ten.

`You alive? I'm at Finsbury Park in twenty. If you make me wait, I'll buy the good sausage without you.`

He looked at the words long enough that the screen dimmed. Then he put on his cleanest jumper, the dark grey one without fraying at the cuffs, counted the cash in his wallet by habit, and left before the room could start smelling properly of him again.

Rain had stopped but left the city wet. The pavement outside the flat shone with oil and old wrappers flattened into the cracks. On the bus south he sat upstairs and watched the roofs slide past under a low white sky, aerials black against the weather. Church steeples, launderettes, and fried-chicken places went by with their shutters half up, and men smoked under plastic awnings because nowhere indoors belonged to them. His body loosened by inches as the route became familiar. The bus passed Seven Sisters, then Manor House, then Finsbury Park. There was no steering wheel in his hands and no client breathing at him from the back.

Eszter was waiting outside the station under the eaves of a closed phone shop, one canvas bag folded into her coat pocket and the other looped over her wrist already, as if she did not trust the day not to require carrying. Her hair was down for once, flattened at the crown by drizzle and then breaking loose at the ends. She had on a dark green coat with one button hanging by a thread and boots that needed new soles. When she saw him she looked him up and down and snorted.

"You dressed up for paprika?"

"It's one jumper."

"It's the funeral jumper."

"I don't own a funeral jumper."

"You do now." She stepped in and kissed him fast on the mouth, quick enough that anyone watching could miss it. "You look tired."

"I'm tired."

"Good. Then you won't talk too much."

She linked her arm through his and turned them toward the grocer before he could answer. That was one of the things he liked most about her and one of the things that scared him: she moved as if the next fact was waiting and there was no point pretending otherwise.

The Hungarian shop sat halfway down the street between a betting place and an off-licence, its window full of tins with sun-faded labels and packets of wafer biscuits arranged in a pyramid that had started to lean. The bell over the door gave a tired metal knock when they went in. Warm air hit him first, then coffee, smoked pork, vinegar from the pickle barrels at the back, damp wool steaming off coats. People were talking in Hungarian on both sides of the aisle, not loudly, just with the ordinary speed he missed until he heard it. A man in a quilted bodywarmer was arguing about the price of goose fat. Two women by the freezer chest were discussing whose nephew had gone to Birmingham and come back with teeth too white to trust.

Eszter's shoulders dropped a little as soon as they crossed the threshold. She was still watchful, but in a different key. She was not staff-space watchful now, and not Ashdown-house watchful either. She picked up a basket and started loading it with the efficiency of someone who had built meals out of worse options than these.

"Not that one," she said when he reached for the cheap sour cream. "That's just sadness in a tub."

"You've tried it?"

"I can read the label. Also, I've got standards."

"High ones."

"Someone has to."

He followed her through the aisles, taking what she passed him and carrying the basket when it got heavy. It filled with smoked sausage, pot cheese, a jar of sour cherries, two bags of flour, sweet paprika, hot paprika, and a smaller paper packet from a shelf behind the till that she said was the only one worth buying. By the time they reached the bread, they also had a net bag of onions, a cabbage with outer leaves split by cold, poppy-seed rolls, and a packet of apricot wafers she tried to hide under the bread.

He touched the corner of the packet. "For you?"

"For a criminal I support from abroad."

"Six-year-old criminal?"

"The worst kind."

The woman at the till looked up when they came over. She had one lens thicker than the other in her glasses and kept a biro clipped through the strap of her apron like a cigarette. Her name badge said KATI in blue marker, though the T had smudged into a crossbar. She saw the wafers and clicked her tongue at Eszter.

"Apricot again? That girl will grow up impossible."

"She already is." Eszter dug coins from the bottom of her bag. "She says the peach ones taste like school soap."

"She's right. Smart child."

Kati glanced at Beni and then back at Eszter with the quick, shameless community arithmetic of a place like this. It was not flirtation, or even gossip yet, just filing facts for later use.

"This yours?" she asked.

Eszter did not look embarrassed. "Temporarily."

"Then feed him properly before somebody rich steals the rest of him."

"That's the plan," Eszter said.

Beni took the heavier bag from her before she could protest. Kati laughed through her nose and started telling the man behind them that the butcher in Wood Green was a thief and always had been. Outside, the air felt colder after the shop's steam. Eszter stopped under the awning and shifted the handles in her hand.

"She's awful," Beni said.

"Kati? She's been in London fifteen years and still thinks everyone else's life is for her entertainment. It keeps her young."

"You come here every week?"

"When I can. If I buy proper things once, I can eat badly three more times and pretend it balances out."

She pulled the apricot wafers from the bag and looked at the packet, not smiling exactly, but softening at one edge.

"I send her these when someone is driving home," she said. "Not by post. Post is robbery. Coach drivers, cousins, people with too much luggage already. The wafers survive. Bananas don't."

They got coffee from the tiny counter at the back of the shop, thick and overboiled and too hot in paper cups, and ate standing up at a ledge by the window while the door kept opening and letting November in. Eszter tore a poppy-seed roll in half and gave him the better piece without making a show of it.

"Did Noor scare Mercer?" she asked.

"Maybe. Or I scared them myself."

"You nearly put a client into a lorry, yes?"

"Nearly."

"Nearly is enough. Men lose jobs for less and keep them for worse."

She said it as if reading weather. It was neither comforting nor cruel. He drank coffee that tasted burnt all the way through.

"You all right now?" she asked after a minute.

He looked at the wet glass instead of her. "Enough."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the one I've got."

Eszter nodded once. She did not let him off it, but she left a little room around him. That too was hers: pressure with somewhere to put your feet.

A phone buzzed in his pocket. He knew the rhythm before he took it out. Nina had a way of calling that felt impatient even in silence; she rarely let it ring long, as if the fact of wanting contact should be enough to bring a person running. The screen lit his hand white.

Mrs. Ashdown.

He turned it face down against his thigh before Eszter could glance over. The vibration stopped. Then came a message almost at once, the same name bright at the top of the screen. He slid the phone back into his pocket without opening it.

Eszter had noticed something. Maybe it was not the name, but the movement itself.

"Work?"

"Maybe tomorrow." He shrugged. "Mercer."

"On a Sunday?"

"They like panic in all seven days."

She accepted that, or seemed to. He felt the lie sit down between his ribs like a passenger who had every right to the seat.

They left the shop and walked toward the station with the bags knocking against their knees. Street vendors were setting out bruised clementines and damp herbs under striped tarps. A man on the corner sold phone cases from a blanket and sang along badly to a Romanian pop song playing from his speaker. Eszter moved through it with purpose, swerving only once to buy a packet of pink elastic hair ties from a pound shop display.

"Lili likes pink?" he asked.

"Lili likes whatever the girls with mothers at home already have." Eszter tucked the packet into the bag with the wafers and flattened the crinkled plastic with her thumb. "This week it's pink hair ties. Next month it'll be some stupid backpack or shoes with lights. She'll look at me through the phone and say everybody else has them, and there goes another ten quid."

He laughed before he meant to. "You make her sound terrible."

"She is terrible. She once cried for forty minutes because I cut her toast in squares instead of stairs. Stairs only, apparently. She has principles."

"And you miss that."

Eszter looked at him then, straight and unguarded for a second. "Yes," she said. "I miss exactly that."

The Victoria line platform was crowded with Sunday people: couples with plants, boys in Arsenal jackets, a woman holding a foil tray level in both hands as if balancing an organ for transplant. When the train came it was already warm and full. They found two seats apart at first, then a family got off at Seven Sisters and Eszter slid in beside him, the bags at their feet, her shoulder pressed to his upper arm because there was nowhere else for it to go.

The train noise took some of the duty out of speaking. Eszter watched reflected strangers in the black window between stations and talked without looking directly at him, which made it easier to hear.

"My mother says London has eaten my best years and sent back money instead," she said.

"What's the score?"

"What?"

"If London's winning."

She huffed a laugh. "At the moment? Yes. It always takes a cut. Rent. Oyster. phone top-ups. That agency that placed me in the first house, they charged me before I got the first wage. Then transfer fees, then school things, then medicine. By the time the money reaches Debrecen, it's half wages and half apology."

She nudged one of the bags with the toe of her boot.

"London likes mothers best when the kid's somewhere else," she said. "No school pickup, no fever, no teacher ringing while you've got bleach up to your elbows. You can scrub a bath for twelve hours and never ask to leave early. That's when you're perfect to them."

Beni listened to the train rattle them north. In the tunnel reflection he could see her mouth harden after she finished, as if she had bitten down on something small and sharp.

"Who has her?" he asked.

"My mother in the week. My aunt when my mother picks up cleaning. They trade her like a decent package." She shifted her cup between her hands. "She knows I'm in London. She thinks London is one very long hotel corridor where I make beds for everybody and then come home later. Which is not entirely wrong."

"You go home much?"

"Twice this year." She held up two fingers. "Both times too short. The first time she wouldn't let go of my coat. The second time she was cross because I'd missed sports day and the rabbit at school had died before she could tell me properly."

"What rabbit?"

"Class rabbit. White, one eye always dirty. She'd been feeding it carrot tops from her sandwich box. She made me look at six photos of a dead rabbit because grief likes witnesses."

He could see the child suddenly, not as an obligation or a distant fact but as a small furious person with sticky fingers and exact feelings. It changed the weight of the wafers in the bag at his feet.

"How old was she when you left?" he asked.

"Four." Eszter rubbed condensation from the lid of her coffee with her thumb. "Old enough to understand suitcase means something bad. Too young to understand mortgage, debt, and a man who thinks your wages are a public utility."

He turned to her. "Her father?"

"Yes, that idiot."

She said it lightly, but the line between her eyebrows stayed put.

"He wasn't always an idiot," she went on. "That would make the story too easy. He was funny. He was good with babies. He could fix a washing machine with a spoon and bad language. Then work went bad, then pride went bad, then everything he touched started turning into an invoice." She looked down at her hands. "When there wasn't enough money, he behaved like the missing money was me."

The train lurched into Tottenham Hale. A girl with blue nails stood to let them out. Beni took both bags as they moved toward the doors.

"What happened?" he asked on the platform.

Eszter zipped her coat to the throat before answering. "Later," she said. "I'm not telling that part under Tube maps and somebody's chips."

He nodded. The honesty of the refusal steadied him more than a prettier answer would have.

They took the bus from the station because the bags were heavy and the air was starting to bite through coats. From the top deck the city flattened into terraces and small front gardens full of wet plastic toys. Eszter leaned her head back against the glass and watched the street.

"You know what the worst part is?" she said.

"What?"

"Everyone thinks sending money home is generous. Noble. They say, ah, good mother, working so hard. As if I chose the clever arrangement. Nobody says the real thing, which is that I had to leave my child to afford leaving her father."

The words sat in him with the plain force of a tool dropped on concrete. He looked at her hands. Bleach had roughened the knuckles. There was a small crescent scar near the thumb he had never noticed before.

"You don't have to tell me if you don't want," he said.

"I know. That's why I'm telling you."

When they got off near the flat, the wind was sharper. Beni checked the windows automatically as they crossed to his building. There were no lights in the back room and no movement behind the lace curtain upstairs where the Polish woman watched the street like a hobby. He unlocked the door and let Eszter go in first, then followed with the bags pulling his shoulders down.

The front room embarrassed him every time she saw it, though less than it had the first time. The damp still ran up one corner of the wall in a shape like a map of a country nobody wanted. His blanket was folded tighter than usual on the sofa bed. The little sink held one clean glass upside down on a towel. He had shoved his shoes under the chair and opened the window a crack before leaving, which now meant the room smelled of cold plaster instead of cold plaster and sleep.

"Laci?" Eszter asked.

"Cash job in Enfield. He said late."

"Good." She looked around once, taking inventory the way she always did, not to judge but because space told the truth faster than people. "You've cleaned."

"A bit."

"A dangerous level of effort from you."
