She took off her coat and laid it carefully over the chair. Then she began unpacking the bags as if she lived there often enough to know where things should go. Bread on the sill. Sour cream near the coldest bit of window. Onions under the chair. She held up the cabbage and shook her head.

"This kitchen of yours is criminal."

"There's a hob."

"Shared by seven men and one pan with no handle. Don't romanticize."

He smiled despite himself. She saw it and softened by another inch.

"Make tea," she said. "Strong. I need to thaw before I can insult you properly."

He filled the kettle at the sink while she stood close enough behind him that he could feel the cold of her coat still leaving her body. It had been weeks of this with them: mouths and hands, the charge rising, then the place inside him going blank when he should have stepped forward. The room seemed smaller with the day darkening outside and her in it. The walls pressed close. It was charged.

When he turned, she was holding the packet of hair ties and the wafers.

"Don't sit on these," she said. "I need them intact."

"Are you posting them tomorrow?"

"No. Mrs. Horvath's nephew is driving to Debrecen on Tuesday. He carries half the country's paprika and the other half's guilt in his boot."

"Does he charge?"

"Of course he charges."

She said it with a tired amusement that belonged to a whole system of people making each other possible for a price smaller than the official one and still too large. She put the things carefully back in her bag, then sat on the edge of the sofa bed while the kettle clicked and wheezed.

"Come here," she said.

He stood between her knees. She rested her hands on his hips through the jumper, not pulling yet.

"You still look like someone hit you with a tray," she said. "Did you sleep?"

"Not much."

"Did you eat?"

"Coffee."

"That isn't food."

"There was bread on the bus."

"You see? This is why you need management."

She leaned her forehead briefly against his chest. He put a hand on the back of her head. Her hair was colder than his palm. For a moment neither of them moved. The kettle began to rattle harder.

"Tea first," she said into his jumper. "If I faint and crack my head in your room, the ambulance men will think you're hopeless."

They drank from mismatched mugs, sitting close on the bed with the shopping at their feet, knees touching because the room gave them no choice and because neither of them wanted it to. Eszter ate half a roll, licked poppy seeds from her thumb, and told him about one of the women who cleaned on the square behind the Ashdowns.

"She sends money to two sons and an ex-husband with diabetes," Eszter said. "He had the nerve to complain the notes smelled of bleach. I told her to wipe them over the toilet first."

"Did she?"

"No. She's nicer than me. That's why she's poorer."

"And you?"

"I'm poor with opinions."

They laughed, and the laugh did something dangerous: it made the day feel ordinary enough to trust. Beni felt it at once, that old bad instinct to enjoy what he had and call it safe just because no one was shouting. He set his mug down on the floor before he could spill it.

Eszter saw the shift in him. "What?"

"Nothing."

"Not good enough."

He looked at the damp corner of the wall. "I'm trying not to ruin today before lunch."

"Then don't."

"It's that easy?"

"No." She wiped her mouth with the back of her wrist and nudged his mug away from the edge with two fingers. "But if you're going to sit there staring at that damp patch like it's got answers in it, that's boring, and I don't have time for boring men."

He looked back at her. She held his gaze and offered no softness as discount.

"Last time," she said, more quietly now, "you went away while you were still in the room. If that happens again, I want to know before I'm halfway naked. I'm not here to guess for you."

There it was, the standard from the other night, cleaner now, no whisky on it, no room for misunderstanding.

"All right," he said.

"No, not all right. Say you understand."

"I understand."

"Good."

She put her mug aside. Then she leaned in and kissed him with practical certainty, as if a decision had already been made elsewhere and this was the part where it got carried out. He kissed her back with more hunger than care at first. She caught his lower lip between her teeth and pulled once, enough to make him slow down.

"Don't attack," she murmured. "I'm not a sandwich."

He laughed against her mouth. The laugh opened him more than any apology would have. He put a hand at the side of her neck and kissed her again, slower now, feeling the warmth finally return to her skin. She climbed onto the bed properly, one knee on either side of his thigh, and pushed the jumper up under his ribs with both hands.

Her palms were rough from cleaning chemicals and hot now from the mug. The contrast made his back tighten. She dragged her thumbs along the skin of his stomach and looked at him the whole time.

"Still here?" she asked.

"Yeah."

"Say it like a person."

"I'm here."

"Better."

He pulled her jumper over her head. Her bra was plain black, one strap twisted, and there was a pale line on her shoulder where it had bitten during some long shift. He kissed that mark before he kissed her breast. She made a small sound, surprised clean out of her, and it went through him in a direct line, desire stripped of bargain and wages.

The bus outside hit a pothole and rattled the window. Somebody in the upstairs flat ran water through pipes that knocked in the wall. The room stayed itself: damp, thin, alive with other people's noise. He liked that better than magic.

Eszter unbuttoned his jeans and sat back enough to look at him.

"Condom?"

"Yeah."

He reached into the drawer of the little table beside the bed, felt the foil packet under old receipts, and for a second his fingers brushed the smooth cold shape of the silver lighter beneath it. He closed the drawer too fast. Eszter noticed the movement but not, he thought, what caused it.

She took the condom from his hand and put it on the bed between them.

"I hate that you keep these next to bus tickets," she said.

"It's a drawer."

"It's a junk grave. Move over."

She pushed him back onto the pillows and kissed down his chest, over his stomach, then lower. When she took him into her mouth he had to grip the blanket to stay in the room. The rightness of it hit him hard, and he had less practice with that than he should have. He watched the crown of her head, the dark hair falling forward, the canvas shopping bag upright by the chair with cabbage leaves showing at the top, and kept hauling himself back from the old habit of climbing out through observation. Her hand slid under his thigh and squeezed once.

"Don't go polite on me," she said, and then took him deeper.

He made a sound he would have been ashamed of anywhere else. She looked up at him, still holding him in her mouth, and the look held amusement, tenderness, and a hard refusal to hide what she was doing to him. When she stopped he pulled her up by the waist and kissed her hard enough that she laughed into it.

"Greedy," she said.

"Sorry."

"If you apologize again, I'll charge you."

He got her tights off carefully because the room was cold and the fabric snagged at the heel. Her underwear was plain cotton, dark blue, washed thin. She saw him looking.

"Don't get romantic about practical pants," she said.

"I wasn't."

"Good. They came in a three-pack."

When he touched her through them she spread her legs wider and let her head fall back, one arm crooked over her eyes for a second before she dropped it and looked at him again. Always that. She would not let him hide in the blur. He kissed her stomach, the small soft scar low on one side he had never seen before, then the inside of her thigh. Her hand came to the back of his neck.

"Yes," she said, not loud. "There. Not gentle for the sake of it."

He hooked his fingers into the waistband and pulled her underwear down. Then he put his mouth on her and learned her as directly as he could, not pretending experience he did not have. She tasted of salt and the coffee from the shop seemed still to live somewhere in the warmth of her skin. He kept one hand flat over her hip to stop himself from rushing. She swore in Hungarian first, then in English. The second time her body tightened under his hand she pulled his hair hard enough to sting.

"Don't stop," she said.

He did not. The springs under the mattress gave a soft complaining squeal every time she moved. Outside, a siren crossed the main road and was gone. The room narrowed to the pressure of her thighs against his shoulders, her breath changing, the heel of one foot knocking against the wall. When she came she bit the inside of her wrist instead of crying out, then pushed his hand away from her mouth as if annoyed at herself for needing to be quiet at all.

"Fuck the neighbors," she said hoarsely.

"Laci might not."

"Then he'll learn something."

She pulled him up over her and kissed him with her own taste still on his mouth. He had the condom on clumsily, fingers thick with wanting her. She took over halfway through, rolling it down with the calm competence of a woman who had done more of life than he had and was not interested in making that fact pretty. Then she shifted, reached between them, and guided him in.

He had expected the old wrongness to open under him, the old split where he would watch himself from above because distance had always felt safer than feeling anything straight. Contact hit him clean instead, and her face stayed inches from his, serious and fixed on him. He moved once and she hissed through her teeth.

"Again," she said.

He did. The bed knocked against the wall. She caught the headboard with one hand to steady it, practical even then, and wrapped the other arm around his back. They found a rhythm that belonged to the room they were in: short, careful at first because of the springs and the walls and the fear of noise, then less careful when the fear stopped mattering. Her ankle hooked around his calf. His shoulder hit the damp-cold patch on the wall when she turned them and he barely noticed. She was strong under him, not passive, shifting her hips to tell him yes or no without drama, biting once at his shoulder hard enough to leave a mark.

"Look at me," she said when he closed his eyes.

He opened them. Her hair had come half loose across the pillow. There was a flush rising from her chest to her throat. She looked worked and alive, not arranged. He felt something in himself give way then, not collapse, more like a fist unclenching around a coin it had held too long.

He put his forehead against hers and kept moving. She whispered something he did not catch, maybe Hungarian, maybe just breath turned into shape. When she came again her whole body jerked hard enough to shake the bed, and the sound she made this time she did not try to hide. He came after, mouth open against her shoulder, every muscle in him suddenly stupid and empty in the best way. For a second he could hear only the radiator ticking and both of them trying not to breathe like people who had run for a train.

They stayed that way until the practical world returned. There was the condom, the tissue, and her foot searching blindly for the dropped tights. Somebody laughed in the hallway outside, then a door slammed somewhere downstairs. Eszter lay back on the bed in just her bra and said, "If your cousin comes in now, I'll haunt him."

"He'd deserve it."

"He would."

He stripped the condom off and tied it, wrapped it in tissue, and took it to the bin by the sink. The ordinary ugliness of the thing calmed him. When he turned back, Eszter was sitting cross-legged on the bed with the blanket over her lap, hair fully loose now, looking around for the mugs.

"Tea's cold," she said.

"I can make more."

"In a minute."

He sat beside her. His body still hummed with spent heat and surprise. That was the strange part. No client was waiting to see whether he had understood the assignment, and no money would be folded into his hand after. The mattress springs pushed them together and she let it happen.

She touched the bruise-dark half moon under his eye where sleep had failed him and then the scar at his brow. "You stayed," she said.

"Yeah."

"Good."

Her praise, when it came, was almost mean in its smallness. He trusted it more for that.

He reached for his jeans, meaning only to find a cigarette he did not have, something to do with his hands. The lighter came out of the pocket instead, heavier than he remembered. Silver, scratched on one side, the hinge a little loose. He had not meant to show it. Eszter looked at it, then at him.

"That the thing in the drawer?" she asked.

"No. I mean, yes. It was there too."

"You keep secrets like a child hiding biscuits."

He turned the lighter over in his palm. The engraved initials had worn down so badly they no longer belonged clearly to anyone.

"Whose is it?" she asked.

For a moment he considered the easy exit, the cheap one. He could say he'd found it, bought it, that it didn't matter. He had lived the last weeks inside easy exits until even he was sick of his own footprints.

"Klara's," he said.

Eszter went quiet and watchful. She knew the name from scraps, from the shape his silences took, from the story he had not yet told. She pulled the blanket higher over her legs and waited.

"I was seventeen," he said. "She was married. Older. She had a daughter. Not little-little, school age." He looked at the lighter instead of Eszter. "Everybody thinks if you say that part first it explains the whole thing. It doesn't, but it's part of it."

"No," Eszter said. "It doesn't explain it. Go on."

He swallowed. The room smelled of sex now, warm skin and damp cotton and the cabbage still not put away properly.

"I drove sometimes before London," he said. "Farm roads, town errands, whatever people paid for. She'd ask if I could take her somewhere. Then not somewhere. Just out. Around." He rubbed his thumb over the worn metal. "At first it was stupid. Then it was more than stupid."

"You loved her?"

The question did not sound sentimental in her mouth. It sounded administrative, like asking whether a bill had been paid.

"I thought I did." He breathed in through his nose, held it. "Maybe I did. In the way you can at seventeen when a woman looks straight at you and makes you feel older instead of younger."

Eszter said nothing. The radiator clicked twice.

"One night it was raining," he said. "She'd had a fight at home. She called and wanted driving, nowhere special, just away. I went. We were on a back road outside town. Mud at the edges, no lines you could trust. She was talking fast. I looked at her too long instead of the road."

He could feel it starting again as he spoke: the slick of wet light, wipers too slow, the stupid confidence of youth acting like concentration was the same as skill.

"A van came round the bend," he said. "I pulled wrong. There wasn't enough room. We hit the side, then the ditch. The airbags went off. Glass everywhere." He closed his hand around the lighter. "That's the clean version. She died before they got her out."

Eszter's face did not change much. He loved her a little for that too. There was no performance of pity and no reaching immediately to make him easier to look at.

"And you?" she asked.

"Brow. Ribs. Nothing worth the price."

"Police?"

"Yes."

"Were you charged?"

"No. Rain, road, her not wearing the belt properly. Plenty of blame to share around. In towns like mine they call that mercy and then they still look at you in the butcher's."

He gave a short laugh that hurt his chest. "I left a few months later."

"Because of the crash?"

"Because of all of it." He looked up at her then. "Because every road felt borrowed after. Because people knew without knowing. Because I couldn't go near her street and couldn't stop thinking about it either."

Eszter rubbed the blanket seam between finger and thumb. "Does her daughter know?"

"Know what?"

"That there was an affair. That you were there for more than driving."

"I don't know." He thought of a girl he had seen only twice in person, once kicking a football against a garage wall, once asleep in the back seat with a glitter clip hanging crooked above one ear and biscuit dust on the booster seat. "Probably not then. Maybe now. Small towns are generous with other people's facts."

Eszter let that sit. Then she leaned forward, elbows on knees, the blanket slipping so he could see the marks his fingers had left on her thigh.

"Lili's father wasn't older," she said. "He wasn't glamorous either, before you imagine some idiot with a leather jacket and sad guitar. He stocked shelves in a builders' merchant and knew every football result from three countries. He could make a baby laugh by sneezing on purpose. For a while that seemed enough."

Beni listened. The light outside the window had gone from white to iron grey.

"Then he hurt his back," she said. "Not badly enough for sympathy, but badly enough for anger. He was home more and worked less. Then there was betting, then borrowing, then little lies about what he'd spent. The usual shit." She shrugged. "You can live through a lot of usual shit. Women do it every day."

She picked a loose thread on the blanket and wound it around one finger until the tip blanched.

"The part that made me leave wasn't the first shout," she said. "Not even the first time he took money from the flour tin. It was one evening in winter. I'd hidden cash for Lili's nursery fees inside an old sanitary-pad box because he never looked there. He came home half drunk and went through the kitchen anyway. Flour on the floor, tins open, the whole place turned over. Lili woke and came in crying. He told her to shut up. She wouldn't. He grabbed her by the arm and moved her aside like furniture."

Her voice stayed steady. That steadiness did more than tears would have.

"He didn't beat her," she said. "People start sorting it straight away so they can sleep at night. Bruised, not bruised. Police, no police. Good man, bad man. He left marks for two days. I was still brushing flour off her sleeve and I knew if I stayed he'd have a new reason ready next time. Money. Drink. Shame. Being tired."

Beni looked at the crescent scar by her thumb again and understood it in the company of flour dust and a child crying in a kitchen that should have been safe.

"So I left," she said. "I didn't leave elegantly. My mother took Lili for what was meant to be three weeks. I came to London because a cousin knew a woman who knew a cleaning agency and because I had two hundred pounds and no better miracle. Three weeks became months. Then schools, then rent, then papers, then the stupid fact that once a child settles somewhere you feel like a criminal for moving her again. So now I clean rich people's baths and post hair ties home in other people's boots."

She laughed once, without humor. "That's the romance."

"Do you still talk to him?" Beni asked.

"Only about Lili, and only when I have to." She lifted one shoulder. "He behaves better now. Distance makes some men look almost decent. It doesn't make me stupid."

He reached out and touched the thread she had wound too tight around her finger. She let him unwind it.

"Why are you telling me this?" he asked.

"Because you told me the ugly version and not the souvenir one." She shifted closer. "Because if I'm in your bed and half your history is in the room anyway, I'd rather know which half."

Half your history, she had said. The words landed with a force out of proportion to their volume. They were only half, not all, and the missing part seemed suddenly lit from behind.

His phone buzzed again in the pocket of his jeans on the floor.

Both of them looked toward the sound. Once, twice, then silence.

Eszter raised her eyebrows.

"Mercer?" she asked.

He hated how quickly the answer came, smooth from use. "Probably."

She watched him another beat. "You don't have to answer."

"I know."

He did not move for it. That was the cowardice and the proof at once. He could sit naked beside a woman who had just trusted him with the ugliest architecture of her life and still protect the other arrangement as if it were fragile, as if Nina were the one who might shatter from daylight.

Eszter leaned back against the wall and looked at the jeans on the floor. "Past, fine," she said. "Everybody over fifteen's got one. It's the phone buzzing in your trousers now that's the problem."

He kept his face still. The bed frame pressed cold through the blanket against his spine.

"There isn't anything I need to know?" she asked.

The question landed with care. She had narrowed it enough to be merciful and left it wide enough to matter.

He should have told her then. The room had arranged itself for truth: the dark window, the shopping still unpacked, the smell of them, the lighter in his hand like evidence from an older case. He thought of Nina's mouth in the garage, Nina's money folded into envelopes, Nina telling him he was safe because he never asked for anything. He thought of the phone bright in his pocket at the grocer, the name turned down against his thigh while Eszter bought wafers for her daughter.

"No," he said.

Eszter held his eyes. Belief and doubt moved across her face too quickly for him to sort, and then practicality won.

"All right," she said at last. It landed like a drawer shoved shut with things still rattling inside.

She swung her legs off the bed and reached for her underwear from the floor. By the time he had breath for her name, she had pulled on the dark-blue cotton, then her tights, then her jumper, brisk and practical, as if nakedness had become a bad bargain.

"Eszter-"

"I'm not arguing half naked," she said. She took the lighter from his hand, looked at it once, then set it carefully on the little table. "Put your trousers on. Let's eat before I have to go."

They put the groceries away properly after that, because practical work was easier than touching the raw place he had just covered up. He cut bread. She sliced sausage and spread pot cheese with the blunt knife that bent if you asked it for honesty. Now and then his wrist brushed hers and she did not pull away, but she did not lean into him either. Once she caught herself thumbing one of Lili's pink elastics and slid the card straight back into the bag.

They ate on the bed because there was nowhere else, but not cross-legged and tangled now. Eszter sat with one boot already laced, the other on the floor, talking in shorter pieces about Lili refusing to wear wool tights unless they were turned inside out because she hated the seam on her toes, about a teacher who kept sending notes home about handwriting as if better loops on the page would make the heating work. He told her about the village shop where old men had once made him buy cigarettes one by one because they liked watching boys pretend not to cough. The talk kept the room from going dead. It did not make it easy again.

By six she was already in her coat, bag repacked, Monday back in the room before she opened the door. The Ashdowns would want breakfasts and ironed shirts and the house smelling like money again by morning. When she bent to lace the second boot, he watched the muscles in her forearms move under the skin and thought again of how much of her life required lifting other people's weight.

"Will you sleep tonight?" she asked.

"Maybe."

"Bad answer."

"I'll try."

"Better." She hitched the bag higher on her shoulder and checked it once with her fingertips. "If work calls and you're not right, say so. Dead chauffeurs are no use to anyone."

"Romantic."

"You don't need romance. You need sleep and the sense to say no if your hands start shaking."

He reached for her. She gave him one quick kiss, warm but already leaving, then kept her palm flat on his chest when he started to follow her into a second.

At the door she looked back into the room as if checking what state she was leaving him in.

"Move that cabbage," she said. "It'll stink by morning."

"Yes, boss."

"And Beni."

"Yeah?"

"Don't make me stupid."

She laid it down as terms.

After she left, the room held her for a while. There was warmth in the blanket and one dark hair on the pillow. The smell of her cheap apple shampoo mixed with damp wall and sausage fat. He stood in the middle of the floorboards listening to her steps go down the stairwell and out through the front door, each one taking something and leaving something else behind.

Then the phone buzzed again.

He picked up his jeans from the floor and took it out. Two missed calls. One message.

`Hope you're recovered. Need you tomorrow before Mercer if possible. Delete this.`

Below it, a second message sent five minutes later.

`Please answer when you can.`

He sat on the edge of the bed with the phone in one hand and the silver lighter in the other. The little table held only the lighter now. She had checked the bag before leaving and taken the pink hair ties with the wafers, clearing the room of small excuses. What she left behind was the dent in the mattress and the smell in the blanket. He imagined her on the bus touching the bag once at every stop, making sure she had not left anything behind. That was competence, hard and unsentimental. The room stayed difficult anyway.

Outside, a bus changed gear and sent a long shudder through the window frame. The room was exactly as it had been that morning and not the same at all. He could still feel where Eszter's body had pressed the mattress down beside him. He could still hear her saying present tense is the problem.

Beni deleted Nina's messages and kept the number in the phone. Mercer had him at ten the next morning. The Ashdowns wanted the hours before that.


# Chapter 13

The radiator in the Ashdowns' basement kitchen was on, but only in theory. It clicked and sighed behind the drying aprons and gave off just enough heat to keep the tiles from feeling damp through the soles of Beni's shoes. The room still smelled of coffee, bleach, and yesterday's stock, but the ripe sweetness from the fruit bowl was gone. In its place sat twelve clementines in a neat row on the stainless counter, leaves shriveled to paper at the stems, waiting to be carried upstairs and made to look abundant.

This was how money trouble arrived in a rich house. It did not come as darkness or panic, and no silver went missing. The lights still burned in every hallway. The brass still shone. But there were fewer herbs in the morning delivery, cheaper yogurt decanted into the expensive bowls, and a new rule that only one strip of fluorescent lighting was left on in the mews garage unless a car was actually moving. The house still pretended ease. It just asked the people under it to do more of the pretending.

Mrs. Keene stood by the service door with the invoice clipped to a board, her navy cardigan buttoned wrong at the middle and a fennel seed working from one side of her mouth to the other. She always had something small to chew after ten in the morning, as if appetite needed to be disciplined in public. Today it was fennel. Yesterday it had been mint gum. Her eyes flicked over the crates on the floor.

"Three trays of eggs, not four," she said to the delivery man. "And where are the pomegranates?"

"Order came through for one case only."

"We usually have three."

He gave her the sort of shrug men used when they wanted the paperwork to become somebody else's theology. "Then they've changed their minds."

Eszter, in her grey work T-shirt and black trousers, was checking labels against the old pantry list with a pencil behind her ear. Her hair was tied up hard and high, not a strand loose. She had a dish towel slung through the back of her waistband like a tail. Without looking at Beni she said, in Hungarian, "Apparently winter means they can now survive on one pomegranate."

He set Bea's school bag beside the back stairs and answered in the same language. "Maybe they found religion."

Eszter gave the smallest snort. "No. If it was religion we'd all be eating worse."

She sounded like herself, but not fully. The words landed too cleanly. There was no play after them. She crossed out three things on the pantry sheet and wrote new quantities in the margin. Beni saw the numbers before he saw what they meant: six yogurts instead of twelve, one side of salmon instead of two, no fresh dill at all.

From the corridor came the light, irritated ring of a phone ending and the harder sound of a man's heel turning on stone. Mrs. Keene straightened automatically. Eszter did not straighten. She only moved the pencil from behind her ear to the counter and wiped her fingers on the towel.

Oscar Ashdown came into the kitchen carrying a printed rota and his reading glasses in one hand. He wore a charcoal suit and no overcoat, though it was raining in the mews. His hair at the temples looked silverer in the strip light than it ever did upstairs. Beni had seen him angry only twice, and both times the anger had arrived as precision, not volume. Today he looked almost rested, which was worse.

"Morning," Oscar said.

Mrs. Keene answered at once. "Morning, sir."

Eszter said, "Morning."

Beni was not sure whether he counted as staff for this conversation or furniture. He stayed where he was by the end of the counter, one hand on Bea's bag.

Oscar looked at the crates, then at the invoice. "Good. That's in line with what I asked for."

Mrs. Keene swallowed the fennel seed. "There are a few substitutions."

"There will be more. That's fine." He put the rota down on the counter, flattening it with his palm. "We're tightening the household budget through the gala. Pantry is down fifteen percent from this week. Flowers are reduced except on event days. Duplicate evening cover stops immediately. I don't want any visible drop in standard."

Eszter said, "Visible to whom?"

Mrs. Keene's eyes cut toward her, quick and warning.

Oscar did not raise his voice. "To anyone whose opinion costs money."

For a second the kitchen gave back only the hiss from the kettle. Somewhere above them a door closed softly, thick carpet swallowing the end of it.

Eszter picked up the pencil again. "And invisible standards?"

"You know exactly what I mean."

"I do. I wanted to hear you say it."

Beni felt the cold metal edge of Bea's lunchbox under his hand. The room had narrowed. Mrs. Keene was staring at the rota as if she could enter it by force.

Oscar moved his attention to the page, which was his way of making the page more important than the person. "The live-in room is suspended after Friday. We don't need overnight coverage at current occupancy. Eszter, you'll move to day rota only for now. Monday, Wednesday, Friday until the gala week, then we'll reassess."

Eszter's face did not change. She looked down at the printed lines. "You said that room was part of the arrangement."

"It was. The arrangement has changed."

"To what?"

"To three days' work and no room, for the moment."

Mrs. Keene said quietly, "We can speak after Bea's gone in, Eszter."

Eszter ignored her. "By Friday?"

"By Sunday afternoon," Oscar said. "I'm not putting you on the street. I'm asking for flexibility."

That almost made Beni laugh, and because it almost made him laugh it also made him want to spit. He kept his face still.

Eszter set the pencil down with care. "What a beautiful word."

Oscar adjusted his glasses in his hand. "This doesn't need dramatics."

"No," Eszter said. "Only arithmetic."

He gave her a look that would have made most people back away, as if he were checking whether a door would shut if pushed. Cruelty would have admitted feeling. "You'll have your final overnight payment included. Keene will sort the details."

"And the gala still gets twelve kinds of canape?"

"The gala is not up for discussion."

"Of course it isn't."

Bea's footsteps sounded on the back stairs before anyone could answer. Light, quick, then a pause on the last step while she took in the room. She had her blazer on but no tie, her hair tied badly with a black ribbon that had nearly slipped out. She looked from the small morning delivery to Oscar to Eszter and understood enough to roll her eyes at her father before she looked away again.

"Is there any proper yogurt left," she said, "or are we doing wartime now?"

"Bea," Oscar said.

"I'm asking. Last night the hummus tasted like punishment."

Mrs. Keene moved at once toward the fridge. "There is Greek yogurt, miss."

"The cheap one?"

Eszter said, "It still works if you put it in your mouth."

Bea's mouth twitched. Not quite a smile. "Good to know."

Oscar picked up the rota. "Car in two minutes."

"I can hear the car from here," Bea said. Then she took the yogurt Mrs. Keene handed her, peeled back the lid, and ate one spoonful standing by the sink. Her eyes went back to Eszter for one beat too long. Beni saw it. So did Eszter. Oscar did not, or pretended not to.

Beni took Bea's bag again and headed for the corridor. On the way out he heard Oscar say, to Mrs. Keene, "Please make sure the attic room is clear before the florist storage starts arriving."

He heard the phrase florist storage and understood that there was not even a person in it after all, only square footage.

Outside, the mews smelled of wet brick and diesel. Bea got into the back of the Mercedes and pulled the door shut harder than necessary.

"Did he sack someone," she asked before Beni had even started the engine.

"No."

"That's not a real answer."

Beni checked the mirror. "Seat belt."

She clicked it with enough force to make the buckle snap. "Mum says when he starts talking about efficiencies it means somebody else is paying."

He pulled out of the mews into slow, glazed traffic. Rain had polished the road to the colour of fish skin. "Your school bag's leaking glitter."

"That's because the art department hates us."

That was all she offered. She leaned her forehead against the window and drew a small circle in the condensation with one finger. He drove her to school through Eaton Square and Knightsbridge, passing shop windows already staged for Christmas. Everything glittered harder when the weather was mean.

When he got back to Belgravia, the kitchen had gone quiet in the way kitchens did when anger was being worked off through small necessary tasks. Mrs. Keene was upstairs. Eszter was at the sink scraping labels off old jam jars so they could be reused in the pantry. The hot water had steamed the lower half of the window. Her phone was propped in the corner under the cupboard, playing a child's voice note in thin bursts through the speaker. It was Hungarian, too fast for the cheap phone microphone, and Beni only caught pieces of it: a complaint about maths, a dog barking, then a girl laughing at her own mistake.

Eszter hit pause with her wrist because her hands were wet.

"Lili?" he asked.

"Who else talks like she invented the world."

He stood beside the sink. Up close he could see that the skin over her knuckles had split in two places from cleaning chemicals and dry air. She had rubbed hand cream over it anyway. The smell under the soap was almond.

"What happens now?" he said.

She kept scraping. Paper came away from the jar in wet curls. "Now I work three days unless they decide to discover another emergency. Now I lose the room because apparently peonies need somewhere to sleep before the gala. Now I pay to get to Belgravia for the privilege of cleaning rooms I no longer live in."

"Have you got somewhere?"

"Maybe. Marika from St. Stephen's knows a woman with a church-basement room in Holloway. Clean, strict, no men, no noise, no cooking after ten. Very sexy. She wants two weeks up front because the last girl disappeared with a kettle and forty pounds."

"How much?"

Eszter dried her hands and finally looked at him. Fear made her tidier. Even her eyebrows looked organized. "Two hundred and forty by Friday to hold it. Then Oyster, then the money home. My mother says Lili's winter boots are already pinching. That girl grows out of things like she's doing it for revenge."

Beni knew the exact shape of two hundred and forty pounds in notes. Four fifties, two twenties. Or twelve twenties if that was what the cash machine gave. He also knew what he had in his wallet: sixty-three pounds, a ten for diesel he still needed, and a folded fiver stuck behind his licence for luck he did not believe in.

"I can ask around," he said. It sounded pathetic as soon as it was out.

Eszter tossed the ruined label into the bin. "For a room? Unless you know a lonely duchess in Stamford Hill, don't bother."

"I meant with drivers. Someone might know a place."

"Drivers know places for drivers. I'm not sleeping in a room that smells like Lynx and old socks."

The offer climbed as far as his teeth. He looked at her split knuckles, at the wet jam jars, at the rota with her nights cut out of it, and swallowed it. What he had in his wallet did not cover Holloway. She saw something move in his face and shook her head.

"Don't do the heroic look," she said. "It doesn't suit you either."

"I wasn't."

"You were absolutely about to." She picked up the next jar. "Go on. Nina will ring soon because rich women can smell bad timing better than dogs can smell meat."

That, he thought, was probably true.

He took the empty coffee cup to the sink just to have something to do with his hands. On the counter beside the jars lay the revised rota, face down. He turned it over when Eszter moved to the fridge. The days left were Monday, Wednesday, and Friday, with the nights stripped out cleanly, like a butcher had gone down a chicken and taken the useful parts.

When Nina called twenty minutes later, she did not ask whether he was free. She said, "Bring the Mercedes round. I need Hackney first and then Chambers, and I need you not to discuss either with anyone dull."

He said, "I'm here."

"How lucky for both of us."

* * *

The first box came down from Jonas Hale's studio carried by a young man with a silver hoop through one nostril and a mustard scarf wound around his neck three times although he was sweating. He knocked on the rear passenger window with two fingers, as if the glass might be artistic and fragile, then passed the cardboard box in without meeting Beni's eyes.

"There's another smaller one," he said to Nina. "And a garment bag."

"Of course there is," Nina said.

She had made Beni wait outside in the drizzle for eleven minutes without letting him pull into the loading bay. The studio building rose out of Hackney Wick in wet brick and corrugated metal, all confidence and draft. Someone had spray-painted a bright pink mouth on the roller shutter next door. Nina sat in the back seat with her coat unbuttoned, one hand bare, the other still in its glove, as if dressing completely for the weather had become too much to ask of the day.

The box on her lap was light. When it tilted, Beni saw the top layer: a silk blouse folded badly, a paperback with water damage along the bottom edge, a tangled phone charger, a face cream pot with the lid missing. It was worse than love letters: the small household rubbish of being with someone who had got bored.

The young man came back with the garment bag and the second box. This one was taped shut.

"That's all Jonas asked me to send down," he said.

Nina smiled at him with full social accuracy, the smile that left no legal bruise. "I'm sure he had a very taxing afternoon."

The young man's ears reddened. "He's in the middle of installation."

"I'm sure he's in the middle of something."

He left. Nina stared at the closed studio door until Beni heard the lock click from inside. She laughed once through her nose.

"Drive," she said.

He pulled away from the kerb. The wipers pushed a skin of dirty rain across the windscreen and back again. In the mirror he could see her looking not at the road but at her own reflection laid over it in the glass.

"Lincoln's Inn?" he asked.

"In a minute. Take the long way first."

He took the roads he would take for anyone who wanted to delay arrival without admitting that was what they were doing: west along the canal side where traffic crawled, then down through the City where the rain held people under awnings and made expensive coats look temporary. Nina kept one hand on the edge of the open box, fingers spread over the silk blouse as if pinning something down inside it.

"He couldn't even be bothered to hand me my own things," she said.

Beni said nothing.

"No, don't become even more useful than usual. It makes me murderous."

"Sorry."

"Don't apologize. It's dreary." She shut the box flaps hard enough to bow the cardboard. "Do you know the vulgar thing? I don't even want most of this. The cream's mine. The blouse is not. I wore it to please him and now it looks like somebody else's bad judgment."

He turned onto Farringdon Road. A cyclist hit the roof of the car lightly with the flat of his hand as he squeezed past, angry at a space that had not been his. Beni barely heard it. Nina's voice had gone softer, which with her never meant safer.

"Oscar's done one of his cleansing routines downstairs, hasn't he?" she said.

He did not answer quickly enough.

"Keene looked as if she'd swallowed a drawing pin. That usually means my husband has mistaken subtraction for character."

"They're cutting shifts."

"They're cutting every part of the house that doesn't photograph." She leaned back. "Eszter too, I assume."

His fingers tightened on the steering wheel, enough for the leather seam to print into his palm. He hated how fast she had found the tender place. He hated more that the tenderness existed for her to find.

"You don't have to look so alarmed," she said. "The entire basement knows you're in love with her. Bea probably knows, and Bea notices things chiefly to despise them."

"It's not like that."

"Then you're lying badly. I prefer your usual standard."

He took the corner too cleanly, too fast, then corrected it. The chassis settled under him. He kept his eyes on the lane line ahead, white paint shining with rain.

"What do you need?" he said.

Nina was quiet for a moment. When she spoke again her tone had changed to polished surface with business underneath. "I need Thursday afternoon off the books. Friday morning as well. Possibly Sunday. Chambers, storage, one stop in Notting Hill, and one place in Chelsea Oscar does not need itemized. I could use Mercer and get a different driver every time, but I'd rather not have my life distributed in fragments to men who still call women mad when they mean inconvenient."

"Mercer logs the car."

"Mercer logs what it's told to log."

He said nothing.

"Don't give me the little moral silence now, Ben. You abandoned that months ago."

The words hit cleanly because they were true enough to bruise. He drove through them.

Nina bent, slid her hand into the side pocket of her box, and came up with a cream envelope folded once. It had no monogram and no seal, only weight. She leaned forward between the seats and held it out. Her perfume reached him first, the dry expensive amber of it, then the paper touching the back of his hand because he had not taken it fast enough.

"What's this?"

"An advance."

"For what?"

"For the week. For your discretion. For the fact that poor people are always forced to solve rich people's timing problems." She put the envelope on the centre console instead of back in her lap. "Use the phrase that lets you sleep. Fee. Hazard pay. Seasonal bonus. I don't care."

He glanced down once. It was thick, more than her usual folded twenties.

"I can't take that."

"Of course you can." She gave a short laugh. "You can take money very well. That's part of your charm."

He felt the heat rise up his neck, a dull, angry humiliation. "I mean that much."

"Yes. I know what you meant." Her voice stayed calm. "Take it anyway."

He did not move. The envelope sat between the seats like another passenger.

Nina looked out at the wet traffic, then back at him in the mirror. "Keene told me she has to clear the attic room by Sunday. She wasn't gossiping. She wanted me to intervene, which was touching and idiotic. I'm not going to win a domestic battle with Oscar in the week he's trying to save face for the gala. But I can spare you the expression you're wearing."

"What expression?"

"The one men get when they're counting a woman's problem in banknotes."

He wished she would slap him instead. It would have been cleaner.

"You don't know anything about it," he said.

"I know enough." She nudged the envelope with one finger. Her nail was bare. He noticed that because usually it was painted. "And I know you'll come if I ask. You may as well be paid in a way that does something useful."

"Why?"

"Because I need you, and because I'm not sentimental enough to pretend need is noble." She looked down at the taped box on the seat beside her. "Also because I'm in no mood to watch Oscar's efficiencies turn the whole house Presbyterian."

There it was, one of her stupid good lines, the kind that would have made another man smile. Beni kept his face still.

"Thursday, Friday, maybe Sunday," she said. "After that the gala swallows everything and we can both consider ourselves temporarily released from each other. Doesn't that sound healthy?"

The phrase temporarily released made them sound like reasonable adults ending an arrangement with good administration. As if there had been any arrangement to end except her wanting and him not stopping.

He thought of Eszter in the kitchen with the wet labels curling off jam jars. Two hundred and forty by Friday. Boots for Lili. Oyster fare. He thought of his own wallet, the sixty-three pounds, the ten for diesel, the useless lucky fiver. He thought of Laci's face if rent was late again. He thought of how easy it would be to move numbers around in his head until none of them belonged to the place they came from.

If he took the envelope, it was only for the drives. If he used some of it for Eszter, he could replace it after next week's Mercer wages. If he replaced it before Christmas, then what had really happened? A bridge, that was all. A temporary bridge over a bad week. It was not betrayal if he told Eszter about Nina later, properly, when there wasn't a room and a deadline sitting on the counter between them. No one needed the full truth while the floor was moving. Full truth was a luxury item. He could bring it once the urgent bills were paid.

He heard himself say, "If I take it, it's for the work."

Nina's mouth moved. Not quite a smile. "If that makes you feel cleaner, by all means."

He picked up the envelope and slid it into the inside pocket of his coat. The paper was warm from her hand.

"Good," she said. "Now take me to Lincoln's Inn before my solicitor decides I'm dramatic for being late to my own humiliation."

He drove south and west through traffic thickening into early dark. At the lights near Holborn she opened the first box again and took out the face cream pot, turned it in her fingers, then put it back. By the time he stopped outside chambers, she had her expression set for old carpet, old money, and men who billed in six-minute units.

"Tomorrow at half two," she said before getting out. "And Ben."

He looked back.

"Don't make this tedious by growing a conscience only where money is concerned."

She shut the door without waiting for an answer.

He sat outside the chambers for three minutes after she disappeared inside, engine idling low. Rain ticked on the roof. Somewhere to his left a bus exhaled at the kerb. He took the envelope out and counted in the dim cabin light.

There were six hundred pounds in it: twelve fifties, crisp enough to rasp.

He counted again because the first time had felt like theft and the second time felt like accounting.

* * *

That night in Tottenham, Laci was in the shower singing badly with the radio through the bathroom door, not because he was cheerful but because he was always louder when he was tired. The flat smelled of damp plaster, frying onions from downstairs, and the citrus cleaner somebody had used in the hall to no effect at all. Beni sat on the edge of his foldout bed with the envelope open on the blanket and his own cash laid beside it in a smaller, meaner stack.

He had sixty-three from his wallet and another forty in the tobacco tin under his spare socks. He left the lucky fiver where it was, not from superstition but because some stupid part of him wanted one untouched thing.

He made three piles: Nina's fifties, his twenties and tens, and the money he owed Laci, which at this point was not a pile so much as an accusation extending into the future. The room shuddered once as a bus went by outside.

If he gave Eszter all Nina's notes, even he would be sick looking at them. They were too crisp and too easy, rich money that had never been sweated into shape. So he split them, changed one fifty at the corner shop for smaller notes, bought cigarettes he did not want just to make the exchange look ordinary, then came back and mixed everything together on the bed, with twenties against fifties and tens against twenties, his own worn notes against Nina's clean paper. He told himself he was making the loan honest by dilution.

By the time Laci came out of the shower in his boxers, towelling his hair with one hand, the money was in a plain white envelope from the Chinese takeaway drawer.

Laci nodded at it. "What's that?"

"Nothing."

"That usually means cash."

Beni slid the envelope under his thigh. "Private runs."

Laci snorted. Water still ran down his shoulders and into the waistband of his boxers. He smelled of cheap shower gel and plaster dust that never fully left him. "Then stop staring at it like it called you a cunt."

He went to the kitchenette corner, opened the fridge, and swore at its contents in Hungarian. Beni was grateful. Anger about old cheese and someone else's lager cost less than other kinds.

When Eszter texted just after nine, she did not write anything soft. `Can you come tomorrow after shift? Need help carrying one suitcase.`

He wrote back, `Yes.`

Then, after a minute: `I'll bring tape if you need it.`

Her answer came at once. `Bring shoulders. I have tape.`

The next day the attic room over the Ashdowns' top floor had already begun to stop being hers. That was the ugliest part. The room had always been narrow and mean, a space under the eaves with a single bed, a wardrobe that smelled faintly of cedar blocks, and one sash window that rattled in wind from the square. The radiator banged like a trapped ghost every time it came on. But until now it had contained the shape of Eszter's life in London: two paperbacks in Hungarian, a jar of paprika wrapped in a sock so it would not stain, a photograph of Lili in a yellow raincoat held to the mirror frame by a supermarket receipt, nail scissors, remittance stubs clipped together with a blue hair grip, and nothing decorative except a tea towel from Debrecen with geese stitched on it in red thread and hung over the chair back as if that made the chair less borrowed.

Now half of it was in open bags on the floor.

Eszter was kneeling beside the bed folding T-shirts with the brutal speed of somebody who knew delay only made leaving last longer. She had changed out of her work clothes into leggings and an old sweatshirt with the logo of a cleaning company she no longer worked for. There was dust in the loose hair at the back of her neck.

"You weren't joking about the suitcase," Beni said.

"I'm very funny under pressure. It just doesn't always travel."

He shut the door behind him. The room felt even smaller with both of them in it. Under the sloped ceiling he had to angle one shoulder without thinking. A roll of bin bags, a packet of zip ties, and a bottle of cheap hand cream sat on the bedspread in a tidy line, as if organization itself might count as backup.

"What time do you need to be out?"

"Sunday afternoon. But Keene wants the room stripped tomorrow so the florist can stack boxes in here next week. Imagine dying for lilies and then ending up above a woman from Szeged." She stuffed socks into the side pocket of the suitcase. "Take the blue bag after this. The church room is available from tomorrow night."

"You've got it, then."

"If I turn up with the money and if the woman likes my face." Eszter stood and reached for the wardrobe rail. "Her name is Mrs. Varga, which I told Marika is already suspiciously intimate."

A smile tugged once at his mouth. "Maybe she's family."

"Then she definitely won't give me a discount."

She pulled down two blouses on wire hangers and laid them on the bed. Her movements were efficient, but her mouth had gone thin with tiredness. There were purple half-moons under her eyes. She had put on lip balm at some point and forgotten to rub it in fully; the shine made the split in her lower lip more visible. The room smelled of dust, old radiator paint, and the almond hand cream she used on cracked skin. It tightened something in his chest. Everything here was small enough to carry, which felt like its own insult.

"How much exactly?" he asked.

"Don't start."

"I'm asking."

Eszter turned, one hanger in each hand. "Two forty to hold the room. Another thirty or so in fares until next pay. I can float the rest if I eat like a monk and Lili doesn't suddenly need another winter organ."

"And the money home?"

"That is the rest."

He took the white envelope from inside his coat and put it on the bed between the folded shirts. The mattress gave under it with a tired little dip. Once the paper touched her blanket, the lie stopped being numbers in his head and became an object in her room.

Eszter looked at it, then at him. "No."

"Just listen."

"No."

"Eszter."

"Don't 'Eszter' me like I'm being unreasonable about free money appearing from a nineteen-year-old driver who still owes his cousin rent."

Heat climbed up his neck. Nineteen sounded younger in her mouth, like he'd come upstairs in a borrowed suit to play lender for an afternoon.

There it was, exact as a knife. He could not even resent it because it was true. He kept his hand on the envelope.

"It's not free," he said. "It's a loan."

"From what?"

"Private runs. Nina's got extra things before the gala."

She laughed once, hard and unbelieving. "Yes, and I'm the Queen."

"I'm serious."

"So am I. You don't have three hundred pounds to lend me unless you robbed a bank with a very stupid mask."

"It's from work."

"From Mercer?"

"From Nina."

That made her stop. She did not soften; she just stopped and thought. "What kind of work?"

He kept his face steady and hated that the skill had become easy. "Off-book errands. Same as before. She pays cash when she wants things done quietly."

"Quietly." Eszter looked at the envelope again. "I don't like that woman buying pieces of my week."

"She's not. I'm lending it."

"From her."

"From me. I pay it back when Mercer clears next week."

"And if Mercer doesn't."

"It will."

"You don't know that."

"I know enough."

She folded one of the blouses over the other, buying time by making them align. When she was frightened she reached for straight edges. He had seen that in the kitchen with jars, with cutlery, with a tray of washed grapes she had once sorted by the ones already splitting. Now she squared the blouse cuffs until they matched exactly.

"I don't want to owe Nina Ashdown oxygen," she said.

"Then don't. Owe me for a week and call me an idiot later."

"I already call you an idiot now. That's not incentive."

The word please pressed against his teeth. He would not lay that on the bed beside Nina's money. Instead he opened the envelope and counted onto the bedspread: four fifties, four twenties, two tens. Three hundred, in mixed notes that no longer advertised where they had begun. He pushed them toward her in a small untidy stack.

"Take what you need," he said.

Eszter stared at the money. The room was so quiet he could hear the radiator knock in the wall and the distant vacuum cleaner from somewhere below. She touched the top note with one finger and pulled her hand back.

"You'll need this yourself."

"Not by Friday."

"Laci will still be Laci by Friday."

"He can wait a week."

"Can he?"

"He'll have to."

That landed because it was one of the few hard things Beni could say and make sound true. Eszter sat down on the bed beside the pile of notes and rubbed at the bridge of her nose with thumb and forefinger.

"I hate this," she said.

"I know."

"No, you don't. I hate needing anything from anyone in this city. It makes me feel like my skin belongs to other people."

He sat beside her, careful not to crowd. "Then take the room. Pay me back when you can. Hate that separately."

She looked at him sideways. "That is almost clever."

"Almost."

"Still annoying."

She counted out two hundred and forty with the speed of someone who had done this with worse money in worse rooms. Four fifties. Two twenties. She left the last two twenties and both tens on the bedspread.

"Take the rest back," she said.

"Keep it for fares."

"I said fares were thirty. I'm not suddenly buying truffles because you got dramatic."

"Eszter."

"Beni."

He let it go. She took one of the tens after a second and tucked it under the others. It made two hundred and fifty, enough for the room and a little breathing space, not enough to feel like rescue.

"I'll give it back after Christmas," she said.

"Whenever."

"No. After Christmas. I don't want this sitting between us longer than that."

He heard the phrase and nearly answered too fast. Sitting between us. As if there were only the money there.

She took the notes and slid them into a brown envelope already addressed in her own hand to Mrs. Varga, Holloway Road. On the back she had written `room + key` in block capitals. Then she tucked that envelope into the zip pocket of her passport holder, behind the photograph of Lili in the yellow raincoat. Beni watched Nina's money disappear behind Eszter's daughter's face.

For a second he felt almost relieved. It had become practical now, filed and directed instead of being dirty paper in a man's coat. Room plus key made it look like something solvable.

Eszter let out a breath and looked around the half-emptied room as if seeing it properly for the first time. "All right," she said. "Now lift the suitcase before I decide to burn the house down on principle."

He took the case. Heavy with books and shoes and the dense ordinary things that made a life weigh more than it looked. They carried it down the back stairs together, bumping once at the turn because the wheel caught on the strip of brass. No one saw them until the basement, where Mrs. Keene was pinning new menus to the service board with the focused misery of a woman who knew exactly how many canapes were still required in a crisis.

Her eyes went to the suitcase and away again. "Leave it by the laundry door," she said. Then, lower, to Eszter, "I spoke to Marika. The room is decent."

Eszter nodded. "Thank you."

Mrs. Keene pushed the pin in harder than necessary. "I'm sorry about this."

It was the first thing all day that sounded like apology and not procedure.

They took the blue bag out to Beni's car by the mews. Rain had stopped, but the air still held it. The stone underfoot gave back a weak damp shine. Somewhere nearby somebody was testing Christmas lights and failing; a relay clicked on and off behind a wall.

Eszter stood with her arms folded while he put the bag in the boot. Without the kitchen around her she looked smaller, though he knew she wasn't. Just more exposed. The cold had reddened the tip of her nose.

"Tomorrow after your shift?" he said. "I'll take the rest."

"You don't have to."

"I know."

She nodded once. Then she stepped forward, hooked two fingers into the front of his coat, and pulled him down enough to kiss him. Her mouth was cool from the air, then warmer. She tasted of tea and the mint gum she chewed on long cleaning days. He put his free hand on the side of her neck and kissed her back with more hunger than the mews deserved.

The remaining fifty from the white envelope was still in his inside pocket. When Eszter pressed close, the folded notes made a hard edge against his chest, square and unmistakable. He felt it between them while he kissed her. He could not stop feeling it. Fifty wasn't the rest, and even with her mouth on his he was already counting the other thirty he would have to find before tomorrow. By the time she let him go, the help had already gone where it needed to go, and there was no clean way to tell himself it had not brought something else with it.


# Chapter 14

The school let out into sleet so fine it only became weather when it hit glass. Until then it looked like dirty light. Beni sat with the heater low and watched girls in navy coats come through the gate in bursts, expensive and cold in the same way, their shoes dark with the wet. The car smelled of lemon polish and old damp wool. He had been thinking about the basement kitchen since noon: the radiator under the table, the dented kettle, the way Eszter leaned her hip against the counter when she was too tired to hide it. By then he had eighty pounds less in his wallet than the night before. He could still see the envelope going soft where sleet had hit it, Eszter knocking it once back against his coat with the heel of her hand before the stacked edge of twenties showed at the flap. Then she took it by one corner like it might leave grease, and the relief that crossed her face felt dirtier than the cash.

Bea came out late, alone, carrying her blazer instead of wearing it. One lace had slipped out of the top eyelet of her left shoe and dragged black with road grit. There was a fountain-pen stain along the side of her middle finger as if she had spent all afternoon pressing too hard. She saw him and did not raise a hand. She came straight to the car, opened the passenger door, and got in next to him instead of taking the back.

He looked at her once. "Everything all right?"

"Fine."

Her bag was still on the pavement. He leaned across to unlock the rear door, but she did not move.

"Your bag," he said.

She looked through the windscreen as if it belonged to someone else. "You can get it."

He got out into the sleet, took the bag from beside the kerb, and put it in the back. By the time he slid in again the edge of his cuff was wet through. Bea had both hands under her thighs, pinning them there.

"Home?" he asked.

"Obviously."

He pulled away from the kerb. Traffic on the school road was bad in the ordinary afternoon way, mothers stopping where they were not meant to stop, a white van backing half onto the pavement, a bus breathing diesel into the wet. Bea sat with her face turned toward the side window. Now and then she picked at the torn skin beside her thumbnail with the nail of her index finger, fast and neat, like she did not want even that much movement noticed.

At the end of the road she said, "Is Eszter in today?"

The question was so sudden he almost missed the light changing. He took the turn late and felt the tyres complain softly on the paint.

"She was in this morning," he said.

"So yes."

"Probably."

Bea gave a small shrug that was not agreement with anything. "Good."

He took Sloane Street because Eaton Square was choking again. In the mirror he could see the school gate fade off the wet back glass in pale bars. Bea kept her eyes on the window and he kept his on the road, but the car had changed shape with her in the front. Usually she spent the school run in the back seat, shoe against the opposite leather, saying almost nothing until she suddenly said something exact and unfriendly about one of her parents. Today she had put herself next to him like someone who had come to collect a debt.

At Belgravia the house stood in the same clean block of stone and black railings it always did, but the winter cuts were showing if you knew where to look. No fresh arrangement in the lower hall window, only the old lilies opening too far. Fewer delivery crates by the service step. The brass on the mews gate polished to the same shine as before, because rich houses would starve the kitchen before they let the metal look poor.

Bea was out of the car before he had killed the engine. She took her blazer, left the bag, and went through the side entrance without waiting for him.

"Your bag," he called.

She did not turn around. "Bring it."

He took it from the back seat. It was heavier than it looked, books and what felt like trainers, maybe a laptop. When he came in through the service door the house was in the half-hour between school return and evening performance, before coats were dried and before voices from upstairs had settled into dinner tones. Somewhere above him a vacuum cleaner stopped short. The air smelled of radiator heat, polish, and onions gone sweet in butter.

He knew Bea had gone down because the front hall was empty and the service stair door had not latched properly behind her. It stood open on the rubber strip by a thumb's width. He told himself he was only taking the bag after her. Then halfway down he heard his own name.

He stopped so hard the bag swung against his leg.

"It was him," Bea said. Her voice had gone flatter than in the car. "I know what he looks like."

The basement kitchen door was open just enough to show a strip of yellow tile and the side of the old pine table. Eszter's voice came back lower, not soothing, only exact.

"Start at the beginning."

Beni should have gone in. He knew that even as he stayed where he was with the wet bag strap cutting into his palm. He could see Eszter now through the gap, standing by the counter with a tea towel in both hands, twisting and untwisting it by the corners. Bea sat at the table in her shirt sleeves with her chin lifted as if she hated the shape her own mouth would have to make.

"The gallery night," Bea said. "The one where she came home late and Dad was pretending not to wait up."

Eszter did not speak.

"I came back through the mews because I wasn't in the mood for the front door and the hall lamps and my father pretending he'd just happened to be reading in a suit at midnight." Bea rubbed the pen stain on her finger with her thumb and only made it darker. "The garage door wasn't shut all the way. I saw the car in there."

Beni felt the cold from the stair rail through his sleeve.

"I saw my mother's shoe on the floor," Bea said. "Just one. I saw your tie hanging off the handbrake. And I saw enough through the glass to know no one was discussing parking."

Eszter looked at the table, not at Bea. "You saw his face."

"Yes."

"And hers."

Bea gave a humorless little breath. "Unless you've got another tall blond woman in that garage who wears La Mer and lies for sport, yes."

Eszter set the tea towel down. Beni knew that movement. It meant she was making herself cleaner before touching something filthy.

"Why didn't you say anything then?" she asked.

"Because I wasn't going to be the child who tells on her mother for getting fingered in the car."

The words hit the air and stayed there. Beni closed his hand harder on the bag strap. It dug into his skin through the wool of his glove.

Bea was looking at the table too now, breathing through her mouth as if the room smelled wrong. "And because I thought maybe it had been a one-off and I didn't want to hear about adult loneliness for three hours. But then it kept being him."

Eszter's face had not changed. That made it worse.

"How?" she said.

"Timings. Stupid little things." Bea pushed at the mug in front of her without drinking. "My mother's schedule started bending around him. She'd say pilates in Chelsea and he'd still be in the mews after. She'd say lunch in Mayfair and he wouldn't get sent off for me till twenty minutes late, then she'd come downstairs with her lipstick redone and pretend the whole day had been impossible. She never remembers anybody's name downstairs, but she remembered his from the first week. She asked for him in front of dispatch on speaker once. Twice, maybe."

The bag felt heavier by the second. Beni set it very carefully on the stair.

"And?" Eszter said.

Bea lifted one shoulder. "And I live here. I know what the house sounds like when everyone's lying. It's practically the family instrument."

Eszter's mouth moved, not quite a smile and not kind enough to become one. "That's not evidence."

"No," Bea said. "The evidence was later."

She wiped her stained finger on the inside hem of her shirt. It left a faint dark line.

"Last week," she said, "I came down the service stairs because I could hear Mum shouting at someone on the phone upstairs and I wanted toast without getting any of her on me. He was by the bins with you. He gave you an envelope." She nodded toward the stair without seeing Beni there. "You looked like you were trying not to take it and also like you needed whatever was in it badly enough to hate needing it. That was revolting, so I started paying attention to every single thing."

Eszter was very still.

"You think that envelope came from my mother," she said.

"I think," Bea said, "that if someone lies to you for that long, they usually don't stop at one subject."

Beni stepped into the doorway because staying outside had become more cowardly than entering.

Bea looked at him once, with no surprise at all. "There you are."

Eszter turned. Hard recognition crossed her face, as if a sum she had been checking in her head had finally balanced the wrong way.

He put Bea's bag down by the table. "You shouldn't be doing this downstairs."

Bea blinked. "That's really your line?"

"Bea," he said, and the name came out rougher than he meant. "You don't know all of it."

"Don't insult me." Her voice stayed quiet. "I know enough."

Eszter looked from one of them to the other. "Did it happen?"

Beni felt the kitchen grow small around the old smells of fried onion, bleach, and damp wool steaming by the radiator. He could hear the tick of the cheap wall clock over the freezer. He looked at Bea because Eszter was harder to look at and that too was a kind of answer.

Bea said, "Right."

"Beni." Eszter did not raise her voice. "Did it happen."

He put his wet hands flat on the back of the chair nearest the door. The varnish was worn away at the top rail where a hundred other hands had gripped it. "Yes."

Nothing broke. No one shouted. From upstairs came the brief thud of a door and then the house settled again around their silence.

Bea got up so suddenly her chair legs skidded on the tile. She shoved them back with her knee and reached for her bag.

"I wasn't telling for fun," she said, not looking at Beni. She was talking to Eszter, but the edge of it was for both of them. "Dad would turn it into payroll. Mum would make it about whether she's still wanted. I told you because downstairs is the only place in this house where people still talk like things happened in a real order."

Eszter nodded once.

"Go upstairs," she said. "Do your work. And if either of them asks why you were down here, say you wanted toast."

Bea pulled on her blazer without sliding her arms properly into it first. "I'm sorry," she said, and the apology sounded like something she was ashamed to own. Then she looked at Beni. "I'm not doing witness protection for either of you."

She went out past him. On the stair she stopped just long enough to say, without turning around, "I didn't tell him. Or her. Yet."

Then she was gone.

Beni stayed by the chair. Eszter picked up the tea towel again and folded it into a square so precise it looked sarcastic.

"Say something," he said.

"Not while the girl is still on the stairs."

"Eszter."

She pointed with the folded towel toward the door, not at him, at the space beside him. "Go and do your job. You can come back when the house quiets down and tell me exactly how stupid you think I am."

He wanted to cross the room, touch her wrist, force it into the shape of the afternoon before this one. Instead he said, "I never thought that."

She gave him a look so clean it made his neck burn. "Then you weren't thinking at all."

He left because there was nothing else that did not make him smaller.

In the mews the sleet had thickened into rain. The car roof clicked as it cooled. Beni sat in the driver's seat with both hands on the wheel and watched a drop crawl sideways across the passenger window where Bea's shoulder had leaned. He could still hear her voice in the kitchen, not loud, not dramatic, only exact. That was what made it fatal. If she had been hysterical he could have defended himself against that. Facts were harder. They sat there with one shoe on the garage floor and an envelope by the bins and his own silence in the middle of them.

He took his wallet out and looked at the notes still left from Nina's cash, folded by size, damp at one corner from the day it had rained through his coat. There were one hundred and twenty pounds left: too little to fix anything, enough to have poisoned more than one room.

He put it back.

The house sent him nowhere for almost an hour. A delivery van came and went with flowers that looked cheaper than usual but were trying hard not to. A boy from the butcher stood in the rain with two crates balanced on his thigh and swore softly into his scarf when no one buzzed him in fast enough. Beni should have been grateful for the wait because it meant he did not yet have to go downstairs again, but all waiting had shape, and this waiting had Eszter in it. He kept seeing the way she had set the towel down before she asked Bea for sequence and detail, like a surgeon clearing space.

At half six Nina came out by the side entrance in a charcoal coat with the belt hanging untied. She had one earring in and the other in her hand. The missing one made her look younger and more dangerous in the way unfinished rich women sometimes did, as if disorder were another accessory they could afford. She slid into the back seat without looking at him.

"Pimlico first," she said. "Then Sloane Street. Oscar's dining with some carrion banker and I'd rather be dead than sit through it."

Beni pulled out of the mews.

In the mirror he saw her fit the earring into place by touch alone. Her face in the glass was pale with the day's makeup rubbed thin under the eyes. She smelled of cold cream over perfume.

"You look grim," she said after a minute. "Has Mercer finally discovered you have a personality?"

"Long day," he said.

"Everyone's having one." She snapped the compact shut and let it fall into her bag. "My daughter isn't speaking to me, which is either developmental or deserved, and the house now buys coffee that tastes like boiled envelopes. Oscar says we're economizing for a quarter. In this house that means the flowers suffer before the men do."

Beni kept his eyes on the road. Pimlico Road was wet black under the lamps. Cyclists flashed by with plastic over their backpacks and red lights trembling on puddles.

"Did Bea say why?" he asked before he could stop himself.

Nina gave a short laugh. "Why she isn't speaking to me? Because she's seventeen. Because she thinks my existence is an act aimed specifically at her nerves. Because she's not wrong." She leaned her head back. "I assume she'll recover in time for university applications."

Beni said nothing.

At a red light Nina leaned forward a little, close enough for him to smell the mint on her tongue. "Whatever is wrong," she said, "don't bring it into the house in your face. Oscar notices moods when he can't find numbers."

Then she sat back again, and that was all. He drove her to Pimlico, waited, drove her to Sloane Street, waited, drove her home. She made two calls in the back seat and talked about caterers and one donor's impossible husband and whether there was still decent Champagne in the locked cupboard. She kept talking as if the floor under her feet were still level. If she felt any shift at all, she gave him no sign of it and seemed to trust the house to swallow the rest before it reached the drawing room.

Back in the mews, she stayed in the back seat a second longer than necessary after the engine died.

"Tomorrow eight fifteen," she said. "And don't wear that tie. It makes you look like you're going to your own funeral."

She got out and went in through the side door with her coat open to the rain.

Beni sat until the courtesy lights went out. Then he took the tie from his collar, stuffed it into the glove box, and went downstairs.

The kitchen had been cleaned to evening order. The pans were racked. The counters were wiped. The smell now was dishwasher steam, stock, and the bitter edge of over-steeped tea. Eszter stood at the table with her own coat on and a net shopping bag looped over one wrist. Her hair was still tied back but the short pieces at the nape had worked loose in the damp. Beside her on the table sat his flat key on the cheap red plastic ring from the corner locksmith, the one she had once laughed at because it made even a London key look temporary.

"Close the door," she said.

He did.

For a moment neither of them moved. The freezer hummed. Somewhere in the wall a pipe knocked twice and went quiet.

Eszter touched the key ring with one finger and then took her hand away. "How long."

He knew better than to pretend not to understand. "Since the gallery night."

"Not with dates. I'm not the police." Her voice was level and tired and much worse than if she had shouted. "How long while you were with me."

"From before," he said. "Then during."

"During what."

"Us."

She let out a breath through her nose. "You're nineteen, but you're not so nineteen that words don't exist."

"While I was with you."

"Good." She nodded once. "Better."

He stood by the door with his hands empty. He did not sit because sitting would have meant pretending to belong in the room.

"It wasn't all the time," he said. "It wasn't every day."

Eszter looked at him with plain contempt. "What a beautiful defense."

"I'm trying to tell you straight."

"Then do it straight."

He swallowed. The tea smell in the room had gone stale. "She started it. After the gallery."

"Did she hold a gun to your head."

"No."

"Did she climb into bed with you at Tottenham when you were asleep."

"No."

"Then start again."

He rubbed his thumb along the seam of his palm where the steering wheel leather always left a faint tackiness after a long shift. "I went with it. I kept going with it. I should've stopped."

"Before me."

"Yes."

"And after me."

"Yes."

She nodded again. Every answer seemed to make her cleaner, more exact, while he felt himself get blurred around the edges.

"Was it still happening when you came to me on Sunday," she asked, "after the market, after you told me about that woman in Hungary, after you put your mouth everywhere and let me think that whatever was wrong with you had at least one honest corner left."

Beni looked at the table because the key ring's cheap red plastic was easier than her face. "Yes."

Eszter laughed once, a brief snag of sound like a knife catching on bone.

"Fine," she said. "Now the money."

He looked up.

"Don't do that." Her eyes hardened. "The girl did not invent the envelope. Was that money from her."

He should have answered at once. The pause made the room tilt.

"Some of it," he said.

"How much."

"Most."

She put both hands flat on the table. The shopping bag swung off her wrist and bumped her coat. Inside it he could see leeks, a loaf of sliced bread, a tub of cheap yogurt, practical things for one or two days at most.

"You gave me her money."

"I gave you money because you needed it."

"From her."

"I could pay some back from wages."
