Eszter stared at him as if he had started speaking through the wrong end of his body. "You think this is about bookkeeping."

"No."

"Then why are you talking like a receipt."

He dragged a hand over his mouth. The skin there still tasted faintly of rain. "I was trying to help."

"No," she said. "You were trying to buy a version of yourself you could stand."

He had no answer fast enough to stop her.

"If you wanted to fuck the lady of the house, that's ugly and stupid and very ordinary," she said. Her voice remained low, but each word arrived with the neat force of a plate set down exactly where it belonged. "I'm not seventeen. I know rich people get bored and poor men get used. I know desire doesn't wait for moral paperwork. But you don't take the money from that lie and put it in my hand like you're saving me."

"I wasn't mocking you."

"You think humiliation only counts if someone laughs?"

He shut his eyes for a second. The kitchen light showed red through the lids. "No."

"I told you things I don't tell men who haven't earned them. About Lili. About my room. About why I left home. I took your key." She touched the key ring again, the smallest movement. "Do you understand what that is in my life. How little room I've got to be stupid."

He saw it then not as a sentence but as objects: her folded clothes in the church-basement room, her daughter's photo tucked into the mirror frame, rent counted to the pound, the envelope from him in among it all with its mouth still open like a smear.

"I know," he said.

"No." She shook her head. "If you knew, you wouldn't have done this part."

He stepped away from the door before he meant to. "Eszter, I wanted to stop. With her. I kept thinking I would, then the cuts came and you were losing the room and I thought if I could fix one thing first"

"Fix." She said the word softly, almost curious. "You fixed me with her money."

"It wasn't like that."

"Wasn't it." She straightened. "Tell me where the room came from then. Tell me what paid for the bread I bought yesterday."

He opened his mouth and closed it. There was no sentence that did not put Nina's hand directly in Eszter's shopping bag.

"Exactly," she said.

The refrigerator motor kicked on behind him. It made the silence feel mechanical.

"I would've helped you anyway," he said finally, hating how thin it sounded.

"With what." Eszter's eyes flashed once. "Your good intentions. Your sad face. Twenty pounds of honesty after the fact."

He looked at the shopping bag again because he could not stop looking at what life cost in her hands. The leeks had dirt still trapped high in the stems.

"I loved being with you," he said.

Her mouth changed at that, but not toward softness. "That may even be true."

"It is."

"Don't." She held up one hand. "Don't give me nice clean sentences now. You don't get to climb out through language."

He swallowed whatever else had been coming.

"Listen to me," she said. "The sex isn't the whole insult. The lie isn't even the whole insult. The worst part is that you stood in this kitchen and watched me thank you for money that came from the woman paying you to keep your mouth shut while you fucked her. You let me feel relieved. Grateful. That's filthier than the rest."

He had nothing to put against that. He remembered the exact moment now: the paper gone damp in his glove, Eszter's first refusal quick and hard, the back of her fingers knocking the envelope against his chest. Then the flap had lifted and shown the stacked edge of twenties. She took it without looking at him and pressed the wet corner flat with her thumb as if tidying it could make the money less intimate. She said thank you too fast, like the word had to get out before it turned into pleading. His relief had come before his shame. That was the part he could not forgive.

"I'm sorry," he said, and heard how inadequate it was even as it left him.

"I know." She sounded almost bored by it. "That's not a tool."

She picked up the key and pushed it across the table. It slid over old knife marks and stopped near his side.

"I'm not coming to Tottenham," she said. "Not tomorrow, not after Christmas, not because you miss me, not because you finally tell me the whole truth when the whole truth is all that's left. You keep your room."

He did not move toward the key.

"Take it," she said.

He took it. The red plastic was warm from her fingers.

"And hear this properly. If I'm on shift here, you don't come downstairs unless the house calls you. You don't come for tea. You don't come because you're lonely. You don't come because you think looking wrecked is a kind of apology. You wait where the other drivers wait."

"Eszter."

"No." She hoisted the shopping bag higher on her wrist. "You had the kitchen because I opened it. That's finished."

He felt that more sharply than he had felt the key. The kitchen had been a room with steam on the windows and food in foil and her voice crossing from Hungarian to English and back. It had been somewhere he could arrive without being announced. Hearing her remove it from him in a sentence made the tiled floor look suddenly official, as if he had been trespassing there all along and only just got told.

"Is there anything," he began, then stopped because he heard himself asking for work.

Eszter saved him from finishing. "There's the girl," she said. "If she needs looking after in a practical way, that's separate. But you and me are done. I don't have enough room in my life for this kind of dirt."

She walked to the door. When she passed him he smelled hand cream under bleach, the ordinary smell of her after a shift, and the familiarity of it was brutal.

At the threshold she stopped.

"One more thing," she said without turning around. "Don't make me comfort you about this."

Then she went out.

Beni stayed in the kitchen until the door swung shut behind her. He could hear her steps on the service stairs, quick and even, then nothing. The freezer hummed. Water ticked somewhere into the trap under the sink. On the counter by the bread bin sat the mug Bea had left earlier, tea skin dried across the top in a pale wrinkled film. Beni looked at it and had the absurd urge to wash it, as if labor done neatly enough might still count for anything.

He put the key in his pocket and left the mug where it was.

Upstairs, the house had turned toward dinner. A radio murmured from a room off the back corridor. Someone laughed once, then stopped as if remembering where they were. Beni went to the service landing by habit more than intention an hour later, after Nina had asked for a car blanket and then changed her mind, after Oscar's assistant had sent down a list for the next day's flowers, after he had stood in the mews long enough for the cold to soak through the soles of his shoes. His body still knew the route to the basement kitchen better than most routes in London: left at the laundry chute, down the narrow stair, hand to the rail where the varnish had worn away.

Tonight the door at the bottom stood shut tight, a rolled tea towel pushed along the gap to keep the heat in.

He stood one step above the landing and listened.

From inside came the soft knock of plates, the hiss of the kettle, the murmur of two women talking too low for words. One of them was Eszter. He knew the weight of her voice even through a door. The other might have been the Thursday temp, the skinny Polish cleaner with violet trainers under her black work trousers and eczema cracking white across two knuckles. Last week she had shown Eszter a photo of her son in a cardboard astronaut helmet while they waited for the oven to heat. Beni had been in the room then with a plate in his hand, not family, not exactly staff, but something blurred and warm in between.

Now he was on the wrong side of the green baize.

The temp opened the door just enough to come through with a crate of folded napkins balanced on her hip. She looked up, saw him on the stair, and shifted the crate higher.

"Sorry," she said automatically, though her face said she already understood the map of the evening. "We're keeping the heat."

The sentence was so small and polite it could have passed for nothing. Beni stepped back to let her pass. She did not hold the door for him. Through the gap he saw the corner of the table, the radiator glowing under it, Eszter's coat on the chair back, and steam rising from a pot. Then the door shut again. The smell of onion and stock came out warm as breath and was gone.

He stayed on the stair with his hand on the worn rail until the wood cooled under his palm.


# Chapter 15

An hour after the Ashdowns' basement kitchen door had shut him out, the buzzer went while Beni was ironing the white shirt Mercer wanted for gala work the next day, the iron balanced on a folded towel over Laci's coffee table because the board had lost a leg in October and never been replaced. Rain ticked against the window in a thin patient way, enough to silver the glass but not enough to wash the soot off anything. The flat smelled of damp plaster, frying oil gone cold, and the lemon cleaner Laci used only when he thought the mildew had started winning. Beni set the iron upright and listened. Nobody came for him here unless they wanted money or had brought the wrong takeaway upstairs.

The buzzer went again.

He pressed the street-door release out of habit and crossed the narrow hall in his socks. Cold air came up the stairwell when he opened the flat door. Bea Ashdown stood on the landing under the weak bulb with a navy overnight bag on one shoulder and rain shining on the sleeves of her school coat. Her tie was gone, the top button of her shirt undone, and one thumbnail was half stripped of clear polish as if she had worried at it with her teeth all the way from Belgravia. She did not look frightened or theatrical. She looked as if she had missed a train and decided that was now everyone else's problem.

"Can I come in," she said, "before your cousin sees me and thinks I'm selling God?"

Beni stared at her long enough to feel the draught crawl up his bare legs. "Bea?"

"Yeah." She shifted the bag higher. "Not a ghost. Can I come in or do you need a minute?"

Leaving her on the landing was impossible. Leaving her on the pavement outside the block was worse. He stepped back. She came in without brushing him, bringing cold wool and expensive shampoo gone flat with rain. Her eyes moved once around the front room that was also his bedroom: the sofa bed folded out against the wall, the damp map blooming above the skirting board, Laci's boots under the radiator, the iron steaming faintly on the towel. She took it in with one quick sweep and did him the mercy of not pretending not to see.

"What are you doing here?" he asked.

"Not going home."

Laci came out of the kitchenette before Beni could say anything else. He had one trouser leg rolled to the knee and was re-fastening the beige ankle brace he had been wearing for weeks, the Velcro clogged with plaster dust but still catching if he yanked it hard enough. His T-shirt was inside out. He stopped when he saw Bea, then the bag, then Beni's face.

"No," he said at once. "Whatever this is, no."

"She just came," Beni said.

"I can see she just came. I can also see she's a child with luggage."

Bea gave him a tired, precise look. "I'm seventeen."

"Perfect," Laci said. "A seventeen-year-old with luggage. Even worse."

He limped the last step into the room, tea glass in one hand, and the smell of lager and mint rub came with him. Beni could feel the situation turning ugly before it had even found shape. The flat was too small for surprise. Every new fact had to sit on someone's knees.

"Laci," he said in Hungarian, low. "Just ten minutes."

"You can have eight." Laci looked at Bea again, still hard-faced, weighing trouble against trouble. "If police knock because rich people lost their daughter, I'm not learning polite English for them."

Bea hitched the overnight bag down to the floor. "I'm not missing. I know where I am."

"That helps me enormously," Laci said. "Do your parents?"

She did not answer. She had the self-control to leave the silence there instead of decorating it. That worried Beni more than tears would have.

He switched off the iron and moved it to the windowsill. "Sit down."

"On what?"

He almost laughed, which would have been the wrong sound. The only free chair had a clean shirt drying over it, and the sofa bed was unmade because it was where he slept. Bea solved it by perching on the edge of the windowside chair after lifting the shirt with two fingers and handing it to him. The bus outside ground through its gears at the stop, making the glass hum in the frame.

"How did you find this place?" he asked.

"I called Mercer and said you'd left a scarf in our car and I needed to send it back. The girl on the phone gave me the building and postcode." Bea worried at the split thumbnail with her teeth for one quick bite. "People tell polite girls stupid things."

Laci made a disgusted noise that was half admiration. "Of course they do."

Beni felt a quick stupid pulse of panic at Mercer hearing her voice, at traces he could not now control. "Why didn't you go to a friend?"

"Because my friends have mothers who lunch with my mother, or fathers who'd ring my father before I'd got my shoes off. Also because I didn't want a house in Chelsea full of people pretending this was interesting."

"And you thought this was better?"

"Not better." She glanced at the damp patch on the wall and then back at him. "More honest."

The words landed harder than if she had said something cruel. She sat very straight with the bag between her knees, one hand hooked through the strap like she expected someone to take it away. Mascara had smudged under one eye from rain and the drag of hours. He could see where she had packed in a hurry: the zipper caught on a sleeve, the side pocket bulged with charger cables and a rolled pair of socks.

"What happened?" he asked.

Bea looked at Laci, then at the hall, then back to Beni. "Do you want the short version or the version where your cousin starts charging by the minute?"

"Short," Laci said. "Definitely short."

"Tomorrow is the gala," she said. "The literacy one. Children's books, bad champagne, rich people bidding on first editions so they can feel literate in public. My father had the photographer booked before breakfast. Family on the stairs at six-thirty, then table, then auction, then whatever version of us pays best."

Laci leaned back against the kitchenette doorframe. "Rich people are disgusting."

Bea ignored him. "They want me pressed into it. On the stairs, at the table, in the photographs. Smile here, stand there, don't look like I know anything. I'm not doing it."

"So you left," Beni said.

"Yes."

"Without telling anyone."

She held his eyes. "If I'd told my father, he'd have turned it into drivers, security, and some kind of lesson. If I'd told my mother, she'd have made me carry her feelings first. I needed to get out before either of them put a hand on it."

The room felt smaller. Rain tapped the window harder for a second, then slackened. Beni thought of Oscar's voice when he was controlling himself: quieter, not louder, each word set down like paperweights. He thought of Nina making Bea's absence into fresh material for shame, fear, self-display, all of it at once. He thought of Laci, already at the edge of his tolerance, with rent numbers never far from his mouth and a four-thirty alarm for work. There was only one adult Bea trusted who was not one of her parents, and that was the woman Beni had most recently lied to, humiliated, and lost.

"Why me?" he asked.

"Because I don't know where Eszter's staying now." Bea shrugged one shoulder. "And because you'd at least open the door before ruining my evening."

Laci barked a laugh into his tea. "She has your number."

Beni ignored him. "You can't stay here."

Bea looked around the front room once more, this time without flinching. "I gathered that when I walked in."

"It's not about--"

"The damp? The sofa? Your cousin hating me? I know." She pulled at the split thumbnail. "I can sleep on the floor. I've slept on floors."

Laci made a face. "At Glastonbury, maybe. Not here."

Bea's head turned. "I haven't been to Glastonbury."

"Then congratulations on your moral purity. You're still not sleeping here."

Beni wished, briefly and uselessly, for Noor. Noor would have made three practical calls and told him what order to do the rest in. Beni only had the facts in front of him and the one behind them: Bea was seventeen, rich, angry, and in his flat. Keeping her overnight in a front room where his bed opened six feet from Laci's coat pile was not shelter. It was another kind of danger with worse paperwork.

He looked at Bea's phone on her lap. The screen was black, but he could feel it there like a lit fuse. "Do they know you're gone yet?"

"Probably not." She paused. "Maybe my mother thinks I'm at revision. Maybe my father thinks I'm upstairs being difficult. The house is busy when there's a performance coming. People only count heads when the guests matter."

That line, too old and too clear, made him cold in a place the weather had not reached.

"Did you bring money?" he asked.

Bea gave him a flat look. "A little. I'm not trying to buy your floor."

"I didn't say that."

"No, but you looked worried in a counting way."

He was worried in a counting way. He always was. He had forty-three pounds in his wallet, two twenties and three coins and a limp five he had put aside for tomorrow because gala shifts ran long and staff meals never reached the drivers. The cash Nina had given him sat hidden in a sock in his bag, contaminated even before he touched it. The night had already started costing money, and money was the easiest part of it.

Laci set the tea glass down on the counter with a click. "What is the plan?"

"I'm thinking."

"Think faster. I've got work."

"You always have work."

"Exactly. So I would like to sleep before it."

Bea looked at Beni. Under the watchfulness, there was the first edge of plain tiredness now, adolescent and unguarded. "If you call my father right away, he'll send a car and people and act like I've stolen state secrets."

"If I don't call anyone, that's worse."

"I know." Her jaw tightened. "I know. I just needed one place where nobody would start by arranging me."

Beni went into the hall with his phone and closed the front-room door most of the way behind him. The bulb over the stairs buzzed. He scrolled to Eszter's number and stopped there. Two days ago she had cut him off in the basement kitchen with a face he had not known how to answer, hard not because she was shouting but because she no longer needed anything from him. He had no right to drag her back into his mess. He had even less right to drag Bea into another bad adult choice because his pride hurt.

But the other choices were worse and getting worse by the minute.

He typed, deleted, typed again. Finally he sent: `Bea's with me. She left home. I know I've no right to ask anything. I need to bring her somewhere safe for tonight.`

The message went. No reply came.

He stood there listening to the buses shake the building and the low blur of Bea's voice inside answering something Laci had said. He could not hear the words, only the shapes. He sent a second message before he could talk himself out of it: `I'm coming to the church room unless you tell me not to.`

Still nothing.

When he went back in, Laci was standing at the window with the curtain lifted on two fingers, looking down to the street. Bea sat where he had left her, but her bag was zipped properly now, ready for movement. She read his face too fast.

"We're leaving," she said.

"Yes."

Laci dropped the curtain. "Good."

"Can I stay ten more minutes while he figures out where?" Bea asked.

"No." Laci pushed himself off the sill and went to the hook behind the door, where three umbrellas hung collapsed and leaking onto the tray. He pulled down the black one with only one broken rib and held it out to Beni without quite looking at him. "Take this. Bring it back. If that girl turns up soaked and sneezing in my hallway, I'll kill you from sheer irritation."

Beni took it. "Thanks."

"Don't thank me. I'm protecting my floorboards."

Bea got up and slung the bag over her shoulder. For the first time some awkwardness showed through, not about being there but about taking anything from anyone in it. "I'm sorry," she said to Laci, and this time it sounded less polished.

Laci shrugged as if apology was a currency too small to count. "Be interesting somewhere else."

The rain had thickened to a fine needling mist. Beni did not take Bea to the bus stop. The thought of sitting with her under fluorescent shelter glass while her phone started lighting up made his skin prickle. He used the last of his prepaid battery to call a minicab office near the station and gave the church off Seven Sisters Road as the destination. The man said ten minutes. It became fourteen.

They waited under the umbrella in front of the off-licence, the pavement slick with old flyers and a burst bin bag. Bea kept one hand in her pocket and the other on the overnight bag strap. She was not shivering yet, but the cold had sharpened her face, drawing the seventeen back into it. A strand of hair had stuck to her lip gloss and she kept blowing it away impatiently.

"What did you pack?" Beni asked, mostly because silence was making him hear all the wrong possibilities.

"Toothbrush. Knickers. A jumper. Phone charger. The ugly trainers I wear in the car if my mother makes me put on shoes that hurt." She looked down at the bag. "I left the dress."

"Good."

"You don't even know what it looked like."

"I know enough."

Headlights slid over the wet road and away again. The minicab still had not come. Bea dug at her thumbnail with the edge of a key. "Do you think I made it worse? Telling Eszter."

Beni answered too fast for it to be generous. "No."

She glanced at him. "You didn't have to say it like that."

"Like what."

"Like you were relieved someone did it for you."

He had no defence that would not sound like more of the same. The wet pavement reflected the green pharmacy cross, breaking it under passing tyres. "You weren't wrong," he said at last.

"I know I wasn't wrong." Bea shifted the bag on her shoulder. "That wasn't the question."

The cab arrived with a heater already blasting and a pine-tree air freshener swinging from the mirror so hard it clicked the windscreen. Beni gave the driver the church again, paid half up front in cash because that was the rule with some offices after midnight, and got in beside Bea in the back. The seat fabric was rough and smelled faintly of cigarettes scrubbed with lemon.

For a few streets neither of them spoke. Tottenham slid past in shuttered chicken shops, cash-and-carry light, buses with two wet passengers on the top deck. Beni watched the route out of habit even while someone else drove. Left at the junction, queue by the lights, don't take the shortcut because the roadworks would still be there. He could not stop his brain doing it. Counting roads felt cleaner than counting blame.

Bea leaned her head back and closed her eyes, giving them one minute of darkness. "If she says no, what then?"

"We'll think again."

"That means you don't know."

"Yes."

She opened her eyes. "Fair."

At the red light near Stamford Hill her phone lit inside the bag pocket, then buzzed hard enough to rattle against something metal. She froze before she touched it. On the second buzz she dragged it out and looked at the screen. The colour left her mouth.

"Your mother?" Beni asked.

"Both."

She thumbed the screen dark again without answering. The phone began vibrating once more almost immediately, the trapped-wasp sound of insistence. This time the preview flashed across the glass before she killed it: `Answer now. If I don't hear your voice in ten minutes, I call the police.` Under it came Nina's name and another message: `Please just tell me you're breathing.`

"You have to tell them you're alive," he said.

"Not yet."

"Bea."

"Not yet." She kept her voice low, but the panic in it was real now, fine as wire. "If I answer my mother she'll cry and hand him the phone. If I answer my father he'll turn tonight into an operation. I just need to get to Eszter before he starts moving pieces."

Beni looked out at the wet row of shops, the halal butcher shut behind its metal grille, the betting shop still lit. The problem had changed shape. It was no longer tomorrow's staircase and photographer. It was police, staff calls, and Oscar Ashdown making lists.

"What does he mean by police?" he asked.

Bea stared at the dark screen in her hand. "He'll ring the school, the friends he approves of, maybe the police if he thinks he can keep it private. He'll call it concern and ruin half the night doing it."

"And your mother?"

"She's sending please answer and where are you and little broken bits of herself. Then she'll put him back on. They're a relay race when there's blood in the water."

The phrase kicked a breath out of him that might have been a laugh on another night. "When we get there, you text," he said. "Straight away."

She shut her eyes once, then opened them. "All right. But only when we're there."

The driver turned the heat higher. Warm air hit their shins and brought the smell of wet wool up from Bea's coat. She folded forward, elbows on her knees, suddenly less composed. Her bag had left a damp stripe on the seat where it rested. Beni could hear her stomach make a small humiliating sound under the engine noise.

"Have you eaten?" he asked.

"At lunch."

"Only lunch?"

"There were canapes in the kitchen when I left, but I wasn't staying for donor hummus."

He took out the last coin-pound piece from his pocket, then another from the change tray built into the door, and when the driver stopped by the lights near the station he got out long enough to buy a paper cup of tea and a wrapped cheese pastry from the all-night kiosk. He came back wet at the shoulders. Bea looked at the food like she did not want to be seen wanting it.

"Take it," he said.

"I'm not going to faint dramatically if that's what you're thinking."

"I wasn't."

"You were a bit."

"Eat."

She burned her mouth on the first bite because the pastry had been sitting under heat too long and gone lava-hot at the middle. She swore, swallowed, and then laughed once at herself, small and actual. That, more than anything, made her look her age. She drank the tea too quickly and wiped her upper lip with the back of her hand.

"Did Eszter stop speaking to you?" she asked after a while.

Beni looked at the wipers working the windscreen ahead. "Yes."

"Because of me?"

"Because of me."

Bea held the cup between both hands, warming them. "I didn't tell her to be kind."

"I know."

"I told her because she was the only person in that house who ever looked at me like I wasn't furniture."

The cab took the bend too fast and the tea slopped against the lid. Beni steadied the cup without thinking. Her fingers were cold through the paper.

"She might send us away," he said.

"Probably."

"Then don't be surprised."

Bea looked out at the rain-streaked window. "I'm not here for grace."

The church sat back from the road behind a low black railing and two plane trees shedding bark in pale strips. A side light burned by the basement steps. The upper windows were dark except for one yellow square near the presbytery. Beni paid the rest of the fare, every note suddenly feeling filthy-soft from other people's hands, and got out with the umbrella and Bea's bag.

The gate squealed when he pushed it. Rainwater had gathered in the broken edges of the steps, and the basement corridor smelled of mop water, radiator dust, and the soup someone had cooked earlier for too many people. A laminated fire notice had curled away from the wall by the door. Bea stood beside him with her shoulders up around her ears and watched him knock.

No reply.

He knocked again, harder this time. The old wood carried the sound down the corridor in a way that made him feel intrusive in his bones.

"Maybe she's asleep," Bea said, quieter now.

"She won't be."

How he knew that he could not have said. Maybe it was only that Eszter was never fully off duty, no matter whose building she was in.

He heard movement at last: a chair leg, a bolt, the quick rubber squeak of slippers on lino. The door opened four inches, chain still on. One dark eye looked through the gap, then widened.

Eszter shut the door, lifted the chain, and opened it properly.

She wore thermal leggings under a long grey T-shirt and an old men's cardigan buttoned wrong, one button off all the way down. Her hair was loose and half dry, a child's pink elastic looped around her wrist for later. There was flour on one forearm. Behind her Beni saw a metal table, a hot plate, and a saucepan with the lid crooked, steam fogging the tiny window above the sink.

When she saw him her face closed. When she saw Bea beside him with the overnight bag and wet school coat, it sharpened into action, the way her whole body did when a job announced itself.

"Tell me you've got a better explanation than the one standing in front of me," she said.

"I left home," Bea said before Beni could answer. "He didn't kidnap me."

Eszter's eyes cut back to Beni. "I wasn't asking her."

"She came to my flat," Beni said. "I couldn't keep her there."

"So you brought her to mine."

"I know what that sounds like."

"Do you." She looked him up and down, rain on his shoulders, umbrella dripping onto the concrete. "That would be a first."

Bea stepped forward an inch, enough to claim the scene back. "If you want to tell me to leave, tell me. Don't do the adult thing where you speak over my head and then act like it's for me."

Eszter's gaze settled on her. "You're soaked at the hem and your lips are blue. Come inside before I decide anything."

She stood back. Bea went in. Beni followed because there was nowhere else for him to stand without becoming a weather problem.

The room was smaller than Laci's front room and cleaner by force. A narrow bed ran along one wall under a folded church blanket. Two plastic crates were stacked beneath the window, one full of clothes, the other of food and cheap toiletries. A kettle sat beside the hot plate. Above the sink a laminated picture of St. Joseph had been blu-tacked to the wall, probably by whoever used the room before. Everything had edges of use and none of it had room to pretend.

Eszter shut the door with her heel and took Bea's bag from her before she could resist. "Shoes off."

"They're fine."

"If they were fine, your socks wouldn't be making that sound."

Bea opened her mouth, then bent to unbuckle one shoe. Her fingers missed the clasp twice. On the third try the whole controlled structure of her seemed to slip half an inch. Her grip failed before the rest of her did. The metal buckle clicked uselessly against her nail.

Eszter saw it. So did Beni.

"Enough," Eszter said. She crouched, slapped Bea's hands lightly away, and undid the buckle herself with two fast movements. "Sit. Both shoes. Beni, don't stand there like a coat rack. Take this thing off her."

Beni moved at once. Bea let him pull the wet coat from her shoulders with stiff, furious compliance. Under it her school jumper was damp at the neck. Eszter set the shoes by the radiator, peeled the wet socks off, and wrapped Bea's feet in a tea towel from the counter because there was nothing else close enough.

"Did anyone hit you?" Eszter asked, already rising, already reaching for mugs.

"No."

"Did anyone touch you?"

"No."

"Good. Then this is only rich-people insanity and we can manage it."

Bea gave a broken little laugh that sounded more like her teeth had knocked together. She sat on the chair now, hands flat on her thighs as if she was being told to wait outside a headmistress's office.

Eszter pointed to the bed. "Sit properly. Not on the edge like you're at a private clinic."

Bea obeyed.

The phone started vibrating again from inside the bag Eszter had taken. The sound against the cheap metal table was louder in the small room than it had been in the cab. Bea flinched so hard the tea towel slipped off one foot.

Eszter fished the phone out, looked at the screen, and blew air through her nose. "Mum. Dad. Mum again. Christ."

"Don't answer," Bea said immediately.

"I wasn't planning to take relationship instructions from either of them." Eszter set the phone face down. "But you're sending a message."

"I don't want them here."

"Neither do I. That's why you're sending a message."

Bea's jaw set. "If my father knows I'm alive he'll send someone."

"He already will. The difference is whether he starts with police, staff, and hospitals. I'm not having half of Belgravia climb over cleaners and drivers because you wanted one dramatic night." Eszter put a mug down in front of her with more noise than necessary. "You left because they were using you as decoration. Fine. I believe you. It's still not noble to make poor people do a missing-person shift."

The words hit clean. Bea took them. Her face changed, not into obedience, but into something more honest than that. "I wasn't trying to make work for staff."

"I know. But that is what happens anyway."

For a moment nobody spoke. Water hissed at the edge of the saucepan. The little radiator knocked once like a pipe swallowing air.

Beni stood by the door with the umbrella still in his hand, ridiculous and dripping, and hated how visible his part in all this was. If Bea had not seen him with Nina in the garage, if he had not lied to Eszter, if Eszter had not been driven out of the Ashdown house and into this borrowed basement room, this scene would not exist in this shape. It would be some other damage, maybe, but not this exact one.

Eszter turned to him at last. "Well?"

"She came to me," he said.

"Yes, I heard the plot."

"Laci wouldn't keep her. I couldn't either."

"No, you definitely couldn't."

"I know."

"Stop saying you know. Tonight has too much evidence otherwise."

Bea looked from one to the other with the bleak concentration of someone who had watched adults fail for too long to be shocked by it. "I can leave if this is your hobby."

"Sit down," Eszter said, not unkindly. "My hobby is surviving."

She lifted the saucepan lid. Onion and paprika and lentils came into the room with the steam, humble and immediate. Beni had not realised until then how hungry he was as well. Eszter ladled soup into two mismatched bowls, tore bread with her hands, and shoved one bowl toward Bea and the other toward him without looking at him long enough to make it giftlike.

"Eat," she said. "Talk after."

Bea took the spoon. Her hand was still not steady. She got the first mouthful down anyway. Colour came back to her face in pieces, not all at once.

Eszter sat on the end of the bed opposite her, elbows on knees. "Now. Tell me exactly why you left."

Bea swallowed. "Because if I stayed, I'd have ended up in that dress and in those photographs."

"I know what tomorrow is. I scrubbed candle wax out of silver for it last year. Give me the part underneath."

Bea stared into the bowl. "My father wasn't asking whether I was coming. He was talking about angles. He said if the three of us held still on the stairs long enough the room would stop sniffing around the house. My mother said I owed her one evening of normality after the month she'd had. Like normality comes on a hanger."

Eszter snorted once.

"And I looked at the dress and thought if I put it on tomorrow I'd either smile for them or tell a room full of donors what kind of house they're drinking in." Bea glanced at Beni, then back at the bowl. "About my mother and Beni. About you ending up in a borrowed basement while they still send champagne up three floors. All of it. I left because I didn't trust myself not to do something I couldn't take back."

Eszter's mouth thinned. "Yes. That sounds like a real reason."

"So I packed a bag. That's it. I didn't leave a note because I didn't want to write something I'd have to mean forever. I just left."

"Where from?"

"School first. Then I went home, got the bag, and left again before supper."

"Anyone see you?"

"Probably six people. Nobody who mattered to the version of me they were watching."

Eszter let that sit a second. "You understand this still isn't clever."

"I know."

"Good. Clever is overrated. We need useful now."

She slid the phone back across the table. Bea looked at it as if it might bite.

"Write: I'm safe. I'm not hurt. I'm with Eszter tonight. Don't call the police. I'll ring Mum in five minutes, and Mrs. Keene can collect me in the morning." Eszter ticked each part on her fingers. "No address in the message. No little knife in the wording. No bait."

"You want me to say I'm with you?"

"Yes," Eszter said. "Because you are. And because if I carry the risk, they can know which woman is carrying it."

"If I say morning, they'll start negotiating."

"They can negotiate with my voicemail. Send it."

"My father will hate the don't-call-the-police part."

"Then enjoy that for three seconds and send it."

Bea let out a short breath through her nose, almost a snort. She typed, stopped, deleted something, typed again. Her thumb hovered over send. "Should I send it to both?"

"Yes," Eszter said. "Make them work equally for once."

Bea sent the message to both parents, then turned the phone face down and pushed it away as if it had become dirty. Within ten seconds it buzzed again. She didn't touch it.

Eszter held out her hand. "Now call your mother. Speaker."

"I just sent the message."

"And I just said call."

"I don't want my father on the line."

"Then get to your mother first."

Bea stared at her, then picked up the phone and called. The answer came before the first full ring had died.

"Bea?" Nina's voice came tinny and split by panic. "Jesus Christ, where are you?"

"I'm alive," Bea said. Her knuckles went white around the phone. "I'm not hurt. I'm with Eszter tonight."

Relief broke through the speaker, ugly and wet and furious all at once. "With Eszter where? Your father is about to call the police."

Eszter held out her hand. Bea gave her the phone without argument.

"She's safe," Eszter said. "She's fed, she's dry, and she's staying the night."

"You can't keep her there without telling us where that is."

"I can tell Mrs. Keene where to collect her. Eight-thirty. Church gate off Seven Sisters Road. One car, one adult, no staff circus."

"Oscar will want-"

"Oscar can want. Mrs. Keene can come. If anyone else turns up, Bea stays put."

There was a silence long enough for the radiator to knock once. Then Nina said, smaller, "All right. Mrs. Keene at eight-thirty."

"Good," Eszter said. "Tell your husband not to ring the police. She's accounted for."

Bea took the phone back. "I'm alive," she said, looking at the soup rather than the speaker. "That's all I've got tonight."

Something came back too fast and wet to make out. Bea ended the call before it could turn into pleading.

Eszter rose and went to a plastic drawer unit by the bed. She rummaged, produced an oversized charity-run T-shirt with a faded green logo and a pair of thick socks, and handed them to Bea. "Bathroom is through there. The lock sticks. If you break the handle, I'll charge your father personally."

Bea took the clothes. "Thank you."

"Don't thank me yet. You still might snore."

When the bathroom door shut, the room changed shape at once. Not quieter, exactly, but more adult and more dangerous. Beni set the umbrella in the tray by the door. Water ran off it in a thin black line.

Eszter did not lower her voice. She did not need to. "You had no one else."

"No."

"You thought of her father?"

"Yes."

"And?"

"He'd turn it into control."

"Her mother."

"Drama."

"Your cousin."

"He wanted her gone before the tea cooled."

Eszter folded her arms. "And me."

He met her eyes because not doing it would have been cowardice stacked on cowardice. "You were the only person she'd let me bring her to."

"That isn't what I asked."

The truth of it sat between them, ugly and exact. Eszter was witness, judge, and still, because the world was rotten in its usual practical way, the safest room available.

"You didn't deserve this ask," he said.

"No."

"But she needed somewhere."

"Yes."

He waited for the rest, the cutting line he had earned. Eszter gave it to him without hurrying.

"Don't stand there and mistake this for trust," she said. "A girl turned up wet and half frozen. I opened the door for her. Don't confuse that with opening it for you."

"I know."

"Good. At least one sentence tonight can die after being used."

The bathroom tap ran, then banged off. Beni looked at the small room, the bed that was really one person's bed and would now have to become shelter for two, the loaf of bread already cut down by half, the bowl he had almost finished because hunger did not stop for shame. "I can stay outside," he said. "On the hall chair. In case--"

Eszter's face went incredulous. "No."

"If Oscar sends someone--"

"Then a young man in a church basement with a rich girl and no witness makes the story filthier, not cleaner. Use your brain. Here it's a woman and a child for one night. That I can argue. You in the corridor like a tragic extra helps nobody."

He looked away. She was right so fast it hurt.

"You'll go home," she said. "You'll keep your phone on. If she bolts again or vomits or sets this stupid building on fire, I'll call you."

"She won't."

"Excellent. Then you can sleep badly in Tottenham instead of artistically on church furniture."

The bathroom door opened. Bea came out in the oversize T-shirt, which hung to mid-thigh, and the thick socks folded at the ankle. She had washed her face. Without the mascara smudge and school coat she looked younger and stranger at once, a rich girl temporarily stripped of props. She carried her uniform folded over one arm with more care than Beni would have expected.

"Where do I put these?" she asked.

Eszter took the damp clothes and spread them over the radiator and the back of the chair with the ruthless efficiency of someone who had dried too many things in too little space. "There. Sit. Finish the soup."

Bea obeyed, then looked at Beni. "Are you going?"

"Yes."

She nodded once. Relief and guilt crossed her face so quickly they nearly canceled out. "Sorry."

"For what."

"For turning up at your flat."

He could have said several things then, most of them useless. That she had done the right thing. That she had done a foolish thing. That he was sorry too, for larger reasons than doors and taxis and soup. None of it would have helped the next ten minutes. "You're safe here," he said instead.

Eszter shot him a look as if warning him not to turn simple facts into speeches. Bea caught it and dropped her face to the bowl, but one shoulder loosened.

The phone buzzed again. Bea ignored it. Eszter picked it up, read the preview without unlocking, and set it back down. "Mrs. Keene says she'll be at the gate at eight-thirty. Your father says be ready and don't make this worse. So the house is still the house."

Bea made a face into her bowl. "That sounds right."

"You'll sleep. Until eight-thirty you're my responsibility, and after that you go with Mrs. Keene." Eszter spoke as if listing shopping steps, which was maybe why the words calmed the room instead of grandstanding in it. "In the morning you'll wash, you'll eat, and you'll go. No sneaking out at dawn. No dramatic notes. No social-media martyrdom."

"I wasn't going to do social media."

"Good. Nothing becomes noble because strangers like it."

Beni picked up his empty bowl and automatically took it to the sink. Eszter let him rinse it under the cold tap, but only because refusing would have taken longer. The ordinary act of his hands in her small room felt almost obscene in its familiarity.

"Leave the bowl," she said. "You don't live here."

He set it down.

At the door he reached into his pocket for his wallet, then stopped. Money was the wrong shape for this. Whatever he put on the table would feel purchased or contaminated or both. Eszter saw the movement anyway.

"Keep it," she said.

"I wasn't--"

"You were. Keep it."

He did.

Bea had finished the soup. Her shoulders had dropped a fraction. The tea towel still lay crumpled beside the bed where Eszter had wrapped her feet in it. Small domestic wreckage already, proof of a real body passing through. Not a donor photo, not a scandal, not a lesson. Just a wet, angry girl who needed somewhere to sleep.

"I'll keep my phone on," he said.

"You already said that," Eszter replied.

"Right."

He put his hand on the door. Bea looked up once more. "Beni."

He waited.

"I know this doesn't fix anything."

He thought of the garage, of the kitchen after Bea had spoken, of Eszter's face when she understood what kind of money he had pressed at her problems, of all the clean lies he had told because speaking late always felt easier than speaking in time. "No," he said. "It doesn't."

Bea nodded, apparently satisfied by that much honesty. Eszter was already turning down the bed and pulling the church blanket free with sharp economical snaps. Her pink elastic flashed once on her wrist in the yellow light.

Beni stepped out into the corridor. The door stayed open just long enough for Eszter to say, without looking at him, "Bring back Laci's umbrella tomorrow if you want one decent thing not to rot on top of the others."

"I will."

Then she shut the door.

He stood under the buzzing basement light with the damp umbrella in his hand and the smell of lentils and paprika leaking faintly through the wood. Inside, he heard Eszter say, "Move over, your wet hair will soak the pillow," and Bea answer something too soft to catch. It was not forgiveness. It was a lock, a bowl in the sink, a bed being made wider by necessity. He went up the stairs before he could turn any of it into more than that. By morning he'd be back at the Ashdowns' house for the gala.


# Chapter 16

"Thank Christ," Mrs. Keene said when Beni came through the basement door.

He had not even set the first champagne case down. Rain had not stopped since dawn. It lived in the cloth of his coat and in the hems of the temp staff waiting by the service gate with garment bags under plastic covers. Up front the Ashdowns' brass had been buffed, the black steps washed, the wreath fixed square between the lamps. No one with weight in their hands came through that door. The actual day was happening in the mews and the basement: florist vans breathing diesel into wet brick, crates of rented glass knocking together on the cobbles, a catering boy in a white jacket smoking under the arch with one cuff still basted in blue thread. Everything that would be called effortless by seven o'clock arrived in cardboard and went downstairs first.

Mrs. Keene stood at the junction where the service corridor met the kitchen passage and the back stairs, holding a laminated floor plan so damp at the edges it had started to peel. She had a cough sweet parked in one cheek and a yellow pencil tucked through the bun at the back of her head, point outward like a tiny weapon. "Take those champagne cases to the south pantry, then get the garment rails out of the garage. And if anyone from Delacourt asks where the ice sculpture goes, tell them no one in this house has ever wanted an ice sculpture and send them to me."

"Morning to you too," Beni said.

He had parked the Mercedes half a street over because the mews was already jammed with a refrigerated van, two flower lorries, and a small transit from the event company whose driver kept apologizing in Irish while blocking everybody anyway. The house smelled stronger than usual down here: polish and lilies and radiator heat, yes, but also wet wool, cut stems, tape, coffee grounds, bleach, and the hot metallic breath of catering ovens brought in for the evening.

She gave him the look she used for remarks that failed to improve logistics. "Move."

He moved. The champagne came in wooden boxes with rope handles that bit his palms through the damp. He took them one by one past the laundry alcove, the humming wine fridge, the folded trestle tables waiting like bodies at a station. On the service stairs above him temp waiters were learning the house by error: one came down backwards with a stack of charger plates, another froze flat against the wall to let a florist pass with a cage of bare branches wired with tiny lights. The branches shed drops onto the runner and left dark little wounds on the fabric.

At the kitchen door he almost stopped from habit, then did not. The door was open anyway. He saw enough in one glance: steel counters loaded with canape trays under cling film, boxes of herbs on the floor, a rented salamander grill giving off a hard baking heat. Eszter was at the centre of it in a black crew-neck top and the same work trousers she always wore, but with a short white apron tied over them for service. Somebody had pinned her hair up fast and badly; one dark strand kept coming loose above her ear and she kept blowing it away instead of touching it with her hands. On her wrist, under the elastic bite of a hair band, was a cheap plastic bracelet with yellow beads spelling LILI. He had never seen it on her at work before.

Beside the prep sink a brown church blanket hung over a laundry rack, still dripping weak grey water into a roasting tray someone had shoved underneath. Next to it, Bea's dark blazer lay turned inside out on the radiator shelf to dry the lining. Mrs. Keene had stacked folded tea towels in front of both as if cotton could make them kitchen objects instead of evidence.

Mrs. Keene was saying, in the level voice she used when shouting would have carried upstairs, "You brought her in through the mews at seven in yesterday's clothes and the milkman saw the cab. Fine. We lived through it. But if that agency girl says the word runaway before midnight, I'll bury her in canapé spoons."

Eszter did not look up from the tray she was setting. "Then bury her after service. Bea was shivering hard enough to crack her own teeth. I got her upstairs before Mrs. Ashdown came down, I put her in the shower, and the story now is migraine. That's the cleanest lie this house is getting."

She was showing two waitresses how to carry smoked-salmon spoons without tipping the creme fraiche off them. "Not over people's shoulders," she said. "You come in lower. Otherwise somebody's tuxedo gets fish on it and suddenly the end of the world is happening."

One of the waitresses, a girl with bitten burgundy nails and a silver stud through one eyebrow, laughed because she was nervous. "Right."

Eszter turned, saw Beni in the doorway, and let her eyes rest on him for no longer than the tray in his hands required.

"South pantry," she said. "Not here. If Keene sees bottles in my walkway she'll start speaking in tongues."

"I'm going."

"Then go."

Nothing in her voice was for him. That was almost easier than the chapter before last, when anger had still wanted an answer. This was work. Work was clean, even when people were not.

He took the boxes on. The south pantry, usually a sober little room for spare wine and table linen, had become an organ under strain. Cases of champagne were stacked by label. Silver tubs waited on the floor with their blue plastic still half torn off. A boy from the hire company, narrow-faced and red-handed from polishing, was counting flutes into rows of twelve while whispering football scores to himself. Every time he reached thirty-six he tapped the bridge of his nose with his little finger as if that kept the number in place.

"Need room there, mate," Beni said.

The boy looked up. "Sorry. I've got four hundred and eighty and they all think they're precious."

He shifted fast enough and Beni wedged the champagne into the gap by the wall. Four hundred and eighty flutes were enough mouths to make a house look successful for three hours.

All morning the mews kept filling and clearing, filling and clearing. Florists brought in trees with their roots wrapped in hessian and wire. A woman from the rental company arrived with chair covers no one had ordered and cried in the corridor because the return paperwork meant she'd pay for the taxi herself. Mrs. Keene took the forms from her, found the dispatch number, and fixed it while insulting everyone involved under her breath with the precision of a woman who had done this for fifteen years and no longer mistook competence for affection.

Oscar crossed the basement twice before noon. He wore trousers and shirt sleeves, cuff links still undone, jacket over one arm. Without the full suit he looked younger only in the sense that a machine looks younger with one panel off. He had a folded run sheet in his hand and kept opening and flattening it against whatever surface was nearest: counter, wine crate, wall. He asked for timings, counts, contingencies. He never raised his voice. He did not need to. Every question implied somebody else's failure in advance.

"The donor cars start at six forty-five," he said to Mrs. Keene while Beni took a rail of pressed coats out through the garage. "I want the first tray live in the front hall at six forty-seven, not before. Early reads desperate."

"Yes, sir."

"And the staff line on this morning?"

"Miss Bea was unwell and back before breakfast. The milkman, the porter, and one agency waitress all decided they saw weather and nothing else."

"Good."

"And the portrait lighting?"

"Tested again at four."

"Good."

His eyes slid to Beni. "You'll keep the Mercedes ready in the mews until Nina comes down. Once the guests begin, no dead space at the front. If a car idles where it can be seen, move it."

"Yes, sir."

"And if Bea refuses the photographer again, tell Keene, not me. I won't have a scene on the pavement."

The phrasing landed harder than if he had said daughter. Beni almost looked up, almost asked where she had been since last night, how that had been handled, whether anybody in this house had used the words thank you or where were you or are you all right. But Oscar was already reading the next line of paper. Family and staff existed for him in columns today: arrival, placement, problem, solution.

In the garage the garment rails rattled over the concrete as Beni dragged them into the corridor. The smell down there always held old rubber and cold petrol, but today somebody had added steamers. Their hiss made the space feel like a launderette in a rich dream. A seamstress from Knightsbridge alterations had taken over the far wall with irons, white cloths, and two evening gowns hanging from hooks on the pipes. She wore half-moon reading glasses on a chain and bright red trainers under her black skirt. Every time she passed one gown with the steamer she muttered to it in Romanian as if coaxing a difficult child through an exam.

"Don't let anyone smoke in here," she said when Beni came by. "These fabrics burn faster than sinners."

"No one smokes in here."

She snorted. "Men always say that about places they've never watched properly."

By one o'clock his shirt was sticking between his shoulders. He had driven nowhere except twice around the block to clear space for delivery vans, but he felt as tired as if he'd done airport runs all morning. Service work in a rich house was often like that: no distance, no destination, just constant small lifting and being in the way until suddenly you were the solution to something not your own.

He was taking a box of votive candles up from the cellar when his phone buzzed once in the inside pocket of his coat. Not Mercer dispatch. Nina.

Garage. Now.

He stood at the turn of the service stairs with the candle box against his thigh. From above came the hard bright chatter of hired women arranging flowers in tall glass cylinders. From below came Eszter's voice saying somebody had put the vegetarian tartlets next to the duck and if that happened again she'd feed the whole tray to them personally. Mrs. Keene was asking for six more black napkins. The house needed hands on every floor.

His phone buzzed again.

Now, Ben.

He set the candles on the stair, went down two flights, cut through the laundry, and crossed the garage with his pulse doing something stupid and adolescent in his neck. That room had been where Nina first pulled him into the back seat in daylight with her hand already under his zip, and later where she had him on his knees on the rubber mat so her dress would not crease against the leather. Even empty, it remembered.

The Mercedes waited under the strip light, polished for the evening, black enough to take the garage into itself. Nina was in the back seat with the door on the driver's side open, one heel on the floor, the other foot tucked under her on the leather. She wore a pale slip under a robe the colour of watered wine, and someone upstairs had done half her face for the gala and not the other half. One eye was lined, the other bare. It made her look both younger and more dangerous, as if different women had started claiming her and neither had finished.

On the seat beside her lay two passports, a leather folder, and a pair of long earrings tangled together like hooks.

"Close the door," she said.

He did.

"Sit down."

"I can't."

"You can. You're not carrying Christ."

He got into the front, not the back. The car still held last night's leather polish and the faint powdery perfume from whatever she had been sprayed with upstairs. Rain tapped the garage roof in a thin metallic scatter. Through the windscreen he could see only the shut garage door and, above it, the strip of wired bulbs somebody had tested for the mews arch.

Nina picked up one passport, turned it over in her hand, then the other. Her nails were bare again. He had started noticing when they were bare because it usually meant she had been chewing at the edges.

"I'm going to Paris after tonight," she said.

"For how long?"

"Long enough." She looked at the passports, not at him. "Maybe Christmas. Maybe spring. Maybe until Oscar has learned the restorative pleasures of missing me. The apartment on Rue de Verneuil is free until April, and after that I can take something else if I choose to stay."

Beni said nothing.

"Don't make that face. It's Paris, not exile in a salt mine."

"I didn't make a face."

"You did, but never mind." She finally looked up. "I want you to come."

The garage gave back the sentence in a softer shape. He thought first of the obvious practical thing, because practical things were easier to hold. "For driving."

"For that," she said. "For the apartment. For nights when I don't want a stranger in my bed or in the back seat. Don't give me that face. We've already practiced the distinctions."

It should have made him angry. It did, a little. But under the anger came the remembered facts: the leather sticking to his shins, her fingers in his hair, the garage light burning over the windscreen while he wiped the seat clean afterward with blue roll from the workbench. Then came the image so fast it embarrassed him: a flat with dry windows, his own key, a bathroom where the towel stayed warm after you hung it up, no cousin's boots under the bed, no Tube calculation every time he wanted to cross the city. He thought of his foldout mattress in Tottenham, the spring that caught his hip, the window that sweated all winter onto the sill. He thought of Laci counting what he owed as if each note had already left his own pocket and only Beni's incompetence delayed the transfer.

Nina watched him take the picture in. She had always been quick with appetite in other people.

"You'll have a room," she said. "A proper one. The little room off the kitchen, but with its own shower, shutters that close, and a mattress made for an adult spine. Most mornings I won't need you before ten. You'll bring the car up from the underground, collect flowers, deal with deliveries, take me to dinners, wait through galleries and lunches and whatever legal farce Oscar insists on. I'll pay you better than Mercer, and on Fridays I'll put cash in your hand so you stop looking hunted every time rent is due. If we stay past April, I can have papers done sensibly through a service company and save everyone the melodrama."

She touched the passports again, aligning them exactly with the leather folder. She controlled herself through objects. He had seen it often enough to know what it meant. Under the poise there was panic moving furniture around.

"Why me?" he asked, and hated how young it sounded.

"Because I know you." She smiled without warmth. "Because you know where all the embarrassing pieces are buried. Because you can keep your mouth shut without making it a theological problem. Because you don't bore me. Pick whichever answer flatters you least."

He looked away to the rearview mirror, which showed them both badly: him in his damp shirt and work suit, her half-made face and robe, the passports bright as teeth between them.

"And if I don't want to be buried with the embarrassing pieces?"

"Then you have mistaken me for a sentimentalist." Her tone stayed light, but the words did not. "Ben, don't be childish. I'm offering you a life that is visibly better than the one you have."

From somewhere overhead came the muffled thunder of something heavy rolling across floorboards. The house was still moving. He pictured Mrs. Keene asking where the candles were, Eszter sending a temp back for more pickled onions, some donor's stupid dietary requirement becoming urgent in a pantry. Down here, cut off in the garage, the offer sat in the warm air like a second body.

Nina leaned forward, forearms on the front seat. "Listen to me. No one in Paris will care who you were here. No one will ask whether you came through the front door or the side, whether some girl in a kitchen stopped speaking to you, whether your employer's daughter has become tiresome. You can have a decent coat, a decent wage, a better city, and fewer witnesses. Most men wouldn't need that offered twice."

He saw it at once: dry windows, a towel still warm on the rail, no Oscar, no Mercer logs, no Laci counting what he owed across the table, no church-basement room where he had stood last night with Bea's overnight bag against his shin while Eszter made a child tea and looked past him. He could leave the whole London arrangement behind and call it survival, and nobody in Paris would bother to argue.

Then the house thudded again above them and he thought, stupidly, of the service corridor on the second floor where the wallpaper had worn dark at shoulder height from staff carrying trays. Paris would have the same dark band on fresher paper, rubbed there by trays, garment bags, and men who never used the front bell.

"What would I be there?" he asked.

Nina blinked once, thrown more by the wording than by the question itself. "What do you mean?"

"I mean in Paris. What would I be there?"

She gave a short impatient laugh. "Employed. Comfortable, if you let yourself be. Useful. Why does everyone under forty insist on turning everything into an identity crisis?"

"Useful to what."

"To me, obviously. At first. And then to yourself, if you have any ambition beyond martyring yourself for people who can't even afford your loyalty." She sat back. "You'd drive. You'd keep the apartment supplied. You'd carry envelopes I don't want receipted and book things that don't belong on my statements. And when I told you to come upstairs or lock the car, you wouldn't stand there pretending you didn't know whether I meant the bed or the back seat. You're very good at lives that need discretion."

Nina had put the bed and the car on the same line and left them there.

He looked at his hands on the steering wheel. They were clean because the house had forced him into cleaner tasks today, but the rope from the champagne cases had left pink grooves across the base of his fingers. He would drive, stock the apartment, carry the cash, kneel when she was lonely or furious, and wipe the trace of it away afterward. In London he did it through the mews, the garage, the altered logbook, and the waiting silence in parked cars. In Paris the leather would be softer and the coffee better, but the work would still cut the same grooves into him.

The shame of wanting it did not vanish just because he understood it. He still wanted the dry room, the money, the heat, the ease of not counting every Oyster top-up against food. He wanted the kind of life where the emergency amount in his wallet could stay an amount instead of dissolving into fares and rent gaps and other people's boots. Nina knew exactly which part of him still counted coins before sleep.

"I can't leave tonight," he said.

"I didn't ask you to leave mid-canape." She sounded annoyed now, which meant she had heard the answer forming and disliked waiting for it. "After the gala. Tomorrow, or Monday at the latest. I can book the train this afternoon if I must. I assumed speed would appeal to you."

"Why so fast?"

"Because delay gives everyone time to perform principles. Because Oscar will be unwatchable after tonight. Because I would rather be gone before this house starts discussing itself in that ghastly controlled voice rich people use for disaster. Must I really narrate every motivation as if you're my analyst?"

His gaze caught on the passports again, two navy covers on pale leather. If he said yes in this light she would book the ticket before he got the candles upstairs, and the whole decision would shrink into luggage, timings, and whatever story the house told tomorrow. He kept one hand on the door release. "I need to think."

Nina studied him. Half her mouth had lipstick; half did not. The effect was oddly childish and made the next line rougher.

"No," she said. "You need to recognize luck before it gets embarrassed and leaves."

He met her eyes in the mirror then, properly. For a second he could see the version of it she meant to sell: train doors shutting on this house, on London, on everybody who had used him and everybody he had lied to, a new city where he could be kept without being called kept. The relief came too fast, the way heat hits when you climb into a car that's been waiting underground. She was not selling fantasy. She was holding out a key.

Then he heard the radio crackle on the dash from the house set someone had left in the cup holder.

"Beni?" Mrs. Keene's voice, thin with static. "If you're still alive, where are the candles?"

Nina rolled her eyes toward the roof. "You see? Even Providence is middle management."

He opened the driver's door. Cold damp air rushed in around his knees. "I've got to go."

"Tonight," she said behind him. "Not here. Not now. But before the speeches I want an answer."

He got out and shut the door softly. Through the darkened glass he saw her tip her head back against the leather and close the made-up eye first, then the other.

The candles were still on the stair where he had abandoned them. He carried them up. Mrs. Keene took one look at his face and, to her credit, asked nothing. In this house everyone senior knew the value of other people's silence as long as it kept the timetable moving.

The afternoon compressed. Rain thinned to a fine mist and the house grew hotter, louder, more arranged. The service corridor outside the drawing room filled with black coats on staff pegs, radio chargers, spare votive lighters, tape, three hidden boxes of supermarket mince pies decanted into a Fortnum tin in case anybody downstairs needed to eat without offending the concept of elegance. Beni drove once to collect an extra run of ice from the hotel supplier in Knightsbridge and once more to move Oscar's second car to a nearby private garage because the mews had become all loading zone and no exit. The city beyond Belgravia looked ordinary and mean in comparison, buses wet and overfull, Deliveroo riders hunched under boxy bags. Every time he came back through the service entrance the house swallowed him again and handed him another small necessary thing.

At four thirty the first musicians arrived through the back gate because the cello case was too large for the side stairs and no one wanted marks on the wall. The cellist was a woman in her fifties with nicotine fingers and a string of tiny silver fish around her neck. She asked where she could boil water for her thermos and, when Mrs. Keene pointed her toward the staff kettle, said, "Lovely, because if I drink donor tea I start resenting the dead." Then she vanished into the morning room with the other two players and began tuning in long private notes that made the walls feel expensive.

Beni saw Bea for the first time just after five.

She came down the back stairs in a black dress that looked too adult in the cut and too honest in the way she wore it, as if she'd been dressed by committee and rejected the committee by posture alone. Her hair had been blown smooth, but she had tied the old frayed black school ribbon around her wrist instead of leaving it in her hair. At the heel of one foot a blister plaster showed above the strap of her shoe. Mrs. Keene was behind her carrying a steamed shawl and the expression of a woman escorting unstable glass.

Bea stopped when she saw Beni by the service table with the guest place cards.

"You look thrilled," she said.

"You look tired."

"That's because I've spent the day being upholstered."

Mrs. Keene said, "Miss Bea."

"What? It's accurate."

Her face was pale under the makeup, but not frightened. Worse. Resigned in the flat adolescent way that made adults think compliance had been achieved when really it was only being stored for later. Beni wanted to ask if she'd eaten, if she'd slept after the church room, if Eszter had managed to keep the whole thing from blowing the house apart by breakfast. None of those belonged to this corridor with place cards and a tray of canapes sweating under cling film.

So he said, "Do your shoes fit."

Bea blinked, almost smiled, then looked down. "Not morally."

"There's a chair in the pantry if you need five minutes before photos."

"That's the most romantic thing anybody's said to me all month."

Mrs. Keene made a noise in her throat. "The photographer is waiting."

Bea turned to go, then looked back once. "Eszter says if I faint on purpose she'll know."

"Then don't faint."

"I wasn't planning anything so gauche."

She went on. The ribbon on her wrist trailed like she'd forgotten it was there.

At six Oscar changed into black tie and became, somehow, even more managerial. He did not look handsome exactly. He looked settled into the costume people expected money to wear. He had a printed guest list in one hand and his cuff links closed now, both perfect. Only once, when a florist misread the plan and sent the large white arrangement to the library instead of the front hall, did he show the strain underneath. He took off his glasses, pinched the bridge of his nose, then put them back on and said, very softly, "If the flowers are wrong by the front door the entire evening begins as apology. Move them."

Everyone moved them.

The thing Beni hated most about watching rich households under pressure was how often the language of theatre turned out to be the language of fact. Place. Entrance. Backdrop. Hold. Delay. Reveal. The family was not outside the machinery. They were the central props that cost the most to damage. Oscar stood in the front drawing room with the photographer and the event manager, placing bodies in air.

"Not there," he said to the photographer. "If Nina stands under that painting she'll look shorter."

"We'll adjust."

"No, let's avoid adjusting. Bea half a step left. There. Keep the piano in frame, not the drinks station. If my mother arrives before the speech, she goes into the blue room first. I don't want her cornering Nina in public."

He turned when Beni came to the door.

"Good. At six forty-eight you bring Nina round to the front once the first two donor cars have cleared. Not before. If she isn't ready, you wait in the mews and keep the engine out of earshot. At seven-oh-three Bea comes down for photographs. At seven-ten she's in the morning room until the chairman arrives. At seven-fifteen, if the family sequence is stable, we do the portrait. Stable, Beni. I'm not improvising around adolescent moods with forty people watching."

He spoke as if reading route instructions for cargo. Beni heard the daughter's name, the wife's name, the time codes, the car positions, all laid into the same calm sequence.

"Yes, sir," he said.

Oscar checked the list. "And if Nina asks where I've gone, you tell her I'm receiving guests. If she asks twice, you tell Keene."

"Yes, sir."

"Good."

By the time Beni stepped back, Oscar had already dropped his eyes to the list again.

He took the service stairs down because the main hall was no longer a place to walk through unless you belonged to the show. Halfway down he stopped at the landing window that looked into the mews. The light outside had gone fully blue. Wet brick shone under the strings of bulbs. A van from the florist was still parked crooked by the bins because the driver had lost his keys and was searching every jacket pocket with greater and greater despair. From the basement kitchen came the smell of butter warming, then the quick clean cut of citrus as somebody dressed oysters. From upstairs floated the first proper notes of the cello, low and polished and meant to make people feel their own importance as if it had a soundtrack.

Paris would have its own hook board by somebody else's kitchen. Nina might call the flat elegant and his room generous and the work civilized, but the order of it was already plain enough: wait here, bring this, not yet, keep that off paper. It came with a room off the kitchen and cash on Fridays. His name would stay where no one important could trip over it.

The pull stayed in him because he could already see the room: shutters closed, towel warm, mattress made for an adult spine. Tottenham would still be damp. Laci would still want money. Mercer would still be Mercer. Eszter still might never touch him again except by accident over a tray. Refusing Nina would not sweeten any of that. It would only leave him standing in the life he already had, without her better upholstery laid over it.

He stood there long enough for the landing radiator to click on by his shin.

He rubbed the rope groove at the base of his finger and looked at the paint worn off the sill by other hands. It would be another city, another corridor, another woman calling for the car. He knew the job already.

Downstairs a radio snapped alive. "Front gate open in two. Car on approach."

Beni straightened, one hand still on the paint-chipped sill. He did not say the answer out loud because there was no one there to hear it. He only let it settle into the place where he usually kept route numbers and night addresses.

He would not go to Paris, not after the speeches, not on Monday, not into a better room built around the same arrangement.

The first donor bell sounded upstairs, neat and resonant through the house. In the mews somebody swore with helpless fury about the missing van keys. Beni went down the rest of the stairs, crossed the service corridor, and took the Mercedes keys from the hook board. When Mrs. Keene's voice came over the radio asking for the car at the front, he answered at once and kept the no to himself.


# Chapter 17

Beni moved between the front step and the garage with a radio clipped under his lapel and rain drying in a cold stripe down his back. The front hall smelled expensive and overworked. Lilies heated too close to the radiators had gone thick and sweet there, and under them lived the smells nobody put on invitations: dishwasher steam from the basement, frying oil from the caterers' burners in the mews, bleach, wet wool, the sour metal breath of lids lifted and dropped over chafing dishes. Every room upstairs looked effortless because three rooms below it were not.

The gala had eaten the whole townhouse. The library tables were gone under white cloth and auction cards. The morning room had become a champagne station. The drawing room held a small forest of hired trees, pale roses wired into them so tightly they looked pinned there by force. On the easel by the stairs a cream card explained, in a font that managed to seem both charitable and expensive, what the evening would do for women and children in emergency accommodation. One table funded six weeks. One premier bid funded a whole room. Beni had seen Eszter's church-basement bed the night before, the blanket folded with church neatness and the metal chair that left dents in the wall when you scraped it back. He read the number once and did not read it again.

The staff moved with the flat, shut face of people who could not afford a mistake. A coat-check woman from the agency kept blotting at her nose with the inside of her wrist because the lilies had started her hay fever, then smiling at fur-collared guests before her eyes had stopped watering. One of the catering boys, red-haired and too young for the jacket they had put him in, slipped his left foot out of his shoe whenever he thought nobody was looking; the back strap had rubbed his heel raw, and he was working on the ball of that foot like a dancer with no glamour in it. In the kitchen a dishwasher tray jammed and somebody swore in Polish. Upstairs a trustee laughed too loudly at something not funny, and the laugh travelled through the house better than any order.

Oscar Ashdown moved through it all with the stillness of a man who believed nothing ought to touch him directly. He never hurried. He only redirected. A flower arrangement shifted six inches. A donor's place card changed tables because the woman who ran the foundation had finally understood which divorce could not be sat beside which remarriage. Two champagne trays were held back until the photographer was ready. When a waiter crossed the front hall too early with oyster shells stacked like wet fingernails on silver, Oscar did not tell him off. He said, "Not yet," in the same voice other men used for prayer or threat, and the waiter went red all the way to the ears.

Nina was even better at it than Oscar because she could make command look like grace from a distance. She stood beneath the staircase in dark blue silk that looked black until the chandeliers caught it, greeting the board women by name, touching elbows, listening with a tilt of the head that implied warmth while keeping her body out of reach. She had dressed for scrutiny as much as beauty, and Beni knew the labor that had gone into that too: the steamed gown, the pinned hair, the makeup spread over fatigue like lacquer over cracked wood. Once, crossing the hall with a message from the mews, he came close enough to smell her perfume under the lilies, something dry and expensive with a bitter edge like grapefruit peel. She did not look at him. Her mouth moved through a donor smile while she said, low enough that only he heard it, "Stay close after the auction."

He had already decided he was not going to Paris with her. That decision had sat in him all afternoon like an object in a pocket, hard-edged and private and easy to touch without changing anything. Not going was one thing. Speaking was another. All day he had been telling himself that refusal counted as a kind of honesty. He would finish the shift, hand the future back, keep the rest buried where it had already done the damage. That was how he had imagined surviving it.
