Near eight the first real crush hit: late donors in damp black cars, women with bare shoulders pulling cashmere over them against the draft, men already pink from cocktails elsewhere. Beni took coats, passed glasses, opened the front door, closed it, crossed the hall, went down the service stairs, came up again. The auction catalogues were printed on paper thicker than anything in Laci's flat except the front door. A woman in diamonds said to her husband, not quietly, that emergency housing had become "terribly difficult politically" and took a second blini she did not finish. A man with a face like suet asked whether the mews entrance was where "all the magic happened." Beni told him yes, sir, because the truthful version would have taken too long and cost the wrong person.

Bea came down just before the call for the family photographs. He knew it was her before he saw her face because nobody else her age in that house moved like she was bracing for school inspection. She wore a black dress cut plain enough to pass for adult taste, but she kept pinching the side seam near her thigh as if the fabric had offended her personally. Her dark hair was pinned back too tightly. Someone had put earrings on her that looked old enough to belong to a grandmother she probably never liked. She paused on the last stair and looked across the front room with the dry, appalled expression she usually saved for her mother's friends.

Nina's hand landed briefly at the middle of Bea's back. Oscar was already near the sponsor wall the foundation had built beside the tree, all white flowers and printed logos and calculated innocence. The foundation chair, a narrow woman with a tennis elbow strap still under her sleeve, smiled with all her teeth and said, "Just one quick family shot before dinner, if we may."

Oscar turned. "Bea. Here, please."

Bea did not move.

Nina kept smiling for the chair. "Darling, one minute."

"No."

The chair laughed as if she had heard a joke and not a seventeen-year-old refusing a public order. Oscar's face altered by almost nothing. "Now isn't the time."

The radio clipped under Beni's lapel knocked once against his ribs when he breathed. He knew this kind of pause in the house: the little rich silence before blame took the service stairs. A donor got embarrassed for twelve seconds and by morning somebody downstairs lost hours over it.

Bea looked at the flowers, then at the sponsor logos, then at her parents. "Use the orchids," she said. "They hold still better."

Two donors within earshot discovered urgent interest in their champagne. The photographer lowered his camera a fraction without lowering it all the way. Nina's smile tightened at one corner.

"Don't be vulgar," Nina said.

"I'm not the vulgar thing here."

The back of Beni's neck went cold. In the car Bea had watched enough pickups, enough lies about timing, enough late returns smoothed into the next morning's schedule. He had let himself call that silence adolescence because the truer version made him part of what she was refusing.

Oscar stepped nearer, voice dropping instead of rising. "You'll come here, stand for thirty seconds, and then do whatever performance of outrage you've planned in private."

Bea gave a short laugh that sounded older than her face. "That's the whole point, isn't it? Private. Everything ugly private, and then this." She touched the air between them, the flowers, the logos, the camera. "I'm not doing it."

Nina said, still smiling for the room, "Beatrice."

"No. You can crop me in from some other year."

She turned before either of them could get a hand on her and walked across the hall, not fast, which made it worse. She passed Beni close enough that he saw the skin at her throat blotched red above the dress neckline. She smelled of hair spray and the peppermint gum she always chewed in the car after school. The front door was blocked by guests, so she cut left through the little passage toward the coat room and the service stairs, knowing the house well enough to disappear by its useful routes instead of its pretty ones.

For one suspended second the whole front room held. The foundation chair still had the bright fixed face of someone who would rather be bitten than acknowledge disaster. The photographer pretended to check his settings. A waiter bent to wipe a splash of champagne from the marble with his napkin because the tray in his hand gave him something to do besides stare.

Oscar did not look at Nina. He looked at Beni. "Go after her," he said. "Find out where she's going."

Beni moved before he had answered. The passage smelled colder than the front hall and faintly of polish and old umbrellas. He heard Nina behind him say something to the chair in the soft apologetic murmur rich people used when they expected the room to help them keep lying. Then the swing door cut the sound off and he was in the service corridor with the fluorescent lights and the stacked crates from the florist.

Bea had not gone down to the basement. She had gone out toward the mews, fast enough that one of the side doors was still rocking on its hinge when Beni reached it. Cold rain needled his face. The stone behind the house shone black under the security lights, and at the far end, just beyond the bins and the catering van, Bea was crouched on the lowest step with one heel already off. A black wool coat lay beside her in a heap. She had a canvas tote open between her knees and was dragging out a pair of white trainers with the laces knotted together from being jammed in too fast.

"Bea."

She did not startle. She only looked up once and went back to the laces. "I'm aware."

"You can't go out like that."

"In a coat and shoes? London will cope."

He came down the step into the rain. Up close her hands were not steady. She was fighting the heel buckle with fingers made clumsy by cold and fury, and the tiny clasp kept slipping. Beni crouched without asking and held the shoe still while she yanked the strap free. She let him do it because it was quicker, not because she wanted help.

"Where are you going?" he asked.

"Away from the nativity."

"Be serious."

She shoved one trainer on hard enough to bend the back. "I'm being serious. That's why everybody hates it."

He looked past her toward the mouth of the mews. No car was waiting yet. No friend on the pavement. Just wet brick, bins, the florist's wilted stems in black sacks, and the red pulse of brake lights from the street beyond. "I need to know you're not just walking off."

She straightened up, one trainer tied, the other unlaced, coat still hanging open over the black dress. "Why? So my father can say you recovered the asset?"

"So I know you aren't alone."

That checked her for a beat. Her face did not soften; it only shifted, taking him in from a different angle. She pulled her phone from the tote, thumb moving fast across the screen. Dark polish chipped at the edges. "I'm not doing yesterday again," she said. "I'm not turning up at your flat with a bag and making my life everybody else's problem. Nessa's mum's coming for me. She knows. She actually asked if I wanted picking up instead of saying I ought to think about optics."

The phone glowed between them. A map pin flashed on it. She had already sent messages to three people. One of the names was Eszter. He looked away from that before she noticed.

"Did you tell them where?" he asked.

"I told them I wasn't dead in a canal, if that's what you mean."

Her mother's first name pressed hot against the back of his tongue. He swallowed it with rain and said nothing. Bea tucked the phone into her coat pocket and crouched again to knot the second trainer. The wet stone darkened the knees of her tights. The expensive earrings were still in, absurdly formal above the hunched, practical business of tying a shoe in the rain.

"When he doesn't like something," she said, eyes on the laces, "he turns it into somebody else's hours. He'll say the evening was disrupted and suddenly the kitchen has too many staff or the driver should've managed me better or the agency girl in coats wasn't attentive enough. That's how it works here."

Beni said nothing.

She glanced up, sharp enough to see the silence land. "You know that."

"Yes."

"Then don't let him do it for this."

From inside the house came a burst of applause, hollow through the walls. Somebody had started speeches or pretended to. Bea stood and pulled on the black coat. It was plain, warm, and far more like her than the dress. She pushed her hands into the pockets and hunched once against the cold before forcing herself upright again.

Headlights turned into the mews, too quick for a hired car idling for money. A dark Volvo nosed between the catering van and the Ashdowns' garage and stopped. Music thudded once from inside before being cut off. The back door flew open and a girl Beni recognized vaguely from school pickups climbed half out, waving. She wore an oversized velvet blazer over a silver slip dress and had bitten the lipstick off the centre of her mouth. Another girl in the back scooted sideways to make room, one false eyelash lifting at the corner as she blinked. In the front passenger seat sat a woman in navy tracksuit bottoms under a camel coat, hair flattened at the crown as if she had jammed a knit hat over it earlier and not cared what happened after.

"Bea," the woman called, with the resigned competence of a parent already interrupted three times that evening. Then she looked at Beni, took in the house, the suit, the wet mews, and said, "She's with us."

Bea walked to the car but stopped before getting in. "Nessa's mum knows where we're going," she said. "There's an adult. Everybody can unclench."

Nessa's mother snorted. "A debatable adult, but yes."

The line was dry enough that Bea nearly smiled. It passed over her face and was gone. She looked back at Beni. For a second she seemed younger, not because she softened but because the rage sat on top of something thin and frightened and still trying not to show. "Tell him I'm not missing," she said. "Tell him I just wasn't available for display."

Beni nodded.

She held his gaze another beat. "And don't let them put this on Eszter."

"I won't."

"You'd better not."

She got into the car. The girl with the loose eyelash shoved a packet of salt-and-vinegar crisps at her without comment. Nessa's mother waited until Bea had shut the door and fastened the belt, then gave Beni a short glance that managed to say she was not asking questions tonight but could if forced. After that she pulled the Volvo back toward the street. Bea did not look out again. The taillights turned left at the mouth of the mews and were gone toward Sloane Square.

Beni stood in the rain long enough for the cold to get through the shoulders of his jacket. He could still go back upstairs and say only that Bea was safe with friends. He could let the house close over the scene the way it closed over everything: a lowered voice, a shifted schedule, somebody else docked by morning for an inconvenience that had not started with them. Nina would take him aside later, after the auction, maybe after midnight, and talk about Paris again as if a new city changed the arrangement. Oscar would count the evening a success if the pledges held. The driver would drive, the staff would clean, the lies would move to their next container.

Eszter's church-basement room came back to him: the neat blanket, the bag under the bed containing everything that could be packed in four minutes. Then Bea on the step in a black dress and trainers, fighting with a heel buckle like it was a hand on her. The decision he had been holding all day turned useless in his chest. Refusing Paris was not enough.

The Mercedes sat in the Ashdowns' garage where he had left it after the last airport pickup, black paint filmed with the damp that crept in even under cover. He opened the driver's door and leaned into the leather hush he knew too well. The route logbook was in the glove compartment under the insurance papers, a black Mercer binder with duplicate pages and October's lies in his own careful handwriting. He took it out. Then he opened the inner pocket of his jacket and pulled out Nina's envelope.

What was left in it came to one hundred and seventy pounds: three fifties and two tens, the notes still flat and almost crisp except for the one he had sweated through last week. It looked smaller outside the house than it had inside it. Smaller than the rent it had helped. Smaller than the hours it had bought. He slid the notes back into the envelope, tucked the binder under his arm, and went in through the service door.

The speeches had started. On the small monitor by the kitchen pass the ballroom showed rows of dinner jackets and bright bare shoulders turned toward a lectern. The foundation chair was talking about dignity, safety, and the importance of private generosity in public times. Freya, the catering girl with the blistered heel, stood by the dumbwaiter with six empty champagne flutes in each hand and rolled her eyes so hard she nearly lost one. Then she saw Beni looking and shrugged as if to say she had not meant anything by it. Her mascara had smudged at one corner from heat.

Beni went upstairs.

Oscar was not in the ballroom. Beni found him in the morning room just off the hall, door mostly closed, phone in hand, listening while someone on the other end spoke too long. A half-finished whisky sat on the side table beside a tray of untouched canapes, the pastry already sweating butter into the napkin. Through the wall came the softened cadence of the speech and polite donor laughter at lines meant to prove the evening had a heart.

Oscar saw him before he finished the call. He lifted one finger for wait, listened another ten seconds, then said, "No, keep the press line outside until after the second course. No exceptions." He ended the call and set the phone facedown. "Well?"

"Bea's safe," Beni said. His voice sounded drier than his mouth felt. "She left with a school friend and the friend's mother. Nessa Doyle."

Oscar nodded once. He knew the name, or pretended to. "Thank you."

Beni did not move.

Oscar's eyes dropped to the binder under his arm, then to the envelope in his hand. Nothing showed in his face, but the room tightened anyway. "What's this."

Beni put the envelope on the side table beside the whisky and set the Mercer book next to it. "I need to give you these."

Oscar did not touch either one. "Explain."

"The money is from Mrs. Ashdown."

"That narrows very little."

"Private cash," Beni said. "For drives outside Mercer. For keeping quiet."

Oscar looked at the envelope then, properly now. The speech next door reached a point where the room beyond them applauded and then settled again. "About what."

Beni felt the vinyl edge of the logbook digging into his palm where he still held it flat to the table. "Routes. Where she went. Who she saw. I changed the book."

Oscar's gaze moved to the binder. "You altered company records."

"Yes."

"When."

"October first. After the gallery. Then later." He swallowed. "Hackney Wick entries. Some riverside nights. Pickup times. Mileage."

"Specifics, Mr. Varga."

Beni opened the binder because it was easier to point at ink than keep talking into Oscar's face. The pages crackled under his fingers. His own writing looked almost offensively neat. "These here. And these. She called late and I wrote earlier returns. If dispatch asked, I kept the route inside Belgravia or Chelsea. Sometimes she was with Mr. Hale. Sometimes we only drove."

Oscar did not sit. He stood over the table reading the pages in quick, efficient flashes, not as a husband first and not even as an employer first, but as a man presented with falsified numbers. "How many times."

"I don't know exactly."

"You must."

"Not exact." The shame of that irritated him, because it was a childish answer and also the truth. "Enough."

Oscar gave him a look cold enough to take the heat out of the room. "Enough for what."

Beni forced himself not to look away. "Long enough that Bea knew something was wrong, and Eszter knew I had money that wasn't clean."

The speech next door ended. Cutlery and conversation swelled up at once, a whole room relieved to be allowed back into appetite. Oscar put one hand on the back of a chair and asked, very quietly, "Was this arrangement sexual."

Beni could have lied smaller even now. He knew that in the second before he answered. He could have said personal. He could have said she leaned on him. He could have said it got mixed up. All the phrases that left some air in a story for everybody else to breathe. Bea had stood in front of the flowers and said no. He heard that no again, flat and young and expensive.

"Yes," he said.

The door moved behind him. Nina had come in without either of them hearing the handle over the cutlery noise. She shut it with more care than the moment deserved. Her face was still the gala face, makeup perfect, mouth painted, but the control in it had thinned. "Oscar," she said, "not now."

Oscar did not turn. "How much did you hear."

"Enough to know this conversation can wait until we're not hosting two hundred people."

"It has been waiting for months by the look of it."

Nina looked at Beni then, and whatever she felt hit him through polish before it hit him through anger: disbelief first, then contempt sharpened by embarrassment. "What are you doing."

He had no good sentence ready. That was the point. "I brought him the book."

"I can see that."

"And the money."

She looked at the envelope with immediate understanding. "Jesus Christ." The curse came out clipped and low, not for effect but because she had forgotten who she was performing for. "This is not confession. This is panic."

"Maybe," Beni said.

"You could have simply left."

"I know."

Her eyes narrowed. "Then why didn't you."

He did not answer. Every answer he had was partial and ugly. Bea had walked out. Eszter had folded her life small enough to fit under a church bed while donors next door bought emergency rooms by raising paddles. Nina had offered him Paris like a prettier service entrance. He was tired of hearing his own silence praised as if it were character. None of it could be said in a room like this without coming out better than it was.

Oscar picked up the envelope at last and thumbed the notes inside without counting them in front of Beni. "Is this all."

"No."

"How much is missing."

"I used some."

"On what."

Beni heard the humiliation in the question before he answered it. "Rent. Food. Some went to Eszter when her shifts were cut."

Nina shut her eyes once, fast, as if against a bright light. "Of course it did."

Oscar looked at him over the envelope. "You took money from my wife to conceal an affair with my wife, falsified a company's records to support it, and then redistributed some of that money to a member of staff whose employment was already compromised by this household."

"Yes."

"Did Ms. Toth know where it came from."

"Not all of it. Then later, yes."

Oscar nodded once, almost to himself, assembling sequence. "And my daughter."

"She knew enough."

"Evidently."

For a second nobody spoke. The ballroom next door had moved on to the first course. Plates clicked. Somebody laughed hard and false at the wrong end of the room. Somewhere in the hall a radio hissed with kitchen timing, the little static burst weirdly intimate in the silence.

Nina put a hand on the edge of the sideboard. Her nails were pale and perfect and pressed hard enough at the wood that he could see the tendons in the back of her hand. "Oscar, if you would like to conduct a forensic audit of my humiliations, do it tomorrow. Right now what matters is that the evening stays intact."

Oscar turned to her then. He did not raise his voice. "The evening isn't intact."

"It's salvageable."

"For donors, perhaps."

Something like a smile touched her mouth, bitter and gone at once. "Please. As if you care about any other category."

He let that pass without dignity because dignity was cheaper than time just then. He looked back at Beni. "Give me your keys."

Beni stared at him a moment too long before understanding. Then he reached into his pocket and set the Ashdown house key, the mews fob, and the Mercedes key on the table beside the envelope and the whisky. The small metal sounds they made seemed louder than the cutlery through the wall.

"You're finished here," Oscar said.

Nina drew breath to speak, but he kept going.

"You won't drive for this household again. I'll inform Mercer tonight that their records were falsified and that you're no longer to be assigned to any member of this family. Accounts will settle the hours already worked. There'll be no recommendation from me, and if Mercer asks whether you handled routes honestly, the answer will be no."

The disaster arrived in figures before it arrived in feeling. Monday's wage. The Ashdown gratuity that would not come at Christmas. The extra cash nights when Nina wanted the car and paid as if banknotes could keep consequence decorative. The line on a future form where somebody important wrote reliable. Laci's rent notebook on the kitchen table, opened flat under his thick hand. It all came at once, practical and exact, and Beni found himself looking not at Oscar or Nina but at the half-melted canape grease staining the napkin under the untouched pastry.

"All right," he said.

Oscar's expression altered slightly at that, maybe because he had expected pleading, maybe because he had wanted it. "Do you have anything further to say."

A hundred useless sentences crowded up at once: apology, explanation, his age, Nina, want, shame. Dead on arrival, all of them. None of them would change the ledger now in front of him.

"This was me," he said instead. "Not the kitchen. Not Eszter. Bea walking out wasn't staff failure."

Oscar's eyes sharpened, and for the first time a little heat touched the edges of his control. "Don't tell me how to categorize failure in my own house."

"I'm telling you who did what."

"You are, in fact, in exactly that position for ten more seconds," Nina said. "Unless you'd like to sack the florist and the dishwashers for your daughter's perfectly legible opinion."

Oscar cut his eyes to her, then back to Beni. When he spoke, the anger had gone back into colder language. "Fine. The kitchen keeps its staff. The agency girl in coats keeps her shift. Ms. Toth's name doesn't enter another sentence about tonight. Bea walked out because she chose to walk out, and you will be the only employee consequence of what you've just put on this table."

Beni nodded once.

Nina laughed once under her breath, a sound with no amusement in it. "There. Belated ethics, and it still ends in payroll language."

Oscar ignored her. "Leave by the mews. Tonight. If you contact my daughter directly again, I'll treat it as harassment. If you contact my wife, that's between the solicitors currently earning too much money from us."

That, absurdly, was what made Nina's face go white under the makeup. Not the affair. Not the envelope. The neat public-private line being redrawn in front of her by someone who already had a room full of witnesses to manage.

Beni nodded because there was nothing else to do with his head. He turned for the door.

"Beni." Nina's voice stopped him before his hand reached the handle.

He looked back.

She had dropped the gala smile completely now. What was left was needier and meaner and more honest for it. "You think this makes you honest?"

He considered lying to her out of habit and did not bother. "No."

The answer seemed to offend her more than any defense would have. Her mouth tightened. For a moment he thought she would say something cruel enough to keep him in the room with her. Instead she looked past him to the door, toward the dining room noise, and said, "Then what a waste."

He went out before she could make him stay for the rest.

The hall had already sealed over. That was the expertise of houses like this. Silver travelled. Guests lifted forks. The foundation chair smiled at a donor as if the entire evening were proceeding under divine management. The photographer was back near the tree taking table shots instead of family ones. On the sponsor wall the printed promise of dignity and shelter shone in the candlelight as if nobody had just argued over who would pay for truth.

Beni took the service stairs because he knew them better than any formal route in the house. At the coat room the agency woman was still blotting at her streaming eyes between guests, still employed for at least the next five minutes of somebody else's emergency. On the half-landing he passed Freya carrying a crate of dessert spoons against her hip, limping openly now that she thought she was out of sight. She gave him one quick look at his empty lapel where the radio had been and understood enough not to ask. The dumbwaiter rattled. Somebody in the kitchen called for more beurre blanc. Upstairs a donor laughed with his whole throat. The night had not paused to let his life break in it.

In the mews the rain had started again, finer now but steadier, needling the black paint of the cars and drawing silver lines down the catering van doors. Beni stood under the weak yellow light by the bins and listened to the house behind him go on earning its own story. He had one hundred and seventy pounds less than silence used to be worth and no shift in the morning worth counting on. He had no key in his pocket for the side door. No next instruction. No respectable sentence to put between himself and what he had done.

The practical disaster had already started. Laci would still want rent. Mercer would hear by morning. Eszter would not forgive him because unemployment was not virtue and confession did not make her housing safer retroactively. None of that changed because he had finally put objects on a table and said yes when asked a factual question.

Still, when he stepped out of the mews and into the wet Belgravia street, the air felt different in his chest, not cleaner, not lighter, but no longer rented. He turned away from the front door and walked because walking was what remained once the car, the keys, and the house had been taken back.


# Chapter 18

The Mercer suit hung in its plastic cover behind Laci's work jackets, too good for the room and useless in it now that January had come. Weeks had passed since the Ashdowns' mews and the rain. Beni went out at dusk in dark trousers with shine at the knees, Noor's spare fleece over a thin jumper, and the cheap black shoes he had once kept for wet weather. Damp plaster sweated through the walls. Old onion oil from too many nights' frying sat in the curtains. Water had dried in crooked lines on the window and started again with the next rain. Laci sat at the table in thermal socks, rubbing white dust from the seam of his thumbnail with the edge of a Metro receipt.

"Night airport?" Laci asked.

"Yeah."

Laci grunted, not kind and not hostile either. He reached beside his chair and pushed over a dented red thermos with a sticker half torn off it, some cartoon excavator still visible in the scrape. "Take it. Machine coffee there tastes like burned screws."

Beni looked at him.

"Don't make a face," Laci said. "It's tea. I'm not giving you my rakia."

"I'm not making a face."

"You are. Go. If you miss the last bit of A406 before it clogs, you save twenty minutes."

Laci had never before offered him road advice without first attaching a lecture about rent. Beni unscrewed the thermos cap. Strong black tea hit up out of it with lemon peel and too much sugar. The sweetness made his teeth ache before he even drank it.

"I can give you one-fifty Friday," Beni said.

Laci kept scraping the receipt edge under his nail. "Good."

"That still leaves-"

"I know what it leaves." Laci shrugged into a hoodie dark with rain and powdered with plaster. "I'm not a bank statement."

Beni nodded. It was the nearest thing to mercy he was likely to get. He put the thermos in his backpack and went down the stairs past the bicycles with no front wheels and the pram frame somebody had left by the letterboxes in November and never claimed again.

Noor had texted him an address behind the airport hotels, an industrial strip where coach companies, minicab firms, and catering warehouses lived shoulder to shoulder in units with roller shutters and bad security lights. Beni had done airport runs in Mercer cars often enough to know the roads west, but he had done them from the expensive side of the glass: polished dash, silent cabin, bottle water in the door, passengers who acted as if travel existed to carry their inconvenience in velvet. Grease filmed the puddles by the unit, and every passing van pushed diesel and soaked-cardboard stink against the shutters. A blue sign above the office window said SKYLINE AIRPORT CARS in letters two shades brighter than the building deserved.

Noor was outside with a paper cup of tea, still in his Mercer suit. The cuffs had gone shiny from years of steering wheels and door handles. His tie was loose, coat open, eyes tired and alert in the same practical way Beni had always trusted.

"There he is," Noor said. "You look like a sixth-former going to court."

Beni glanced at the fleece. "It's your fault. This is your jacket."

"Then wear it with more confidence." Noor knocked his knuckles against the office glass. "Come on. Nita's in a mood already."

Inside, the office was half the size of the Ashdown pantry. A printer coughed on a shelf by the kettle. Three radios hissed at once. The heater by the wall made more noise than warmth. The woman behind the desk had a chipped navy manicure and one strip of New Year's glitter left stubbornly on her ring fingernail. She was maybe forty, hair pinned up with a pencil, reading glasses hanging on a beaded chain against a sweatshirt that said BRUGES in peeling white letters.

"This is the Hungarian?" she asked Noor.

"He has a name," Noor said.

"So do I, and no one uses it at three in the morning." She held out her hand to Beni. "License."

Her name, according to the badge clipped crooked to her chest, was Nita. Cigarette smoke clung to her cardigan and hair under a losing layer of cheap rose soap. While she copied Beni's details she kept a pile of wrapped mint imperials lined along the desk blotter as if counting them calmed her. Every time the radio cracked too loud she straightened one without looking.

"No points?" she asked.

"No."

"No drama with clients? No shouting back, no driving off, no cleverness?"

Beni shook his head.

"Good. They can be animals. You don't get to be one back." She pushed a laminated airport pickup card across the desk. "You pick up at the lane they give you. Not the lane that seems faster. If police move you, you move. If client says you've kept them waiting half an hour, check the timestamp. They lie from habit. If somebody vomits, bag it and tell me before the smell sets in. I don't negotiate bodily fluids after dawn."

Noor hid a grin in his cup.

Nita looked up at Beni over the top of the glasses now halfway down her nose. "You done airport before?"

"Yes."

"In a nice car?"

"Sometimes."

She snorted. "Forget nice car. This one has heating on one side and a boot that sticks if you flatter it too much."

The key fob had a cracked plastic tag marked 14. Outside, bay fourteen held a silver Skoda Octavia with one hubcap missing, a pine-tree air freshener brown with age, and a driver's seat rubbed shiny at the edges. The dashboard phone mount had been reinforced with black tape. There were no bottled waters, no tissues folded into hotel triangles, no smell of leather polish. Somebody had once burned the passenger seat with a cigarette and dabbed at it with superglue. The car started on the second turn and settled into a tired diesel thrum that came up through the steering wheel.

Noor leaned through the open window before Beni pulled out. "Listen."

Beni waited.

"Do the shift. Don't try to prove anything on the first night. If it's shit, it is shit. You still do the shift."

Beni nodded.

Noor tapped the door twice. "And don't go silent if the airport roads get in your head. Ring me. I mean it."

He meant the lorry. He meant the white line bending wrong under sodium light and the hard shoulder and the airbag memory coming up hot and chemical in Beni's throat. Beni didn't answer because the answer was already in the job. Rent did not care what roads got in his head. He put the car in gear and followed the arrows toward the holding area.

The first fare was a man from Terminal 5 to a hotel near Paddington. He came out in a waxed jacket with a golf bag, talking into a headset about severance packages as if he were bored by the cost of ruining other people's week. His suitcase wheel was wrapped in silver tape. When Beni got out to help with the bags, the man kept talking and let the handle drop so the full weight of it yanked through Beni's shoulder.

"Can you not?" the man said, not to Beni, into the headset. Then to Beni, as if changing channels. "Bit sharper, mate. I'm frozen."

He got in without a hello. Two minutes into the drive he said the car smelled of chips. Five minutes after that he demanded a charger Beni did not have. When they hit roadworks near the flyover he sighed theatrically and said, "Of course," like Beni had arranged the cones himself.

At the hotel awning he left a damp half-moon of mud on the rear mat and went inside still discussing staff reductions. He forgot a black leather glove on the seat. Beni picked it up, soft and expensive, warmer than the car. For a second he thought of putting it in the glove box and letting it go to lost property in the morning. Instead he ran after the man through the revolving door and handed it over. The man took it without breaking his sentence or his pace.

Back in the Skoda, Beni sat with the door shut and the engine idling. The heater blew lukewarm air at his right knee and nothing at his left. On the Mercer app a ride like that would have carried polished silence, a hotel doorman, a tip if the client was drunk enough to confuse gratitude with largesse. Here the meter history lived on a scratched company phone and the job was done when Nita's screen said done. He liked, with an irritation that felt almost shameful, how clean that was. Nobody had asked him to erase a stop or forget a face. Nobody cared what he knew so long as he found the terminal and kept the car moving.

The next hour brought a family with two sleeping boys and a pink hard-shell suitcase plastered with cartoon horse stickers. The mother apologized for the crumbs before the crumbs existed. The father smelled of airport coffee and panic. They were going to an aunt in Harrow because the cheaper hotel had cancelled on them and one boy had started coughing on the plane. The younger child woke once, sat straight up, and said, "Not the fox house," then fell back asleep with his cheek against the car-seat strap. The parents laughed a thin, tired laugh and the mother explained in a whisper that the child had become convinced all London foxes lived in one particular brick house near their aunt's bus stop.

When Beni lifted the suitcase out, the father tried to help and knocked his own shin hard on the curb. He cursed softly, laughed at himself, and paid exact fare plus one pound in coins from a pocket full of receipts. "For the patience," he said. It was one pound in coins, and still more grace than the man with the glove had managed.

Near midnight the radio sent Beni back west empty. Rain had thinned to a shine on the road, enough to hold every lamp in a trembling line. The airport signs began again: Terminal 2, Terminal 3, Cargo, Short Stay, Long Stay. The slip road widened under sodium orange, the white lane markings curving away with that same indifferent geometry that had once broken him open on the hard shoulder. His hands tightened around the wheel before he realized they had. The cheap fleece stuck damp at the back of his neck.

A lorry came up fast on the right, spray needling across the windscreen. For a moment Beni could smell the old airbag powder, bitter and intimate as a tooth drilled at the gum. He could see Klara's red lipstick on the overturned cigarette pack between the seats, wet road in Hungary, her laugh cut off so fast it had left part of itself hanging in the car. Then the lorry passed. It was only a lorry. The Skoda stayed true. The wipers beat a flat metronome over the glass.

He eased off the accelerator by three miles an hour, no more, and kept going.

At one in the morning he picked up a woman from arrivals with a lilac suitcase zipped overfull and tied round once with a luggage strap from another airline. She wore nurse shoes with the backs crushed down and held supermarket roses upside down by the stems, petals bruising against her sleeve.

"North Finchley," she said. Her voice had the slack edge of somebody whose feet had given up two hours earlier.

When Beni pulled away she caught him glancing at the flowers and smiled without embarrassment. "For my mum. She says they die slower if you buy them at the airport because they've already been through customs."

"Is that true?"

"No idea. She reads nonsense on Facebook and believes it harder if the spelling is bad."

By the time he reached North Finchley she was asleep against the window, leaving a round fog mark in the glass. He woke her gently. She fumbled in her bag for the fare, then for a second smaller purse, then for one wrapped cough sweet she pressed into his palm as if that were the proper balance of accounts. "For the road," she said. "Cherry."

He drove back south with the sweet on the dashboard untouched. The night's meanness had not improved, but it had widened. There were people in it who were simply exhausted, people whose tempers came from cancelled flights and sick children and two jobs stitched together without enough sleep in between. The old Mercer world had trained him to think humiliation travelled downward in a smooth chauffeur line: client to driver, rich to poor, silence to the man paid to keep it. Airport nights were messier. Everybody arrived wrung out. Everybody wanted someone else to absorb the spill.

At half two he sat in the holding lane and drank Laci's tea, now metallic with the thermos and still too sweet. The car park lights made every puddle look skinned. Drivers smoked under the shelter and argued in bursts of Punjabi, Polish, Arabic, English, then laughed because the argument had never been about language in the first place. On the company phone Nita sent him the week's terms again, as if reminding the men in cars would make the number larger: full rota, no missed shifts, four hundred and thirty before the tax the company pretended was a favor.

The number came to four hundred and thirty, ninety less than Mercer wages on a good week and far below what Nina could slide across a seat without folding it first. It mattered. It wasn't going to ease anything quickly.

He counted it anyway against what remained of the debt to Laci, against travel, against food, against the new shape of a life that did not include cash envelopes or Belgravia tips or the sick thrill of being summoned by name by a woman in the back seat. The number held. Barely, but it held.

Just before dawn, when the sky over the perimeter road went from black to the color of wet cement, his phone buzzed in the cup holder. It was not dispatch. It was a text from Eszter.

Can you carry boxes and keep your mouth useful? New place. Ten. If you're dead, say so.

He read it twice, then once more, because the screen was greasy with his thumb and he did not trust the first meaning when it came from her. There had been Bea's emergency, the gala, the half hour outside the church-basement room when Eszter had let him know Bea was asleep and alive and nothing else. There had been silence since then, not theatrical silence, not punishment sent in installments, only the absence of a reason.

He typed, I'm not dead. Off at eight. Where?

The address came back in under a minute, a road in the north again, not far from the retail park where Beni sometimes bought socks because they were cheaper there after nine. Then another message.

Bring screws if you have them. The landlord thinks one loose hinge is a personality.

By eight fifteen damp wool, airport coffee, and the cherry cough sweet he still had not eaten had worked themselves into the Skoda's fabric. Beni handed the keys back to Nita, who inspected the fuel receipt like a priest checking a confession.

"All right?" she asked.

"Yes."

She looked at him longer than the answer deserved. "You still have both eyes. Good start."

Outside, Noor was leaning against his own car with a bacon roll wrapped in paper gone translucent with grease. He had waited though Beni had not asked him to.

"Well?" Noor said.

"It's bad."

"Obviously."

"The heater works only on one leg."

Noor laughed. "And?"

Beni thought of the printed jobs, the ugly little office, the roads west that had not killed him. "And it's work."

"Yeah." Noor took a bite, chewed, swallowed. "That's the trick of it. Shit work is still work."

Beni told him about the text from Eszter.

Noor's expression changed by a degree, no more. "You going?"

"Yes."

"Then go tired and honest. Tired you can manage. Honest is the hard part."

Beni looked at the roll in Noor's hand. "You wait here every morning for everybody's love life?"

"No. Just yours. It's cheaper than television."

Beni almost smiled. Noor saw it and did not press. He only stepped forward and squeezed Beni's shoulder once, a brief driver-to-driver grip, then pointed west with the bacon roll. "Sleep later. Don't say anything stupid because you think it sounds noble."

The new place was above a shuttered barber shop on a side street where the buses hissed every four minutes and the front doors all needed paint. The carpet gave underfoot with a damp chill, and fried fish seeped down the stairwell from another floor. Eszter had propped the building door with a sack of potatoes and was waiting at the foot of the stairs in a grey sweatshirt, hair tied up in a green elastic that looked ready to snap. Her forearms were reddened from cold and carrying. There was a smear of white paint high on one sleeve.

"You look terrible," she said.

"I came from work."

"Then work is ugly. Take that end."

She pointed at a flat-pack wardrobe box so long it nearly blocked the hall. Her mouth stayed hard but not shut. For now, that was more than Beni had expected.

They carried the box up two flights sideways because the turn on the landing was too tight. The new flat was one room, a kitchenette behind a half wall, and a bathroom so narrow the sink sat crooked over the toilet cistern to make peace with gravity. Fresh white paint had failed to kill the old smell underneath, some mixture of old frying oil, damp plaster, and last tenant's air freshener. A single window looked over the bus stop and a betting shop sign blinking blue even in daylight. The radiator clicked but gave only a shallow warmth. On the floor against the wall stood two saucepans, a kettle, three books in Hungarian, a box of winter clothes, and a rolled child mattress tied with string. Leaning beside it was a small white chest of drawers with a cartoon frog sticker still on the top drawer, one eye peeled away.

Beni set down his end of the wardrobe and looked at the mattress.

Eszter caught the look. "Yes, for Lili. Don't get sentimental. It's thinner than prison bread."

"Does it fit?"

"If she doesn't grow four inches by April, yes."

There was already a paper calendar tacked to the wall by the kitchenette. Several dates in spring had been circled hard enough to dent the plaster behind them. A shopping list lay on the counter in two languages, half food and half repair: child pillow, pan with lid, curtain hooks, school-holiday dates, airport fare.

"Where do you want this?" Beni asked.

She pointed with the screwdriver in her hand. "Not there. That's the only place where the drawer can open. Here. No, tilt it first. Christ, did the rich people ever let you touch furniture or only cars?"

"Mostly cars."

"That explains your face."

They worked without grace. The wardrobe instructions had been printed by a sadist who believed one smiling line drawing could replace nouns. The screws came in a bag with three missing. The hinges were not the right size. Eszter had already unpacked plates but not cutlery. She moved fast, furious at useless objects in a way that was not really about the objects. Every few minutes she would stop to line up something small and necessary: soap by the sink, the three mugs on the shelf, Lili's plastic yellow bowl in the only cupboard that did not smell faintly of bleach.

Beni found the loose hinge from her text. The screws had stripped the wood clean. He packed the holes with snapped matchsticks from the stove box and drove in longer screws he had brought from Laci's tools. The door held.

Eszter watched him test it twice. "Good. The landlord kept saying he'd come tomorrow. Tomorrow from men like that means after the ceiling falls."

"What does he want for this?"

She named the rent. It was low by London standards and obscene by any honest measure of the room.

Beni looked around once more. "He gets that for this?"

"He gets it because I need an address where my daughter can sleep without the church ladies listening for sin through the radiator."

That shut him up. Eszter bent to cut open another box. When she straightened she winced and pressed a fist into the small of her back.

"How much did you carry before I came?" he asked.

"Enough to hate the second-hand market forever."

"What else is downstairs?"

"Table. Two chairs. One bag of pans from the Hungarian woman on the next road. She gave me extra jars because her son moved to Bristol and now apparently Bristol means he only eats quinoa."

They went down again. The table was small enough for a kitchen and too big for the room. One chair had been painted dark blue, the other left raw wood, as if the set had split in a divorce and reassembled under protest. As they maneuvered it up the stairs, a door on the first landing opened and an elderly man in a vest looked out with a tabby cat over his shoulder. The cat wore a red collar with a bell that had been wrapped in electrician's tape to silence it.

"Mind the wall," the man said. "Landlord'll blame all of us if you scrape it."

"We're minding it," Eszter said.

The man looked at Beni, at the table, at Eszter again. "That chair's missing a screw in the back. I meant to tell you."

"Of course it is," Eszter said.

He shrugged, not offended. "Cat sat on the bag. I forgot." Then he retreated, the taped bell making a dull useless tap against the cat's chest.

Beni got the table in with less damage than it deserved. When he turned, Eszter had almost smiled. Almost was not nothing.

"You found a strict palace," he said.

"It has a door that locks from inside and a bathroom that is only mine. Luxury."

He wiped his hands on his trousers. Sawdust, dust, and old paint coated his palms. Rich houses bred a cleaner kind of dirt, polished over before it became shame. This was honest mess, or at least mess that admitted its own source.

"Have you eaten?" Eszter asked.

"Not since midnight."

"Then cut that cabbage."

She set a cheap metal pan on the hob and handed him a knife that dragged a little on the chopping board. The kitchen corner was barely large enough for two, so they worked by passing behind each other sideways, hip to drawer, shoulder to wall. She fried onions in oil, added cabbage, caraway, noodles, half a sausage cut mean and thin. It was poor food and smelled good almost at once. The window fogged from the steam and from the buses breathing outside.

He watched her reach automatically for a fourth plate and correct herself to two. She saw him notice and did not apologize.

"I bought the yellow bowl yesterday," she said, nodding toward the cupboard. "She hates eating from white plates. Says white plates make everything look like dentist food."

"How old is she now?"

"Six and a half. Which is an age designed by the devil. Too big for carrying, too small for sense."

Beni smiled despite being tired enough for his skin to feel full of grit.

"Don't make that face," Eszter said, stirring. "You haven't met six and a half in wet socks because somebody said no to ice cream."

"I've met airport passengers."

"Same height?"

He laughed once, quietly. The sound startled him by existing at all. Eszter heard that too. She turned the heat down and let the pan sit. The room grew fuller with the smell of cooked cabbage, cheap sausage, and damp wool drying slowly on both of them.

They ate at the table before it was fully stable, one leg propped with the folded property section of a Metro. Eszter had not unpacked proper napkins, so they used paper towels from the roll. Outside, the blue betting-shop sign blinked on and off through the thin curtain she had clipped in place with laundry pegs.

For a few minutes they spoke only about the flat: which cupboard did not shut, which bus stop was nearest, whether the boiler noise meant danger or only character. It felt almost easy until easy itself became suspicious.

"Bea called me after the gala," Eszter said at last.

Beni put down his fork. "All right."

"She wanted to know if you'd been thrown out into the snow like in some Victorian book."

"There wasn't snow."

"I noticed." Eszter ate another mouthful. "She said her father did not shout. That was worse. She said Nina looked like she might either break a glass or swallow one."

Beni looked at the wobbling table leg. "That sounds right."

"She also said you gave him the book and the cash."

"I did."

Eszter nodded once. There was no forgiveness in it, only receipt of information. "Good."

He waited. When she did not add to it, he said, "I should've done it sooner."

"Yes."

"You don't have to keep agreeing."

"Why not? It saves time."

The directness should have stung. Instead it eased something in him. There was no edge of performance to fight through, no hope disguised as punishment, no invitation to make a better speech. He had earned the simple truth of yes.

He ate another bite because the noodles were there and because she had cooked them. He did not look at her until he could do it without asking for more.

"Thank you for letting me help," he said.

Eszter wiped her fork clean with bread. "I let you carry furniture. Don't write a poem."

He almost answered with something safer, smaller. Tiredness made lies look efficient. It also made them harder to lift. He heard Noor again: don't say anything stupid because you think it sounds noble.

"I didn't come for points," he said.

Eszter raised an eyebrow. "Good, because I don't have any."

He breathed through the urge to stop there. The old instinct was to deliver the acceptable line, the one that sounded ashamed enough to pass inspection, that he was sorry, that he had been weak, that he had made a mess. Those generic things cost him less than the precise version.

"It wasn't only the money," he said.

She did not move. Even her fork stayed in her hand the same way. He knew that stillness from kitchens and arguments and beds. It meant continue or shut up forever.

"It mattered," he said. "The money. Your rent. Mine. All of it. But it wasn't only that." He looked at the yellow bowl in the cupboard because it was easier than looking at her while he made himself ugly on purpose. "When she started asking for me, when dispatch sent me because she said my name, I liked it. I liked being the one called. Not the driver in the pool. Not the spare. Me."

Eszter's face did not soften. That helped.

"I know how that sounds," he said.

"No," she said. "You know how nice lies sound. Keep going."

He let out air through his nose. The room was too warm at the table and too cold by the window. Cabbage grease and metal from the stripped screw heads filmed his hands.

"I liked the back way into that house," he said. "The mews, the side door, the feeling that I knew the route nobody else saw. I liked being useful in the dirty places because it felt like I had a trick. Like I could be invisible and special at the same time. It was rotten. It still felt good."

Eszter drank water before she answered. "Yes. That's true."

He looked up. "That's all?"

"What do you want, Beni? A certificate?" She shook her head. "No. But that's true. And it's more useful to me than hearing you say you were helpless. You weren't helpless. You were flattered."

The word landed cleanly. The word was flattered, smaller than desire, uglier than love, harder to romanticize. He could not argue with it without beginning another lie.

"I was," he said.

Eszter pushed her plate back a little. "And the money made it easy to keep telling yourself a story about being practical. Men love practical when it covers appetite."

He almost objected at men, because Laci was not like this, Noor was not like this, because general statements always made him itch. Then he remembered Nina's envelopes, his own relief at them, the way he had carried both hunger and self-disgust like they cancelled each other out. They never had.

"I used you to feel clean," he said. The sentence came out flat and unadorned, which made it worse. "Not every minute. But enough."

Eszter's mouth tightened, not in surprise. "Yes."

He stared at his plate. One noodle had stuck to the rim and gone glossy with oil. He thought absurdly of the Ashdown kitchen, white china, the silver warming drawer, how quickly a mess vanished there if somebody was paid enough to see it first.

"I'm sorry," he said.

This time she let the sentence sit.

"I'm not saying it to get back in," he added.

"Good. There isn't a back in."

He nodded. The words hurt less than the space after them. Space was cleaner. You could arrange furniture in space. You could put a child mattress against the wall and know where it ended.

Eszter stood to clear the plates. He stood too on reflex and carried them the three steps to the sink.

"Leave it," she said.

"I can wash."

"You can dry. The hot tap only works if you threaten it first."

They worked shoulder to shoulder again, closer than the room allowed and not close in the old way at all. She washed fast, forearms wet to the elbow. He dried and stacked. The rhythm of it steadied him more than any speech could have. When she passed him the yellow bowl, she dried her thumb on the tea towel before touching it, an unnecessary care that told him exactly how much the object mattered.

"Lili likes foxes?" he asked, because he needed a thing in the world between them.

Eszter snorted. "She likes things that should stay far away and not bite. Foxes, ponies, actors on television. She has decided London is full of all three."

"Will she fly?"

"If the ticket doesn't go mad, yes. My sister might bring her. Or I pay for the assistance service and let some airport woman hate me for paperwork." Eszter set the bowl carefully in the cupboard. "I want spring because winter here is ugly enough for adults. I don't want her first look at London to be these windows and everybody coughing."

Beni thought of the airport roads, the holding lane at two in the morning, the fluorescent arrivals hall with its sticky floors and tired children crying at baggage reclaim. "I can meet you," he said. "If you need. I know the terminals."

Eszter did not answer at once. She wrung out the cloth, hung it over the tap, wiped the counter in three economical strokes. Under stress she arranged things. Her silence had always worked that way, practical before anything else.

"Maybe," she said finally. "If the shift lets you. And if I ask. Don't hear more in it than that."

"I won't."

"And you can text sometimes. About real things. Work. If you're alive. If Bea sets fire to Belgravia. Not at two in the morning because you feel lonely and suddenly remember I speak your language."

"All right."

"That's all I've got." Her voice stayed level. "I can talk to a man who tells me the ugly version. I can't sleep with him because he finally learned to say it."

The plainness of that did more than anger would have done. It left no clean wound to bandage, only a shape to live around. Beni nodded once.

"I know."

"Do you?" She gave a short, tired laugh without humor. "Maybe now."

Her phone buzzed on the windowsill. She glanced at the screen and recognition loosened her face by a degree, as if somebody had reached in and loosened one bolt.

"Lili," she said.

She did not leave the room. She did not step into the stairwell for privacy. She thumbed the voice note open where he could hear, which was not intimacy and was not nothing either.

The child's voice came small and bright through the cheap speaker, Hungarian rushing over itself with the impatience of a six-year-old who believed any pause might lose her the world. Eszter translated the first line automatically under her breath for Beni and then stopped because he was already following.

Mama, Grandma says the rabbit can't come because he smells like soup, but he's coming. Also if London buses have two floors, do foxes use stairs? And I want the bed by the window when I come, not the wall, because I need to see if the moon there is different.

There was a muffled older woman's voice in the background telling her not to shout into the phone. Lili shouted something else anyway, laughter skidding behind it, then the message cut off in a rustle.

Eszter listened to the first part twice more, not because she had missed it but because that was how missing worked. On the second play she laughed once at the rabbit smelling like soup. She rubbed at her mouth immediately after, as if the laugh had escaped some rationing rule.

"She has a rabbit?" Beni asked.

"One ear, no eyes, stuffed with whatever was cheap in 2013." Eszter put the phone down more gently than she had picked it up. "She named him Minister because my ex used to shout at the television."

"Does he smell like soup?"

"He has smelled like everything. Soup would be an improvement."

Beni looked at the rolled mattress, the frog sticker drawer, the window over the bus stop. The room was still too small. The rent was still theft. The boiler still clicked like something thinking mean thoughts in the wall. But the child voice had changed the geometry. The place now had a future weight in it, one pair of small socks and one rotten toy rabbit already occupying air.

Eszter followed his glance. "She'll want the window bed until the first siren. Then she'll want my side and my ribs and half the blanket. She always sleeps with one foot outside like she's testing the room."

The detail went through him more sharply than any plea could have done. He could see that foot outside the blanket. That was the danger of children, even other people's. They became precise too fast.

"What else do you need?" he asked.

She understood the question for what it was and also what it was not. Money hovered around it like steam but he had not said money. Perhaps because he did not have enough to offer. Perhaps because they both knew the old contamination of help.

"A thicker curtain," she said after a moment. "The blue sign from the betting shop is like living inside a fish tank. Maybe a second saucepan. Maybe a proper pillow. Nothing heroic."

"I can find curtains."

"I know you can. You know routes to sad discount shops better than anyone."

"I know one on the retail park."

"I know. You love a car park more than a human being."

"That's not true."

She gave him a look.

"Not anymore," he said.

That earned him a brief crooked smile, real and gone quickly. "Careful. That almost sounded charming."

He took the rubbish down because it needed taking down. On the landing the old man with the taped cat bell was smoking out the window in slippers. The cat sat on the sill watching buses with the contempt of a landlord.

"Got the chair fixed?" the man asked.

"Not yet."

"I've got screws somewhere. If my daughter hasn't stolen them for art." He squinted at Beni's tired face. "You working nights?"

"Yes."

"Rotten for the stomach." He tapped ash into a mug he had dedicated to the purpose. "My wife did nights thirty years. She still eats breakfast at midnight sometimes. Tell your girl to get thicker curtains. This street does not sleep."

"I will."

When he went back up, Eszter had folded the flat-pack cardboard and leaned it by the door. She had also put half the leftover noodles into a plastic container with a lid that did not quite match. The mismatch felt almost luxurious in its ordinariness.

"For later," she said. "You look hollow."

"I can make my own food."

"I didn't say you can't." She held out the container until he took it. "The spoon goes back to me."

He slipped it into his bag beside the thermos. Laci's sweet tea smell had sunk into everything. For one strange second the bag held his whole present life: borrowed fleece, dented thermos, screws, leftover cabbage noodles, airport card.

At the door he stopped because leaving required an exactness he had once avoided by simply lingering until somebody else defined him. Eszter waited, one hand on the frame.

"If I text," he said, "and you don't answer, I won't keep pushing."

"Good."

"If you need the airport when she comes-"

"I heard you the first time."

He nodded.

She looked at him properly then, not as furniture mover or liar or emergency contact, but as the tired nineteen-year-old man he still was underneath all the service polish and damage. Her gaze did not pardon him. It did something harder and smaller.

"Sleep before you drive tonight if you can," she said. "I'm not having you wrap the car round a lorry because you wanted to be useful twice in one day."

It was care, limited, unsentimental, entirely on her terms. It nearly undid him because it had edges.

"I'll sleep," he said.

"Good. And Beni."

"Yeah?"

"When you text, text like a person. Not like a man leaving receipts on a table."

He let out one breath that almost became another laugh. "All right."

Then he went down the stairs with the container warm through the bag and the smell of cabbage following him into the street.

He slept three hours in the front room with buses shaking the glass and Laci coming in once, looking at him, saying nothing. At dusk he took the Skoda west on a pickup from Victoria to Heathrow, one businessman and two cases, no conversation. Rain had started again, fine enough to seem like the air itself had decided not to dry out. Near Belgravia the traffic slowed. White facades rose clean and blank above the shine of the road. A florist's van blocked half a lane. Ahead on the left the mouth of the Ashdowns' mews opened between brick walls, narrow, hidden, the route by which he had once arrived to keep everything shameful and efficient.

His hand moved a fraction on the wheel before he caught it. It was an old habit, old training. The back way was quicker. The back way kept you out of sight. The back way had been where Nina first climbed over the seat and where he had learned how easy it was to confuse being chosen with being used.

He left the indicator off.

The main road was clogged with buses, cyclists slick in waterproofs, and a delivery van whose rear doors had been tied shut with blue rope. Everybody saw everybody there. There was no service entrance and no mews wall to swallow the car whole. The businessman in the back glanced up from his phone, impatient already for the airport, for whatever small misery waited after that. Beni kept the wheel straight.

The mews slipped past on his left and was gone. He stayed in the lit lane with the rest of London and drove west.
